Ibn Abidin's Symbols and Scholarly Etiquette in Al-Hashiya
Biographical note on Ibn Abidin's methodology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Abidin's symbols (ط and ح) and explain his method of respectful critique in fatwa literature.

Core Idea
Ibn Abidin teaches that critique does not require contestation; a simple 'ponder' suffices for the discerning reader.

This passage from the introduction to Ibn Abidin's al-Hashiya reveals the systematic use of symbols and the scholarly decorum that characterizes Hanafi fatwa literature. By understanding his symbols, you gain access to the rich layer of marginal commentary that shaped later Hanafi fiqh. Moreover, his indirect refutations illustrate a profound respect for the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the symbols
Look for the symbols 'ط' and 'ح' and note what they refer to. The passage states that 'ط' means al-Tahtawi and 'ح' means al-Halabi.
These are consistent throughout the Hashiya.
2
Understand the practice
Recognize that Ibn Abidin uses these symbols consistently to reference specific commentators. In later volumes, he also uses symbols from other works like al-Qunyah.
Symbols are not arbitrary.
3
Note the adab of critique
See how he critiques by using phrases like 'تأمل' (ponder) instead of outright rejection. This shows politeness and respect.
He intends to reject, but indirectly.
4
Apply in reading
When reading Hanafi texts, if you see 'ط' or 'ح', recall these references. If you see 'تأمل', understand that the previous opinion is being challenged subtly.
This enriches your comprehension of marginal notes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يكن للعلامة ابن عابدين كثير من الرموز والاصطلاحات المغمضة في كتابه الحاشية، فحيث رمز "ط" فمراده العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على "الدر" ، وحيث رمز "ح" فمراده العلامة الحلبي، محشى "الدر المختار". وفي الجزئين الرابع والخامس بعض الرموز التي ينقلها عن بعض الكتب كـ القنية وغيرها تعرف بمكانها، وهو في حاشيته كعادته في جميع كتبه على أدب جم مع العلماء السابقين . إلا أنه غالبًا ما يورد عبارة الطحطاوى أو الحلبي أو غيرهما، ويضيف بعدها "تأمل" أو "فليحرر أو فيه نظر" أو غير ذلك، وحينئذ مراده أن لا يُسلّم بذلك بل يردّه بشكل غير ظاهر تأدبا .
The scholar Ibn Abidin did not have many obscure symbols and terminologies in his book al-Hashiya. So when he uses the symbol "ط", he means the scholar al-Tahtawi in his gloss on al-Durr; and when he uses "ح", he means al-Halabi, the annotator of al-Durr al-Mukhtar. In the fourth and fifth volumes there are some symbols that he transmits from certain books like al-Qunyah and others, which are known in their places. In his Hashiya, as is his habit in all his books, he maintains excellent etiquette with earlier scholars. However, he often quotes the phrase of al-Tahtawi or al-Halabi or others, and then adds "ponder" or "it needs to be examined" or "it is questionable" or other such expressions. In this he intends not to accept that point but to refute it in a non-apparent manner, out of politeness.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a biographical note on Ibn Abidin's methodology in his celebrated commentary al-Hashiya (Radd al-Muhtar). It explains his use of symbols: 'ط' for al-Tahtawi and 'ح' for al-Halabi, who wrote glosses on the same base texts. In later volumes, he uses symbols from other works like al-Qunyah. The passage highlights his scholarly decorum: while he often critiques earlier scholars, he does so indirectly with phrases like 'ponder' or 'needs examination', out of respect. This reflects the Hanafi tradition of respectful critique (adab) in fatwa literature.

Key Terms
ط (Ta): Symbol referring to al-Tahtawi in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar
ح (Ha): Symbol referring to al-Halabi, the annotator of al-Durr al-Mukhtar
تأمل (ta’ammul): A phrase used by Ibn Abidin to politely disagree with a previous scholar's opinion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the symbol 'ط' refer to in Ibn Abidin's Hashiya?
A) Al-Halabi
B) Al-Tahtawi
C) Al-Qunyah
D) Al-Baydawi

Key Takeaway
Ibn Abidin's system of symbols and his respectful method of critique are foundational for navigating later Hanafi fatwa literature.

The Mufti's Duty When No Precedent Exists
Introduces condition for mufti's striving and fear of God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition (lack of maqalah) and describe the mufti's required response (ijtihad with fear of Allah) when no precedent exists.

Core Idea
When no madhhab text exists, the mufti must combine ijtihad with fear of Allah.

A mufti's work is built on the transmitted positions (maqalat) of the Hanafi imams. But what happens when a novel case arises with no such text? This passage from the Rasm al-Mufti tradition answers that question. It sets a high standard: the mufti must not simply guess; he must exert serious ijtihad and remain conscious of standing before Allah. The warning is severe: only a wretched person would issue fatwas without this care.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Find the phrase 'حَيْثُ لَمْ تُوجَدْ لِهؤلاءِ مَقَالَةٌ' – 'when there is found no statement of these [scholars]'. This is the trigger for the mufti's special duty.
These scholars refer to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and other authoritative early Hanafis.
2
Recognize the required action
The mufti must 'ponder with seriousness and ijtihad' (بِجِدٍّ وَاجْتِهَادٍ). This is an active, rigorous effort to derive a ruling from the principles of the madhhab.
Ijtihad here is not independent reasoning but ijtihad within the school (takhrij or talfiq).
3
Understand the required attitude
The mufti must fear Allah's punishment on the Day of Return (وَلْيَخْشَ بَطْشَ رَبِّهِ يَوْمَ الْمَعَادِ). This fear restrains him from issuing rulings carelessly.
Fear (khashyah) is the antidote to arrogance and recklessness in fatwa.
4
Note the warning
The passage ends with a sharp warning: only a wretched loser (شَقِيٍّ خَاسِرِ الْمَرا) dares to issue rulings without such fear and effort.
This underscores the gravity of the mufti's role—he must never treat fatwa lightly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَيْثُ لَمْ تُوجَدْ لِهؤلاءِ مَقَالَةٌ وَاحْتِيحَ لِلإفْتاءِ فَلْيَنْظُرِ الْمُفْتِي بِجِدٍّ وَاجْتِهَادٍ وَلْيَخْشَ بَطْشَ رَبِّهِ يَوْمَ الْمَعَادِ فَلَيْسَ يَجْسُرُ عَلَى الأَحْكَامِ سِوَى شَقِيٍّ خَاسِرِ الْمَرَا
And when there is found no statement of these [scholars] and there is need for giving fatwa, then let the mufti ponder with seriousness and ijtihad, and fear the strike of his Lord on the Day of Return. For none dares to [issue] rulings except a wretched loser of the contest.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule for the mufti when no statement from the earlier authorities (Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn, etc.) is available. It imposes a duty: the mufti must exert himself in ijtihad and fear Allah. The final line warns that only a wretched person would dare to give fatwa without such care. This emphasizes the gravity of the mufti's responsibility.

Key Terms
مقالة (maqālā): A statement or opinion from the authoritative Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, etc.)
اجتهاد (ijtihād): Exertion of mental effort to derive a ruling from the sources or principles of the madhhab.
خشية (khashyah): Fear of Allah that restrains one from wrongdoing and motivates carefulness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence for a mufti who issues rulings without seriousness and fear of Allah?
A) He is excused because he acted in good faith.
B) He is described as a wretched loser of the contest.
C) He must repeat the ijtihad.
D) He should refer the matter to another mufti.

Key Takeaway
When no madhhab text exists, the mufti must combine serious ijtihad with fear of Allah; anything less is spiritually ruinous.

Limits of Using Weak Narrations in Fatwa and Qada
Poem setting conditions for using weak narrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that weak narrations cannot be used in fatwa or qada, identify the two exceptions, and explain why a judge's verdict based on a weak narration is void.

Core Idea
Weak narrations are generally prohibited in fatwa and qada, with narrow exceptions for necessity or scholarly caution.

This lesson examines a didactic poem from a Hanafi manual on mufti adab. It sets a strict boundary: a weak hadith (da'if) cannot be the basis for a fatwa or a judicial verdict. The poem carves out two limited exceptions—necessity (darurah) and a well-known scholar's personal practice—but absolutely bars judges from using weak narrations, tying them instead to the preponderant opinion (rajih) of the madhhab. Understanding these layers is essential for any mufti or qadi to maintain the integrity of legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the general prohibition
Identify the baseline rule: acting upon a weak narration and answering a questioner with it is not permissible. This establishes the default position of the madhhab.
2
Recognize the exceptions
The poem carves out two exceptions: (1) an agent with a necessity, and (2) a person with well-known knowledge (a qualified scholar). Understand that these are narrow, not blanket permissions.
The first exception often applies in cases of darurah; the second reflects the principle that a mujtahid may cautiously use da'if in fada'il or when no stronger evidence exists.
3
Analyze the judge's restriction
The poem explicitly forbids a qadi from using a weak narration. If he does, his judgment is void and not executed. This is stricter than the mufti's position.
The reason: qada is binding and must be based on certain evidence. The judge is also restricted to the preponderant opinion of the madhhab, not any weak report.
4
Connect to Hanafi judicial principle
The poem ends by reminding that Hanafi judges are 'qayyidu birajihi al-madhhab'—bound by the preponderant view. This reinforces the school's insistence on strong, reliable evidence for judicial decisions.
This principle is rooted in the need for consistency and predictability in the judiciary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجُوزُ بِالضَعِيفَ العَمَل وَلا بِهِ يُجَابُ مَن جَاءَ يَسأل
إِلَّا لِعَامِلِ لَه ضَرُورَه أَوْ مَنْ لَه مَعْرِفةٌ مَشْهُورَه
لَكِنّمَا القَاضِي بِهِ لا يَقْضِي وَإِنْ قَضَى فَحُكمُه لَا يَمْضِي
لا سِيِّمَا قُضَاتُنَا إِذْ قَيدُوا بِرَاجِحَ الْمَذْهَبِ حِيْنَ فَلَدُوا
And it is not permissible to act upon the weak, nor to answer with it the one who comes asking. Except for an agent (factor) who has a necessity, or one who has a well-known knowledge. However, the judge cannot judge by it, and if he judges, his judgment is not executed. Especially our judges, for they are restricted to the preponderant of the madhhab when they adjudicate.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This section clarifies the limits of using weak narrations (al-da'if) in the Hanafi madhhab. The general rule is that it is not permissible to act upon a weak hadith, nor to give it as an answer to a questioner. However, there are two exceptions: (1) a person acting in a capacity (such as a factor or agent) who has a necessity (darurah), and (2) a person who possesses well-known knowledge (i.e., a qualified scholar) may sometimes use it with caution. Importantly, a judge (qadi) cannot base his verdict on a weak narration; if he does, his judgment is void and does not pass. This is especially true for Hanafi judges, who are bound to follow only the preponderant opinion (al-rajih) of the madhhab when making their rulings. This reinforces the strict standard for judicial decisions.

Key Terms
الضعيف (al-da'if): A weak narration or report, lacking the conditions for being sahih or hasan.
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge who rules in disputes.
الضرورة (al-darurah): Necessity that permits an otherwise prohibited action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is the ruling if a judge bases his verdict on a weak narration?
A) The verdict is valid but discouraged
B) The verdict is void and not executed
C) The verdict is executed but the judge is sinful
D) The judge must repeat the case

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school forbids using weak narrations for fatwa and qada, with narrow exceptions for necessity or qualified scholars, and a judge's verdict based on weakness is void.

6 min
The Duty to Follow the Rajih in Fatwa
Quote on mufti and qadi duties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the duty of following the rajih in fatwa and identify the prohibited practices of mujazafah and hawa.

Core Idea
A mufti must only issue a fatwa based on the rajih opinion with thoroughness, avoiding recklessness and personal desire.

This passage from Fatawa Khayriyya lays down a foundational ethical and legal obligation for muftis and qadis: they must distinguish between rajih (preponderant) and marjuh (weak) opinions and act only on the rajih. It warns against recklessness (mujazafah) and following personal desire (hawa), which lead to slander against Allah. Understanding this duty is essential for anyone issuing fatwas or judicial rulings in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the ultimate goal of the mufti
The passage states that knowing the rajih and its degrees is the peak of scholarly aspiration. Understand that the mufti's primary task is to identify the strongest opinion.
This sets the standard for iftaa: not just any opinion, but the rajih.
2
Identify the obligatory duty (mafrud)
The text says it is obligatory upon the mufti and qadi to be thorough (tathabbut) and avoid recklessness (mujazafah). This is not a recommendation but a binding duty.
Tathabbut means careful verification of the evidence and the opinion's weight.
3
Recognize the prohibition of hawa and worldly inclination
The passage explicitly forbids following personal desire (hawa) and inclination toward wealth, calling them great calamities. Only an ignorant miserable person dares such.
This prohibition protects the integrity of the fatwa from bias.
4
Understand the consequence of violating these duties
Recklessness or bias leads to slander against Allah (al-iftira' ala Allah) by making lawful unlawful or vice versa. This is a grave sin.
The mufti is accountable to Allah for his rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي آخر الفتاوى الخيرية (۳) : " ولا شك أن معرفة راجح المختلف فيه من مرجوحه ومراتبه قوة وضعفًا - هو نهاية آمال المشمرين في تحصيل العلم. فالمفروض على المفتى والقاضي التثبت في الجواب وعدم المجازفة (٤) فيها؛ خوفًا من الافتراء على الله تعالى بتحريم حلال وضده. ويحرم اتباع الهوى والتشهى والميل إلى المال الذى هو الداهية (٥) الكبرى، والمصيبة العظمى ; فإن ذلك أمر عظيم لا يتجاسر عليه إلا كل جاهل شقى " . اهـ . (۳) قوله : " في آخر الفتاوى الخيرية " أي في مسائل شتى . (مظ) (٤) قوله : "المجازفة يقال : جازف فى كلامه أى تكلّم بلا قانون وبدون تبصر . (مظ) وقوله : "فيهما " أي في الإفتاء والقضاء . (٥) قوله : "الداهية " أى الأمر المنكر، والمصيبة، والأمر العظيم. (مظ)
And in the end of al-Fatawa al-Khayriyya: "And there is no doubt that knowing the preponderant (rajih) of that which is disputed from its weak (marjuh) and its degrees of strength and weakness – that is the ultimate aspiration of those who strive in acquiring knowledge. So it is obligatory upon the mufti and the judge to be thorough in the answer and not to be reckless in it, out of fear of slandering Allah Almighty by forbidding the permissible and its opposite. And it is forbidden to follow personal desire, caprice, and inclination towards wealth, which is the great calamity and the immense disaster; for that is a grave matter which only every ignorant miserable person dares to do." End. (3) His saying: 'At the end of al-Fatawa al-Khayriyya' meaning in miscellaneous issues. (mazhar) (4) His saying: 'al-mujazafah' – it is said: jiazafa fi kalamihi, i.e., he spoke without law and without insight. (mazhar) And his saying: 'fihima' i.e., in fatwa and judgment. (5) His saying: 'al-dahiyah' meaning the objectionable matter, the disaster, and the great affair. (mazhar)
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a mufti adab (etiquette) ruling quoting al-Fatawa al-Khayriyya, emphasizing the mufti's duty to distinguish between rajih (strong) and marjuh (weak) opinions and to act only on the rajih. It stresses thoroughness (tathabbut) and forbids recklessness (mujazafah) to avoid falsely attributing rulings to Allah. Following personal desire (hawa) and greed for wealth is prohibited as a grave sin. The commentary clarifies that 'mujazafah' means speaking without proper knowledge or methodology, and 'fihima' refers to both fatwa and judgment. This guards against the misunderstanding that a mufti can simply choose any opinion based on convenience.

Key Terms
الراجح (al-rajih): The preponderant opinion among conflicting views, determined by strength of evidence.
المجازفة (al-mujazafah): Speaking without law (qanun) and without insight (tabassur), i.e., recklessness.
الهوى (al-hawa): Personal desire or caprice that leads to biased rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required of the mufti and qadi in issuing answers?
A) Speed and efficiency.
B) Thoroughness and avoidance of recklessness.
C) Prioritizing the weaker opinion if it is easier.
D) Following the opinion of the majority.

Key Takeaway
The mufti and qadi must meticulously identify and follow the rajih opinion, avoiding any recklessness or personal desire, to avoid slandering Allah.

Prohibition of Hiring for Quran Recitation: The Mufti's Duty to Check Original Sources
Critique of hiring Quran reciters for reward

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi prohibition on hiring for Quran recitation, explain its rationale based on corrupted intention, and apply the mufti's adab in evaluating popular but unsound religious practices.

Core Idea
Hiring for Quran recitation turns worship into trade, negates reward, and the mufti must reject it by returning to original sources.

This lesson examines a critical issue in mufti adab: the practice of hiring someone to recite Quran for the deceased. Although widespread and often considered virtuous, many Hanafi masters have strongly opposed it. The author, a Hanafi jurist, demonstrates how this practice corrupts the reciter's intention, turns the Quran into a means of worldly gain, and leads to numerous evils. He emphasizes the mufti's duty to review original sources and not blindly follow popular custom. Understanding this ruling is essential for anyone issuing fatwa or advising the community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the practice under critique
The passage criticizes hiring someone to recite Quran with the intent to gift the reward to a deceased person. Recognize that this is a common practice in many Muslim societies.
The author calls it 'al-isti'jar 'ala al-qira'a'.
2
Understand the core problem: corrupted intention
The author argues that the reciter reads only for the fee, not for Allah, making the act pure riya' (show-off). Without sincere intention, no reward is obtained, so the hirer's aim fails.
The quote from Imam Qadikhan affirms that taking payment for remembrance prevents reward.
3
Trace the evidence: the dalil from Hanafi masters
The passage references Imam Qadikhan and the work Fath al-Qadir regarding the mu'adhdhin's payment. These are authoritative Hanafi sources. Understand that this is a transmitted proof (dalil naqli).
The author also mentions his own treatise and endorsements from later muftis like Ahmad al-Tahtawi.
4
Analyze the consequences of deeming it valid
The author lists several evils stemming from this practice: consuming orphan wealth, disturbing people, mixing genders, etc. This shows that a mufti must consider the broader societal harm when evaluating a practice.
These consequences strengthen the argument for prohibition.
5
Apply the adab: the mufti's duty to check original sources
The lesson title emphasizes 'reviewing the original source'. The mufti should not rely on popular opinion but must verify rulings from the foundational texts of the madhhab.
The author's treatise and its endorsements show that such critical review is part of the Hanafi tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهمية مراجعة المأخذ الأصلى شرح عقود رسم المفتی الدهر ، ولم يستأجر أحد أحداً على ذلك، لم يحصل به ضرر، بل الضرر صار في الاستئجار عليه، حيث صار القرآن مكسبا وحرفة يتجر بها، وصار القارئ منهم لا يقرأ شيئًا لوجه الله تعالى خالصا ، بل لا يقرأ إلا للأجرة، وهو الرياء المحض الذي هو إرادة العمل لغير الله تعالى، فمن أين يحصل له الثواب الذي طلب المستأجر أن يهديه لميته؟ وقد قال الإمام قاضيخان: "إن أخذ الأجر في مقابلة الذكر يمنع استحقاق الثواب". ومثله في "فتح القدير" في أخذ المؤذن الأجر. ولو علم أنه لا ثواب له، لم يدفع له فلسا واحدا، فصاروا يتوصلون إلى جمع الحطام الحرام بوسيلة الذكر والقرآن، وصار الناس يعتقدون ذلك من أعظم القرب، وهو من أعظم القبائح المترتبة على القول بصحة الاستئجار، مع غير ذلك مما يترتب عليه: من أكل أموال الأيتام، والجلوس في بيوتهم على فرشهم، وإقلاق النائمين بالصراخ، ودق الطبول، والغناء، واجتماع النساء والمردان، وغير ذلك من المنكرات الفظيعة، كما أوضحت ذلك كله مع بسط النقول عن أهل المذهب في رسالتي المسماة: "شفاء العليل وب الغليل في بطلان الوصية بالختمات والتهاليل". وعليها تقاريظ فقهاء أهل العصر، من أجلّهم خاتمة الفقهاء والعباد الناسكين، مفتى مصر القاهرة، سييدي المرحوم السيد أحمد الطحطاوي، صاحب الحاشية الفائقة على الدرّ المختار، رحمه الله تعالى.
The importance of reviewing the original source. Commentary on the contract of the mufti's code. If no one had hired anyone for that, no harm would have occurred; rather, the harm occurred in hiring, because the Quran became a means of earning and a trade to do business with, and the reciter among them does not read anything purely for the sake of Allah Almighty, but only reads for the fee, which is pure ostentation, which is intending an action for other than Allah Almighty. So how can he obtain the reward that the hirer asked him to gift to his deceased? And Imam Qadikhan said: "Taking payment in exchange for remembrance prevents entitlement to reward." And similar is in Fath al-Qadir regarding the mu'adhdhin taking payment. If he knew that there is no reward, he would not pay a single penny. So they resorted to collecting forbidden goods by means of remembrance and the Quran, and people came to believe that is among the greatest acts of worship, while it is among the greatest abominations resulting from deeming hiring valid, in addition to other things that follow from it: consuming the wealth of orphans, sitting in their houses on their furnishings, disturbing sleepers with screaming, beating drums, singing, gathering of women and beardless youths, and other terrible evils, as I have explained all of that along with expanding on the transmissions from the people of the madhhab in my treatise called "Shifa' al-Alil wa Bughyat al-Ghalil fi Butlan al-Wasiyya bi al-Khatmat wa al-Tahleel". And it has endorsements from the jurists of the era, including the foremost, the seal of jurists and devout worshippers, the mufti of Cairo, my master the late Sayyid Ahmad al-Tahtawi, author of the superior gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the practice of hiring someone to recite the Quran for reward. The author begins by stating the importance of reviewing original sources (the lesson's title). He argues that if no one hired for recitation, no harm would occur. The real harm arises from hiring, as it turns the Quran into a trade commodity. The reciter no longer reads purely for Allah but for payment, which is pure riya' (show-off). The hirer intends to send reward to the deceased, but how can the reciter obtain reward when his intention is worldly gain? This negates the purpose. The author cites Imam Qadikhan, who ruled that taking payment for remembrance prevents reward, and similar is in Fath al-Qadir regarding the mu'adhdhin. He emphasizes that if people knew there was no reward, they would not pay. This practice leads people to collect forbidden wealth through the Quran, and many evils follow: consuming orphan's wealth, disturbing sleepers, music, mixing of genders, etc. The author wrote a separate treatise on this, endorsed by the eminent mufti of Cairo, Sayyid Ahmad al-Tahtawi. The lesson demonstrates the mufti's duty to check original sources and avoid blind acceptance of widespread but unsound practices.

Key Terms
الاستئجار على القراءة (al-isti'jar 'ala al-qira'a): Hiring someone to recite Quran for a fee with the intention of gifting reward to the deceased
الرياء (al-riya'): Performing an act of worship for other than Allah, e.g., for worldly gain or praise
الثواب (al-thawab): Divine reward promised for sincere acts of worship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does hiring someone to recite Quran for a deceased person fail to achieve the intended benefit?
A) Because the reciter may not be qualified
B) Because the reciter's intention is for payment, not for Allah, so he gets no reward
C) Because the deceased cannot hear the recitation
D) Because the Quran cannot be recited for hire

Key Takeaway
Hiring for Quran recitation is prohibited because it corrupts the reciter's intention, negates reward, and leads to societal harms; a mufti must reject such practices by checking original sources.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

The Duty to Verify Original Sources in Fatwa
Case study on source verification in fatwa practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of muraja'at al-ma'khadh al-asli by identifying a fatwa error caused by reliance on secondary sources and correcting it using earlier Hanafi works.

Core Idea
A mufti must trace every ruling back to Zahir al-Riwayah – later compilations can misattribute positions.

A fatwa is only as strong as its source. This lesson examines a historical case where the Hanafi ruling on the repentance of one who insults the Prophet (saw) was misrepresented in later collections. By tracing the ruling back to early Hanafi works, we learn why original source verification is a sacred duty of the mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling and its apparent source
The passage presents a ruling from al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya: the sabb (insulter) must be killed and his repentance is not accepted. Note that this source attributes the ruling to al-Shifa and al-Sarim al-Maslul, which are not Hanafi works.
Al-Bazzaziyya is a later Hanafi fatwa collection, but the attribution is to non-Hanafi scholars.
2
Trace the chain of transmission of the error
Later Hanafi scholars like Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath followed al-Bazzazi without checking original Hanafi sources. This shows how errors propagate through taqlid without verification.
The error was perpetuated because scholars assumed later works were reliable without cross-checking.
3
Locate the correct Hanafi position in early sources
The author cites Kitab al-Kharaj by Abu Yusuf, Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi, and al-Nutaf. These sources clearly state that the sabb is an apostate (murtadd), and the ruling of apostasy includes acceptance of repentance.
Early works are from Zahir al-Riwayah or early authoritative commentaries.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction from al-Shifa
The passage explains that al-Shifa's statement 'but they said: it is apostasy' actually implies acceptance of repentance, because the Hanafis treated it as ridda, and ridda's repentance is accepted. The misconception arose from misreading this phrase.
Careful textual analysis reveals the correct meaning.
5
Derive the lesson for fatwa practice
The mufti must never rely solely on later compilations. Every ruling must be verified against the original madhhab sources (Zahir al-Riwayah, early commentaries). If a later work cites a non-Hanafi source, it must be treated with caution.
This is a core principle of Rasm al-Mufti: al-‘amal bi al-ashahh wa al-arsahh.

فرشهم، وإقلاق النائمين بالصراخ، ودق الطبول، والغناء، واجتماع النساء والمردان، وغير ذلك من المنكرات الفظيعة، كما أوضحت ذلك كله مع بسط النقول عن أهل المذهب في رسالتي المسماة: "شفاء العليل وب الغليل في بطلان الوصية بالختمات والتهاليل". وعليها تقاريظ فقهاء أهل العصر، من أجلّهم خاتمة الفقهاء والعباد الناسكين، مفتى مصر القاهرة، سیيدى المرحوم السيد أحمد الطحطاوي، صاحب الحاشية الفائقة على الدرّ المختار " رحمه الله تعالى.

Important verification of the original source. / Their bedding, and disturbing the sleepers with screaming, beating drums, singing, gathering of women and beardless youths, and other terrible evils, as I have clarified all of that with extensive quotations from the scholars of the madhhab in my treatise titled: "Shifa' al-'Ail wa Ball al-Ghalil fi Butlan al-Wasiyyah bi al-Khatamat wa al-Tahaliil". And upon it are endorsements from jurists of the era, among the greatest of them the seal of jurists and devout worshippers, the Mufti of Cairo Egypt, my master the deceased Sayyid Ahmad al-Tahtawi, author of the superior gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar, may Allah have mercy on him. / And among that is the issue of non-acceptance of repentance of the one who insults the noble personage, may Allah's peace and blessings be upon him. The author of al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyyah narrated: "It is obligatory to kill him according to us, and his repentance is not accepted even if he becomes Muslim." And he attributed that to al-Shifa by Qadi 'Iyad al-Maliki and al-Sarim al-Maslul by Ibn Taymiyyah al-Hanbali. Then the general scholars after him followed him on that and mentioned it in their books until the seal of the investigators Ibn al-Humam. / (1) His statement: "bi al-sarakh" means loud screaming. (Muzhir) / (2) His statement: "al-murdan" is plural of amrad: the young man whose beard has not grown. (Muzhir) / (3) His statement: "he attributed that to al-Shifa" and its text: "Abu Bakr ibn al-Mundhir said: The general scholars have agreed that whoever insults the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is to be killed. Among those who said that are: Malik ibn Anas, al-Layth, Ahmad, Ishaq, and it is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i. Al-Qadi Abu al-Fadl said: 'It is the requirement of the statement of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may Allah be pleased with him, and his repentance is not accepted according to these. And similar was said by Abu Hanifa and his companions, al-Thawri, the people of Kufa, and al-Auza'i regarding the Muslim, but they said: it is apostasy ... etc.' (Muzhir) And it is known that among the rulings of the apostate is the acceptance of his repentance and the dropping of killing by it. / (4) His statement: "until the seal of the investigators" where he said in al-Fath: "Whoever hates the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in his heart becomes an apostate, so the insulter is even more so, then he is killed according to us and his repentance is not accepted to drop the killing ... etc." Our master, Shaykh al-Islam Amin al-Din ibn al-Aal, Mufti of the Egyptian lands, said: "Indeed the author of al-Fath followed al-Bazzazi, and al-Bazzazi followed the author of al-Sarim, for he attributed in al-Bazzaziyyah what he narrated from that to him, and did not attribute it to any of the Hanafi scholars." (Muzhir) / (1) His statement: "with certainty of narrating acceptance of repentance" for the statement of al-Shifa: "but they said: it is apostasy ... etc." is explicit in acceptance of repentance because it is an emendation on his previous statement: "He is killed and his repentance is not accepted according to them." So it is known that his statement: "And similar was said by Abu Hanifa ... but they said: it is apostasy" means: he said that he is killed, but his repentance is accepted because the repentance of the apostate is accepted. So the people of Kufa agreed with Imam Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad on the like of it, and they differed regarding non-acceptance of his repentance because the apostate's repentance is accepted whatever his crime. The summary: that he is killed if he does not repent as is the ruling of apostasy, otherwise there would be no benefit to the mentioned emendation. Among those who explicitly stated acceptance of repentance according to us is Imam al-Subki in al-Sayf al-Maslul, and he was preceded in that also by Ibn Taymiyyah al-Hanbali in al-Sarim al-Maslul, for he explicitly stated in several places the acceptance of repentance according to the Hanafis, and that he is not killed. (Muzhir) / (2) His statement: "like Kitab al-Kharaj" and its text as the author mentioned: "And Abu Yusuf said: Any Muslim man who insults the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, or denies him, or reviles him, or disparages him, has disbelieved in Allah Almighty and his wife is separated from him; if he repents, otherwise he is killed." (Muzhir) / (3) His statement: "and Sharh Mukhtasar al-Imam al-Tahawi" narrated in Mu'in al-Hukkam attributing to him its form: "Whoever insults the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, or hates him, that is apostasy from him, his ruling is the ruling of apostates." And similarly it was narrated in Manh al-Ghaffar from the mentioned Mu'in al-Hukkam. (Muzhir) / (4) His statement: "al-Nutaf" and its text as the author mentioned: "Whoever insults the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is an apostate, and his ruling is the ruling of the apostate, and what is done to the apostate is done to him." And it is known that among the rulings of the apostate is acceptance of repentance and dropping of the killing by it. (Muzhir)
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a case study illustrating a critical principle for muftis: always verify fatwas from original madhhab sources. The author begins by referencing his earlier treatise on a related matter, then addresses a widely circulated ruling: that the repentance of someone who insults the Prophet (peace be upon him) is not accepted, and he must be killed. This ruling was attributed to the Hanafi school by later scholars like the author of al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya, who cited al-Shifa (by the Maliki Qadi 'Iyad) and al-Sarim al-Maslul (by the Hanbali Ibn Taymiyyah). Many later Hanafis, including Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath, followed this without checking earlier Hanafi sources. However, the author demonstrates that the original sources—such as Kitab al-Kharaj by Imam Abu Yusuf, Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi, and al-Nutaf—clearly state that such a person is an apostate, and apostates' repentance is accepted. The apparent contradiction in al-Shifa is resolved: the statement "but they said: it is apostasy" indicates that the Hanafis considered it apostasy (not that repentance is rejected), so the repentance is accepted. The author emphasizes that al-Bazzazi and Ibn al-Humam relied on non-Hanafi works without checking Hanafi foundations. This is a lesson in mufti adab: one must trace rulings back to authoritative Hanafi sources, not rely on later secondary works that may contain errors or cross-madhhab attributions.

Key Terms
مراجعة المأخذ الأصلى (muraja'at al-ma'khadh al-asli): The duty of a mufti to refer back to the original, authoritative sources of the madhhab rather than relying solely on later compilations.
الساب (al-sabb): One who insults the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him).
الفتاوى البزازية (al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya): A later Hanafi fatwa collection by Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Bazzazi (d. 827 AH), which sometimes contains rulings that need verification against earlier sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the error made by later Hanafi scholars like Ibn al-Humam regarding the ruling on the sabb?
A) They rejected the concept of apostasy for the sabb.
B) They relied on a non-Hanafi source (al-Shifa) without checking original Hanafi works.
C) They accepted the repentance of the sabb without requiring proof.
D) They followed the Maliki school instead of the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
A mufti's duty is to trace every ruling to its original Hanafi source; later compilations may contain errors that can only be corrected by returning to the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

Prohibition of Self-Study Fatwas Without a Teacher
Ruling on the condition of teacher training for issuing fatwas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that self-study of fiqh books without a teacher does not permit issuing fatwas, and apply the condition of tafaquh under a skilled teacher to evaluate the validity of a mufti's qualifications.

Core Idea
Self-study of fiqh books does not qualify one to issue fatwas; only tafaquh under a master makes ifta permissible.

This passage from Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti establishes a fundamental condition for the validity of ifta: the mufti must have studied under a skilled teacher (ustadh mahir). Mere reading of books, even authoritative ones, is insufficient. The author illustrates this with a personal anecdote where a correct fatwa was rejected by other muftis who blindly followed al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and then states the ruling clearly, supported by the fatwa of Ibn Hajar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
The passage states that it is not permissible for one who reads books on his own and has not studied under a skilled teacher to issue fatwas.
2
Identify the condition for valid ifta
The condition is tafaquh (studying fiqh) under a ustadh mahir (skilled teacher).
3
Identify the consequence of violation
The violator must be given ta'zir (discretionary punishment).
Ta'zir is not a fixed hadd penalty but is left to the judge's discretion.
4
Identify the evidence
The author cites a fatwa of Ibn Hajar and uses a logical argument: if fiqh could be obtained by lookup, no one would be ignorant.
The evidence includes both transmitted (fatwa) and rational argument.

(۲) قوله : "أن يعينني على إتمامها " فقلت : وفقه الله تعالى الإتمام، وأعانه بفضله عليه، وكان رحمه الله بدأ أولا في التسويد من الأول إلى الآخر، ثم شرع في التبييض، فبدأ أولا من الإجارة إلى الآخر ، ثم من أول الكتاب إلى شتى القضاء، فلما وصل إلى أثناءه اشتاق إلى مشاهدة رب الأرباب . . (مظ) فبادر نجله السعيد السيد محمد علاء الدين إلى تجريد الهوامش التي على نسخة والده .

لا تحسب الفقه تمراً أنت أكله لن تبلغ الفقه حتى تلعق الصبرا

٦٣
الإفتاء بمطالعة الكتب بنفسه
شرح عقود رسم المفتی وقد كنت مرة أفتيت بمسألة فى الوقف موافقا لما هو المسطور في عامة الكتب، وقد اشتبه فيها الأمر على الشيخ علاء الدين الحصـفـكـي عمـدة المتأخرين، فذكرها في الدر المختار " على خلاف الصواب، فوقع جوابی الذي أفتيت به بيد جماعة من مفتى البلاد، كتبوا في ظهره بخلاف ما أفتيت به موافقين لما وقع في " الدر المختار" ، وزاد بعض هؤلاء المفتين : " أن هذا الذى في "العلائي " هو الذي عليه العمل ؛ لأنه عمدة المتأخرين، وأنه إن كان عندكم خلافه لا نقبله منكم . فانظر إلى هذا الجهل العظيم، والتهور في الأحكام الشرعيّة، والإقدام على الفتيا بدون علم وبدون مراجعة . وليت هذا القائل راجع حاشية العلامة الشيخ إبراهيم الحلبي على "الدر المختار " ؛ فإنها (1) أقرب ما يكون إليه، فقد نبه فيها على أنه ما وقع للعلائي خطأ في التعبير .
مطلب
لا يجوز الإفتاء لمن طالع الكتب بنفسه و لم يتفقه
لدى أستاذ ماهر ، ووجوب تعزير من
خالفه
وقد رأيت في فتاوى العلامة ابن حجر سئل في شخص يقرأ ويطالع في الكتب الفقهية بنفسه، ولم يكن له شيخ ويفتى ويعتمد على مطالعته في الكتب، فهل يجوز له ذلك أم لا؟
إذ لو كان الفقه يحصل بمجرد القدرة على مراجعة المسألة من مظانها، لكان أسهل شيء، ولما احتاج إلى التفقه على أستاذ ماهر، وفكر ثاقب باهر، شعر :
لو كان هذا العلم يدرك بالمنى ما كنت تبصر في البرية جاهلا
رسائل ابن عابدین : ۲/ ۳۳۸)
(۱) قوله : " فإنها " الضمير راجع إلى "الحاشية" ، وقوله : "إليه" أى إلى هذا القائل والمعنى: أن حاشية العلامة الحلبي كان متيسر الحصول لهذا القائل .
Commentary on the Contracts of the Mufti. 62. Source of oversight in transmission. That annotation is unparalleled in its field; no one is free from needing to seek it. I ask Him, Glorified, to help me complete it (2). When a person of limited knowledge looks and sees a matter recorded in one or more books, he thinks this is the madhhab, and issues a fatwa accordingly, and says: 'These books are by the later scholars who reviewed the books of those before them and recorded in them what is relied upon for practice.' He does not realize that this is only generally true, and that they sometimes differ from it (3), as we have written for you. (1) His saying: 'unparalleled in its field' – in Hanafi fiqh, and Sharh al-Muhadhdhab of Imam al-Nawawi in Shafi'i fiqh is similar in precision of correction. The Imam says in his introduction: 'I say: It is not permissible for a mufti of the Shafi'i school, when relying on transmission, to be content with one or two works and the like from the books of the early scholars and most of the later scholars, due to the abundance of disagreement among them in determination and preference. Because this mufti is merely transmitting the Shafi'i school, and he does not obtain certainty that what is in those works is the Shafi'i school or the preferred view, because of the disagreement in them. This is not doubted by anyone with the least familiarity with the school. Rather, perhaps ten authors may assert something that is anomalous relative to the preferred view in the school, contrary to the majority, and perhaps contradicting a text of al-Shafi'i or multiple texts of his. You will see examples of that in this commentary, God willing. And I hope that if this book is completed, it will suffice without any other work, and one will know the Shafi'i school with certain knowledge, God willing.' (2) His saying: 'that He help me complete it' – I said: May God grant him success in completion and assist him with His grace. He, may God have mercy on him, first began drafting from beginning to end, then started to produce a fair copy. He began first from the chapter of Ijarah to the end, then from the beginning of the book to the chapter of Qada. When he reached the middle, he yearned to see the Lord of lords... (Mazhar) So his noble son, al-Sayyid Muhammad Ala al-Din, hastened to extract the marginal notes from his father's copy. (3) His saying: 'and that they sometimes differ from it' – The commentator said in his treatise 'Tanbih al-Wulah wa al-Hukkam': If you say: If this is the case, then it is not proper for a mufti to issue a fatwa merely by consulting a book, even if that book is well-known. I say: Yes, it is so. Poetry: Do not think fiqh is a date that you can eat; you will not attain fiqh until you taste patience. 63. Issuing fatwa by reading books oneself. Commentary on the Contracts of the Mufti. I once gave a fatwa on a matter of waqf in agreement with what is recorded in most books. The matter was ambiguous to Shaykh Ala al-Din al-Haskafi, the foremost of the later scholars, so he mentioned it in al-Durr al-Mukhtar contrary to the correct view. My fatwa answer fell into the hands of a group of muftis of the land; they wrote on its back in opposition to my fatwa, agreeing with what occurred in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Some of these muftis added: 'This which is in al-Ala'i is what is relied upon for practice, because he is the foremost of the later scholars, and if you have any contrary view, we do not accept it from you.' So look at this great ignorance, this recklessness regarding sacred rulings, and this audacity to issue fatwas without knowledge and without review. If only this speaker had consulted the gloss of the scholar Shaykh Ibrahim al-Halabi on al-Durr al-Mukhtar, for it (1) is the closest thing to him; he pointed out in it that what occurred to al-Ala'i was an error in expression. A desirable point: It is not permissible for one who reads books on his own and has not studied fiqh under a skilled teacher to issue fatwas, and it is obligatory to punish (ta'zir) those who violate this. I saw in the fatwas of the scholar Ibn Hajar: He was asked about a person who reads and studies the fiqh books on his own, without having a teacher, and issues fatwas relying on his reading of the books. Is that permissible for him or not? For if fiqh could be obtained merely by the ability to look up a matter in its sources, it would be the easiest thing, and there would be no need to study fiqh under a skilled teacher and a penetrating brilliant mind. Poetry: If this knowledge could be attained by wish, you would not see any ignorant person in the world. (Rasa'il Ibn Abidin: 2/338) (1) His saying: 'for it' – the pronoun refers to 'the gloss', and his saying 'to him' means to this speaker. The meaning: that the gloss of al-Halabi was easily accessible to this speaker.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti is a self-contained ruling on the conditions of valid ifta. It begins with a general warning: a person of limited knowledge who reads a ruling in one or more books often assumes it is the madhhab and issues fatwas accordingly, unaware that the authority of later books is only generally reliable and that errors occur. The footnotes reinforce this by citing Imam al-Nawawi's similar caution in the Shafi'i school: even ten authors may assert an anomalous position. The author then shares his personal experience with a waqf matter: his correct fatwa was rejected by other muftis who blindly followed al-Durr al-Mukhtar and al-Haskafi, without checking al-Halabi's gloss that clarified the error. This real-world example illustrates the danger of relying solely on books. The passage culminates in a formal ruling (مطلب): it is not permissible for one who reads books without studying under a skilled teacher to issue fatwas, and the violator must be given ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The evidence is the fatwa of Ibn Hajar, who argues that if fiqh could be obtained by mere lookup, no one would be ignorant. Thus, the lesson is a foundational rule of Hanafi ifta: book knowledge without scholarly training is insufficient.

Key Terms
الإفتاء (al-ifta'): The act of issuing a legal ruling (fatwa) in response to a question.
مطالعة الكتب (mutala'at al-kutub): Reading books on one's own without the guidance of a teacher.
أستاذ ماهر (ustadh mahir): A skilled teacher who is proficient in fiqh and can train a student in proper istinbat and tarjih.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment imposed by a judge or ruler for offenses that do not have fixed hadd penalties.
مطلب (matlab): A heading indicating a desired or important ruling; often introduces a subsidiary benefit or rule in Hanafi legal works.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible because he can verify rulings from reliable books.
B) It is not permissible, and he deserves ta'zir (discretionary punishment).
C) It is permissible only if he has memorized the major books.
D) It is not permissible unless he is a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
Self-study of fiqh books without a teacher does not permit issuing fatwas; the mufti must have studied under a skilled teacher, and violators are subject to ta'zir.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
intermediate

The Qualified Mufti: Beyond Books to Mastery
Prohibition of fatwa by non-experts; description of a truly qualified mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential qualifications of a mufti and explain why giving fatwa from books alone is prohibited.

Core Idea
A mufti must be a master with a natural faculty, not a mere book-reader.

In the Hanafi tradition, the role of the mufti is sacred—an intermediary between the people and Allah. This passage from Ibn ‘Abidin’s Rasm al-Mufti draws a sharp line: a person who merely reads books, even dozens, cannot issue fatwas. Only the expert (māhir) who has internalized fiqh through study, apprenticeship, and a God-given natural perception is qualified. This lesson unpacks that prohibition and the three conditions that make a true mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
Recognize that the passage explicitly forbids giving fatwa by anyone who relies solely on book study, even if they have studied under teachers but lack deeper mastery.
The prohibition is absolute: 'not permissible in any way'.
2
Distinguish layman (ʿāmmī) from expert (māhir)
A ʿāmmī is someone without proper training; a māhir has taken knowledge from its people and developed a natural faculty. Understand the contrast.
The māhir 'distinguishes correct from incorrect'.
3
List the three conditions of a qualified mufti
From the footnotes: complete mastery in fiqh, learning from expert teachers with their permission, and acquiring the natural faculty (malaka nafsāniyya).
These are the criteria for being a māhir.
4
Explain the natural faculty (malaka nafsāniyya)
This is an intuitive grasp of fiqh, called 'fiqh of the soul' or 'sharpness of intelligence'. It enables the mufti to apply rulings correctly even when texts are ambiguous.
It is a divine gift attained through sincere effort.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Consider an online fatwa platform: a responder with a degree but no apprenticeship would violate this rule. The mufti must have studied under masters and received ijazah.
This prevents countless evils (mafāsid).

الإفتاء بمطالعة الكتب بنفسه فأجاب بقوله : " لا يجوز له الإفتاء بوجه من الوجوه ؛ لأنه عامى جاهل لا يدرى ما يقول، بل الذى يأخذ العلم عن المشايخ المعتبرين لا يجوز له أن يفتي من كتاب، ولا من كتابين " بل قال النووي رحمه الله تعالى : ولا من عشرة فإن العشرة والعشرين قد يعتمدون كلهم على مقالة ضعيفة في المذهب، فلا يجوز تقليدهم فيها ) . بخلاف الماهر الذى أخذ العلم عن أهله ، وصارت له فيه ملكة نفسانية، فإنه يميز الصحيح من غيره، ويعلم المسائل وما يتعلّق بها على الوجه المعتد به ، فهذا هو الذي يفتى الناس، ويصلح أن يكون واسطة بينهم وبين الله تعالى، وأما غيره فيلزمه إذا تسور هذا المنصب الشريف التعزير البليغ والزجر الشديد الزاجر ذلك لأمثاله عن هذا الأمر القبيح الذى يؤدى إلى مفاسد لا تحصى . والله تعالى أعلم . اهـ . (3) قوله : "إذا تسور" مأخوذ من قولهم: "فلان" تسور الحائط " إذا صعد عليه، أو من قولهم: "تسور السوار إذا لبسه، وعلى كل من الوجهين يكون الكلام مبنيا على التشبيه. (مظ)
[The issue of] giving fatwa by studying books himself, so he answered: 'It is not permissible for him to give fatwa in any way because he is a common ignorant person who does not know what he says. Rather, he who takes knowledge from reputable teachers is not permitted to give fatwa from one book, nor from two books.' Rather, al-Nawawi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Nor from ten, for ten or twenty may all rely on a weak statement in the madhhab, so it is not permissible to imitate them in that.' Contrary to the expert who took knowledge from its people and developed a natural faculty in it, for he distinguishes the correct from other, and knows the issues and what relates to them in the proper manner. This is the one who gives fatwa to people and is fit to be an intermediary between them and Allah. As for others, if he presumes to occupy this noble position, he deserves severe punishment and strong deterrence that will deter his likes from this ugly act which leads to countless evils. And Allah knows best. End. (1) His saying: 'So it is not permissible to imitate them in that' refers to the introduction of al-Majmu' Sharh al-Muhadhdhab attached to this book, chapter on rulings of muftis, seventh issue. (2) His saying: 'Contrary to the expert' is an explanation of what is required for the mufti, and it is three things: 1- Complete mastery in fiqh, attained by occupying oneself with fiqh, teaching it, discussing it, and studying it for a considerable period. 2- Learning fiqh from expert teachers and training in giving fatwa under them; then it is not permissible for him to give fatwa or judge unless his teachers testify to his competence and permit him to do so. This is because jurists often mention a ruling absolutely while intending the restricted ruling that must be observed here, but they omit the restrictions relying on their mention in other places and on the understanding of the listener. So mere study of fiqh books may lead to the opposite of what is intended. 3- Acquiring a natural faculty by which the various aspects of fiqh and what relates to them are known, called 'fiqh of the soul' and 'sharpness of intelligence'. That is a favor of Allah which He gives to whom He wills. So let the one who aspires to iftaa turn to his Lord to attain his goal. (3) His saying: 'if he presumes' is derived from their saying: 'so-and-so scaled the wall' if he climbed it, or from their saying: 'he put on a bracelet' if he wore it. In both interpretations, the statement is based on analogy.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the qualifications required for a mufti, specifically addressing the prohibition of giving fatwa merely by consulting books. The author begins with a response to a question: a person who tries to give fatwa by studying books himself is not permitted to do so because he is a layman (ʿāmmī) and ignorant. Even one who has studied under reputable teachers is not allowed to give fatwa from one or two books. Al-Nawawi is cited to extend this to ten or twenty books, as a group may all rely on a weak opinion in the madhhab. The text then contrasts this with the expert (al-māhir) who has taken knowledge from its people and developed a natural faculty (malaka nafsāniyya). Such a person can distinguish correct from incorrect and knows the issues and their relevant details. He is the one qualified to give fatwa and act as an intermediary between people and Allah. For anyone else who presumes to this noble station, severe punishment and strong deterrence are required. The footnotes elaborate on the three necessary conditions: complete mastery in fiqh, learning from expert teachers with their permission, and acquiring a natural faculty called 'fiqh of the soul'. The passage emphasizes that mere book study is insufficient and can lead to error.

Key Terms
الماهر (al-māhir): The expert mufti who has attained complete mastery in fiqh, studied under teachers, and developed a natural faculty distinguishing correct rulings.
ملكة نفسانية (malaka nafsāniyya): A natural faculty or internal disposition that enables one to intuitively grasp fiqh and its nuances.
عامى (ʿāmmī): A layperson without proper fiqh training, ignorant of the details of the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Ten books
D) No number of books alone suffices

Key Takeaway
A mufti must be a māhir with a natural faculty, approved by his teachers; giving fatwa from books alone is prohibited and leads to great evil.

Distinguishing Ikhtilaf al-Qawl from Ikhtilaf al-Riwaya
Defining two types of ikhtilaf and listing causes of narration differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to DISTINGUISH between ikhtilaf al-qawl and ikhtilaf al-riwaya and APPLY the maxim 'al-rajih qawl wa al-marjuh riwaya' to determine which narration is relied upon for fatwa.

Core Idea
Ikhtilaf al-qawl is from the mujtahid; ikhtilaf al-riwaya is from the narrator—the stronger is the true opinion.

When multiple rulings are attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, a mufti must determine whether the difference reflects a genuine difference in the imam's opinion or a transmission error. This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti establishes the critical distinction between ikhtilaf al-qawlayn (difference of opinions) and ikhtilaf al-riwayatayn (difference of narrations). Understanding this distinction is foundational for correct fatwa work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ikhtilaf
Read the opening definition: ikhtilaf al-qawlayn and ikhtilaf al-riwayatayn. Note that qawlayn are the explicit opinions of the mujtahid himself, while riwayatayn are what is narrated from him, possibly with errors.
Keep in mind: the mujtahid is the source of qawl; the narrator is the source of riwaya.
2
Trace the source of each difference
The text states: 'the difference in the two opinions is from the side of the one from whom it is transmitted, not the transmitter, and the difference in the two narrations is the opposite.' Recognize that qawl is attributed to the imam, riwaya to the narrator.
This distinction determines how you evaluate conflicting reports.
3
List the five causes of ikhtilaf al-riwaya
With the help of al-Balyhī's enumeration, understand the five scenarios that produce differing narrations: (1) error in hearing, (2) the imam retracting an opinion, (3) giving answers by qiyas in one instance and istihsan in another, (4) answering from the angle of ruling versus precaution, and (5) transmission of an earlier opinion before retraction.
These causes show that not every difference reflects a true difference in the imam's final view.
4
Apply the maxim 'al-rajih qawl wa al-marjuh riwaya'
When two narrations conflict, determine which is stronger in transmission. The stronger is considered the imam's actual opinion (qawl); the weaker is merely a narration (riwaya) and is not relied upon for fatwa.
This maxim is a practical tool for the mufti to prioritize.
5
Analyze the critique and response in the text
The author objects that some of the five causes (especially the last three) could also apply to qawl. The response clarifies that 'riwaya' is often used broadly to include both qawl and riwaya. Understand that scholarly usage can be flexible, but the technical distinction remains valid.
This nuance sharpens your analytical precision.

Thus in al-Tahrir by the muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam. The difference between ikhtilaf al-qawl and ikhtilaf al-riwaya. Topic: On the difference between ikhtilaf al-qawl and ikhtilaf al-riwaya. Know that ikhtilaf al-riwayatayn is not of the category of ikhtilaf al-qawlayn; because the two opinions (qawlayn) are the explicit text of the mujtahid, unlike the two narrations (riwayatayn). So the difference in the two opinions is from the side of the one from whom it is transmitted, not the transmitter, and the difference in the two narrations is the opposite, as mentioned by the muhaqqiq Ibn Amir Haj in his commentary on al-Tahrir. Topic: On explaining the five ways of ikhtilaf al-riwaya. But he mentioned after it (2) from Imam Abu Bakr al-Balyhī in al-Durar (3): "Indeed, the difference in narration from Abu Hanifa is from several aspects:" ... Examples: Error in hearing, such as he answers with the letter of negation when asked about an incident, and he says 'not permissible', so the narrator becomes confused and transmits what he heard. And from them: That he has an opinion (1) from which he has retracted, and some of those who attend to him know of his retraction, so they narrate the second, while others do not know, so they narrate the first. And from them: That he stated one (2) by way of qiyas and the other by way of istihsan, so each hears one of them and transmits as he heard. End. And from them: That the answer in a matter is from two aspects: from the aspect of ruling and from the aspect of precaution, so each transmits as he heard. I say: Except for the first aspect (3), the difference in the two narrations is from... (1) His statement: 'And from them that he has an opinion' said in al-Tahbir: This is closer than the first. (2) His statement: 'And from them that he stated one' the apparent is that what is by way of qiyas is often the first, because it is established that qiyas takes precedence over istihsan except in some matters, so qiyas is like the opinion that has been retracted from, and istihsan is like the opinion retracted to, and the retracted-from precedes the retracted-to. This expression is better than what was transmitted from Imam al-Balyhī in al-Tahbir. (3) His statement: 'Except for the first aspect' is an objection to the muhaqqiq's distinction between ikhtilaf al-qawl and ikhtilaf al-riwaya from two aspects: First: The aspects he mentioned for ikhtilaf al-riwaya are not specific to ikhtilaf al-riwaya except the first; the remaining three can also be causes for ikhtilaf al-aqwal. Second: Not every one of these aspects applies to every particular in which there is a difference; rather, some apply to one and others to another. The answer to the first: The word 'riwaya' in the statement of Imam al-Balyhī, 'that the difference in narration from Abu Hanifa is from aspects', is used in a general sense encompassing both opinion and narration, not in the technical sense, and such usage is common among jurists and usuliyyun. The answer to the second: The muhaqqiq himself stated it in his commentary where he said 'I say: Then'
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti is a methodological discussion on how a mufti should understand differences in rulings attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa. The author first clarifies a critical distinction: ikhtilaf al-qawlayn (difference between two opinions) refers to the mujtahid's own explicit statements, while ikhtilaf al-riwayatayn (difference between two narrations) refers to discrepancies in what is narrated from him. The key is that qawlayn are directly from the mujtahid (source-oriented), whereas riwayatayn depend on the narrators (transmitter-oriented). The author then cites Imam al-Balyhī's five causes for differences in narration: (1) Error in hearing, where the mufti's answer is misunderstood; (2) Retraction, where some students know of the retraction and others do not; (3) Different contexts, such as answering by qiyas (analogy) in one instance and istihsan (juristic preference) in another; (4) Different aspects within one case, like ruling vs. precaution; (5) [Implied from the list] Possibly other causes like transmission of earlier opinions before retraction. The marginal notes add that the stronger view is considered the true 'qawl' and the weaker merely a 'riwaya', and that scholars often use 'riwaya' loosely to include 'qawl' as well. The author then critiques whether all five causes truly apply only to riwaya, arguing that the first is specific to riwaya, but the others could also cause differences in aqwal. The responses show that the term was used broadly, and the muhaqqiq himself implicitly addressed this. The lesson teaches muftis to carefully analyze whether a disputed ruling reflects a genuine difference in the imam's opinion or a transmission artifact.

Key Terms
اختلاف القولين (ikhtilaf al-qawlayn): Difference in the explicit opinions of the mujtahid himself.
اختلاف الروايتين (ikhtilaf al-riwayatayn): Difference in what is narrated from the mujtahid, often due to transmission errors or contextual variations.
الراجح قول والمرجوح رواية (al-rajih qawl wa al-marjuh riwaya): The ruling that the stronger narration is considered the true opinion, and the weaker is merely a transmitted narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a cause specifically for ikhtilaf al-riwaya and not generally for ikhtilaf al-qawl?
A) The imam retracting an opinion
B) Error in hearing
C) Answering by qiyas vs. istihsan
D) Answering from the aspect of ruling vs. precaution

Key Takeaway
A mufti must first determine whether a difference is in qawl or riwaya; the stronger riwaya is considered the true qawl and is relied upon for fatwa.

Meaning of Ijtihad for the Mufti: Restricted Ijtihad in Hanafi Fatwa Methodology
Definition of the type of ijtihad required for a Hanafi mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of ijtihad required for a Hanafi mufti and apply the grounds for preferring a companion's opinion over the Imam's when constructing a fatwa.

Core Idea
The mufti exercises restricted ijtihad (al-mujtahid al-muqayyad) by weighing transmitted opinions within the Hanafi school.

This appendix clarifies that the ijtihad relevant to a mufti is not absolute independence but a disciplined practice of weighing existing opinions within the Hanafi madhhab. The passage outlines three valid justifications for preferring a companion's view over the Imam's and affirms that later mujtahids' preferences remain authoritative. Understanding this restricted ijtihad is essential for any mufti issuing fatwas today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of ijtihad
Read al-Biri's statement: 'The meaning of ijtihad is one of the two ijtihads, which is the restricted mujtahid.' This tells you that the mufti is a mujtahid muqayyad, not an absolute mujtahid.
2
List the three grounds for tarjih
The treatise mentions three reasons to prefer the opinion of a companion: weakness of the Imam's evidence, necessity/custom, and difference of era. Note each.
These are not exhaustive but represent the main justifications.
3
Recognize the authority of later mujtahids
Shaykh Qasim affirms that later scholars did engage in tarjih and tashih, and their preferences are binding. Understand that this continuity keeps the madhhab alive.
4
Apply the concept to a fatwa scenario
Consider a modern case where custom has changed. Using these principles, a mufti may prefer a companion's view over the Imam's if the evidence is weaker or custom demands it.
This requires competence in usul and knowledge of the school's transmitted opinions.

Source Text

تتمة في المراد بالاجتهاد هنا
وقال في رسالة المسماة "رفع الغشاء في وقت العصر والعشاء" : " لا يرجح قول صاحبيه أو أحدهما على قوله إلا لموجب، وهو إما لضعف دليل الإمام، وإما للضرورة والتعامل كترجيح قولهما في المزارعة والمعاملة. وإما لأن خلافهما له بسبب اختلاف العصر والزمان، وأنه لو شاهد ما وقع في عصرهما لوافقهما ، كعدم القضاء بظاهر العدالة" .
ويوافق ذلك ما قاله العلامة المحقق الشيخ قاسم في "تصحيحه"، ونصه : " على أن المجتهدين لم يفقدوا حتى نظروا في المختلف، ورجحوا وصححوا، فشهدت مصنفاتهم بترجيح قول أبي حنيفة، والأخذ بقوله إلا في مسائل يسيرة اختاروا الفتوى فيها على قولهما، أو قول أحدهما وإن كان الآخر مع الإمام ، كما اختاروا قول أحدهما فيما لا نص فيه للإمام ؛ للمعاني التي أشار إليها القاضى ) ، بل اختاروا قول زفر في مقابلة قول الكل لنحو ذلك . وترجيحاتهم وتصحيحاتهم ،باقية، فعلينا اتباع الراجح والعمل به، كما لو أفتوا في حياتهم ."
تتمة :
قال العلامة البيرى : " والمراد بالاجتهاد أحد الاجتهادين(؟)، وهو المجتهد المقيد"
Appendix on the meaning of ijtihad here.
And it is said in the treatise 'Raf' al-Ghisha' fi Waqt al-Asr wa al-Isha': 'He does not prefer the opinion of his two companions or one of them over his opinion except for a reason, which is either due to the weakness of the Imam's evidence, or due to necessity and dealing (custom), like preferring their opinion in sharecropping and partnership. Or because his disagreement with them is due to the difference of era and time, and that if he had witnessed what occurred in their time he would have agreed with them, such as not judging based on apparent justice.'
And this is supported by what the verifying scholar Shaykh Qasim said in his 'Tashih': 'Indeed, the mujtahids did not cease to examine what differed, and they gave preference and corrected, so their works testify to preferring the opinion of Abu Hanifa and adopting his opinion except in a few issues where they chose the fatwa according to his two companions, or one of them even if the other was with the Imam, just as they chose the opinion of one of them in matters where there is no text from the Imam; due to the meanings that al-Qadi referred to. Rather, they chose the opinion of Zufar in opposition to the opinion of all for similar reasons. And their preferences and corrections remain, so it is upon us to follow the preponderant and act upon it, just as if they had issued fatwas during their lifetime.'
Appendix:
Al-Allama al-Biri said: 'The meaning of ijtihad is one of the two ijtihads, which is the restricted mujtahid.'
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This appendix (tātimma) clarifies the type of ijtihad relevant to the mufti in the Hanafi madhhab. It begins by quoting a treatise to explain when a mufti may prefer the opinion of a companion over the Imam's. The reasons are: (1) weakness of the Imam's evidence, (2) necessity and customary practice (like in sharecropping where the companions' view is preferred due to widespread custom), (3) difference in time and circumstances—the Imam might have agreed if he had lived in a later era where conditions changed, such as not relying on apparent justice in judgments. The passage then reinforces this with the words of 'Allamah Qasim, who affirms that later mujtahids (muftis) did not stop revising and correcting opinions. Their works show that while the Imam's opinion is usually preferred, in a few issues they chose the companions' view or even Zufar's view over everyone else's. These scholarly preferences remain authoritative, and we must follow the preponderant opinion as if the earlier scholars issued fatwas in our time. Finally, al-Biri defines 'ijtihad' in this context as 'one of the two ijtihads'—specifically 'al-mujtahid al-muqayyad' (the restricted mujtahid), i.e., one who is capable of exercising ijtihad within the boundaries of the Hanafi madhhab, not an absolute mujtahid. Thus, this passage teaches that the mufti's role involves a limited, school-restricted ijtihad based on careful weighing of existing opinions and evidence.

Key Terms
المجتهد المقيد (al-mujtahid al-muqayyad): A scholar who practices ijtihad within the framework of the Hanafi madhhab by weighing its transmitted opinions, not an independent formulator of new rules.
الاجتهادين (al-ijtihadayn): The two types of ijtihad: absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad). Here, the reference is to the latter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Absolute ijtihad (mujtahid mutlaq).
B) Restricted ijtihad (mujtahid muqayyad).
C) The ijtihad of the companions of the Prophet.
D) The ijtihad of the early jurists from all schools.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti exercises restricted ijtihad (al-mujtahid al-muqayyad) by weighing transmitted opinions within the school, and may prefer a companion's view over the Imam's only for valid reasons such as weak evidence, necessity, or changed circumstances.

Defining Ahliyyah for Ijtihad and Fatwa
Defining the legal competence (ahliyyah) for fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ahliyyah for ijtihad and fatwa according to the Hanafi school and distinguish between a mujtahid mufti and a muqallid mufti.

Core Idea
Ahliyyah for fatwa requires knowledge and the malakah to distinguish between opinions.

In this passage, the author defines the key concept of ahliyyah (legal competence) required for giving fatwa. This is not about general knowledge; it specifically refers to the capacity to examine evidence and differentiate between various scholarly opinions. Understanding this distinction is essential for knowing who may issue fatwa and under what conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'ahliyyah' as used in the context of ijtihad and fatwa. Note that this is a technical legal term, not general capacity.
The term appears in the title and is clarified in the commentary.
2
Extract the core components of the definition
The author states that ahliyyah means being knowledgeable (عالم), able to distinguish between different statements (مميزاً بين الأقاويل), and having the ability (قدرة) to derive rulings. Break each component down.
These are shurut (conditions) for the mufti's competence.
3
Understand the term 'malakah'
The passage from al-Bahr introduces malakah (faculty) as the ability to acquire knowledge and provide answers. This is a deeper quality than mere memorization.
Malakah indicates a ingrained skill, not just theoretical knowledge.
4
Apply the distinction between mujtahid and muqallid mufti
The footnote contrasts Ibn al-Humam, who could evaluate proofs and deviate from the Imam's view, with ordinary muftis who cannot. This shows that ahliyyah has degrees.
The capacity to deviate from the madhhab's relied-upon view requires a higher level of ahliyyah.

Additionally, it mentions having the capacity (malakah) to acquire knowledge and provide answers. The passage is incomplete in this window, cutting off the definition, but it highlights the distinction between a mujtahid and a muqallid mufti.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal competence for ijtihad and fatwa: being knowledgeable, able to distinguish between opinions, and having the capacity to derive rulings.

Justification for Fatwa by Non-Mujtahids: Hikayah as the Basis
Explaining why non-mujtahids may issue fatwa through transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a non-mujtahid may issue a fatwa in the Hanafi school and identify the concept of hikayah as the mechanism for transmission.

Core Idea
A non-mujtahid’s fatwa is a reliable narration of the mujtahid’s ruling, not an independent ijtihad.

A pressing practical question arises: if only a mujtahid can give fatwa, how did later Hanafi scholars continue to issue fatwas? The passage addresses this by distinguishing between ifta’ (independent ruling) and hikayah (narration). The mufti today acts as a narrator, conveying rulings that earlier mashayikh have already weighed and preferred. This does not require full ijtihad but does require skill: knowledge of the madhhab, awareness of people’s conditions, and the ability to apply the preponderant view. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The text begins by addressing a potential objection: if only a mujtahid may issue fatwa, how can non-mujtahids in later times do so? Recognize this as the central challenge.
2
Distinguish ifta' from hikayah
The author responds that what issues from a non-mujtahid is not fatwa in the true sense, but a narration (hikayah) of the mujtahid’s statement. Grasp this distinction as the key to the solution.
Hikayah does not require independent knowledge of the original evidence.
3
Understand the role of the mashayikh
The mashayikh (later authorities) examined the evidences of Abu Hanifa and his associates, gave preponderance (tarjih), and ruled accordingly. They filled their books with proofs and then stated, 'The fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf.' This shows they operated within the madhhab, not outside it.
4
Define the qualification for today's mufti
The passage concludes that the mufti must possess 'ahliyyah naql al-fatwa' – capacity to transmit fatwa. This includes a degree of ijtihad, understanding of people's conditions, intelligence, and vigilance. The mufti does not reinvent the madhhab but applies its preponderant rulings.
This qualification is distinct from absolute ijtihad.

Source Text
