۱۳
-- وروينا عن السلف وفضلاء الخلف من التوقف عن الفتيا (1) أشياء كثيرة معروفة، نذكر منها أحرفًا تبركًا.
وروينا عن عبد الرحمن بن أبي ليلى قال : أدركت عشرين ومائة من الأنصار من أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم يُسأل أحدهم عن المسألة، فيردها هذا إلى هذا، وهذا إلى هذا، حتى ترجع إلى الأول " وفي رواية: "ما منهم من يحدث بحديث إلا ود أن أخاه كفاه إياه ، (Y). ولا يُستفتى عن شيء إلا ود أن أخاه كفاه الفتيا " (٢) .
وعن ابن مسعود وابن عباس رضی الله عنهم : من أفتى عن كل ما يسأل فهو مجنون " (۳). وعن الشعبي والحسن وأبى حصين - بفتح الحاء التابعين، قالوا: "إن أحدكم ليفتى في المسألة، ولو وردت على عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه لجمع لها أهل بدر - وعن عطاء بن السائب التابعى : أدركت أقواماً يسأل أحدهم عن الشيء فيتكلم وهو يرعد ".
هاب الفتيا بلفظ : فليطلب لنفسه المخرج (١ : ٥٠) ، وابن الصلاح في أول فتاويه : ۸،۷/۱
(۱) قوله : "التوقف عن الفتيا" وذلك لما ورد في الأحاديث من الشدة فيها، من ذلك ما روى عن أبي هريرة رضى الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «من أفتى بفتيا من غير ثبت فإنما إثمه على من أفتاه . أخرجه الدارمي في باب الفتيا وما فيها من الشدة، والحاكم فى كتاب العلم ، وقال : صحيح على شرط الشيخين ولم يخرجاه، ولا أعرف له علة، ووافقه الذهبي على ذلك .
(۲) أخرجه الدارمی فی مسنده باب في الذي يفتى الناس في كل ما يستفتي : ١/ ۲۷۲ ، والهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد : ۱۸۳/۱ وقال : رجاله موثقون .
(۳) رواه ابن سعد في الطبقات الكبرى : ٣٦٠/٤ ، والدارمي في مسنده، باب من هاب الفتيا وكره التنطع والتبدع : ٢٤٩،٢٤٨/١ .
Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. 12. And each of them mentioned pearls that the other two did not mention. I have studied the books of the three, and summarized a concise collection encompassing all the important matters they mentioned, and added to them pearls from scattered statements of the companions. And with Allah is success. -2- Know that issuing fatwa is great in danger, immense in standing, abundant in merit. Because the mufti is the inheritor of the prophets (peace and blessings be upon them) and fulfills a communal obligation, but he is subject to error. For this reason they said: 'The mufti is the deputy of Allah Almighty.' And we narrated from Ibn al-Munkadir that he said: The scholar is between Allah Almighty and His creation, so let him consider how he enters between them. (1): And piety. He was a pious Imam, abundant in intellect, good in demeanor, well-versed in principles and branches, advanced in seeking knowledge until he became a byword, and he exerted himself in obedience and worship. He was born in the year 577 AH in the town of Sharkhan near Shahrzur from the districts of Irbil, and he was predominantly known by the nickname Ibn al-Salah. He was a shaykh from the shaykhs of his town who were pointed to, and he was famous by his lineage to his town and his madhhab, so he is al-Shahrazuri, al-Sharkhani, al-Shafi'i. Among his works are: 'The Utmost Hope and Question in the Science of the Hadith of the Messenger', printed under the title 'Muqaddimat Ibn al-Salah fi Ulum al-Hadith' which holds a special status among scholars; 'Adab al-Mufti wa al-Mustafti'; 'Silat al-Nasik fi Sifat al-Manasik'; 'Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyyin' (unfinished); 'Sharh al-Waraqat fi al-Usul'; and 'Fatawa Ibn al-Salah' in tafsir, hadith, usul, and fiqh. He used to give fatwa according to the madhhab of Imam al-Shafi'i, but he refrained from issuing fatwa some time before his death out of reverence for its status, and he used to say: In the city there is someone who can take my place. Imam Muhyi al-Din al-Nawawi studied under him, and al-Nawawi said about him: 'He was the first of my teachers, the Imam unanimously recognized for his knowledge, his asceticism, his piety, his abundant worship, and his great virtue.' Ibn Khallikan said: When the word 'al-Shaykh' is absolutely used among hadith scholars, he is meant. He died in 643 AH. His biography is in Shadharat al-Dhahab (2215), al-Bidayah wa al-Nihayah (13/168), Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya al-Kubra by al-Subki (5/137), Wafayat al-A'yan (3/212), and Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyyin by al-Isnawi (2/133). (1) Narrated by al-Bayhaqi in al-Madkhal, in the chapter on caution in fatwa and deliberation, p. 439, no. 821, and by al-Khatib in al-Faqih wa al-Mutafaqqih 3:168, and by al-Darimi in the chapter on... Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. 13. And we narrated from the early predecessors and the virtuous later generations many well-known things about refraining from fatwa (1). We will mention a few for blessing. And we narrated from Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla that he said: I encountered one hundred and twenty of the Ansar from the companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). One of them would be asked about a matter, and he would refer it to another, and that one to another, until it returned to the first. In another narration: 'None of them would narrate a hadith except that he wished his brother had sufficed him.' (Y). And he would not be asked about anything except that he wished his brother had sufficed him the fatwa. (2) And from Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them): 'Whoever gives fatwa about everything he is asked is insane.' (3) And from al-Sha'bi, al-Hasan, and Abi Husayn (with fat-ha on the ha), the Tabi'in, they said: 'One of you gives fatwa on a matter which, had it been presented to Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him), he would have gathered the people of Badr for it.' And from Ata' ibn al-Sa'ib al-Tabi'i: I encountered people; if one of them was asked about something, he would speak while trembling.' Those who dreaded fatwa would say: Let him seek a way out for himself (1:50), and Ibn al-Salah in the beginning of his Fatawa: 7/1,8. (1) His statement: 'Refraining from fatwa' because of the severity mentioned in the hadiths. Among that is what was narrated from Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Whoever gives a fatwa without firm knowledge, his sin is upon the one who consulted him.' Narrated by al-Darimi in the chapter on fatwa and its severity, and by al-Hakim in Kitab al-Ilm, and he said: Sahih according to the condition of the two shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim) but they did not record it, and I know no defect in it, and al-Dhahabi agreed. (2) Narrated by al-Darimi in his Musnad, chapter on the one who gives fatwa to people about everything he is asked, 1:272, and by al-Haythami in Majma' al-Zawa'id, 1:183, who said: Its narrators are trustworthy. (3) Narrated by Ibn Sa'd in al-Tabaqat al-Kubra, 4:360, and al-Darimi in his Musnad, chapter on those who feared fatwa and disliked extremism and innovation, 1:248-249.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage, from Imam al-Nawawi's 'Adab al-Fatwa', opens by highlighting the immense gravity and merit of issuing fatwas. The mufti is described as an inheritor of the prophets and fulfills a communal obligation (fard kifaya), yet he is exposed to error. Hence the famous statement: 'The mufti is the deputy of Allah.' The text then provides a biographical sketch of Ibn al-Salah (d. 643/1245), a prominent Shafi'i scholar and authority in hadith and fatwa, to illustrate the ideal qualities of a mufti: deep knowledge, piety (wara'), and intellectual excellence. Despite his stature, Ibn al-Salah himself refrained from issuing fatwas before his death out of reverence. The passage then proceeds to cite numerous reports from the salaf (early generations) demonstrating their extreme caution and reluctance to issue fatwas. For example, Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla saw 120 companions of the Ansar who would refer questions to one another to avoid giving fatwa themselves. Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Abbas are quoted saying that whoever gives a fatwa on everything asked is 'insane.' These reports underscore the principle that fatwa is a serious responsibility, not to be undertaken lightly. The footnotes provide hadith sources, including a narration from Abu Hurayra that the sin of a fatwa given without certain knowledge falls upon the mufti. This passage thus instills in the student the proper adab: humility, fear of error, and the importance of referring to more knowledgeable scholars when in doubt.

Key Terms
الإفتاء (al-ifta'): The act of issuing a legal ruling (fatwa) on a question of Islamic law.
المفتى (al-mufti): The person who gives a fatwa; the deputy of Allah in conveying His rulings.
فرض الكفاية (fard kifaya): A communal obligation; if some members fulfill it, the rest are absolved of sin.
الورع (al-wara'): Scrupulous piety; refraining from doubtful matters out of fear of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what phrase describes the mufti's relationship to Allah?
A) وارث الأنبياء (inheritor of the prophets)
B) موقع عن الله تعالى (deputy of Allah)
C) قائم بفرض الكفاية (fulfiller of a communal obligation)
D) معرض للخطأ (subject to error)

Key Takeaway
Fatwa is a grave responsibility; the mufti must embody deep knowledge and scrupulous piety, as exemplified by Ibn al-Salah's caution.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
intermediate
6 min
The Caution of the Salaf: Refraining from Fatwa
Collection of reports from the salaf illustrating their reluctance and fear in issuing fatwas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to cite at least three reports from the salaf about their reluctance to issue fatwas and explain the wisdom behind their caution using the hadith about fatwa without knowledge.

Core Idea
The salaf feared giving fatwa so much that they would refer questions to one another, and some declared that answering every question is a sign of insanity.

This lesson continues from the previous one by presenting numerous reports from the companions and tabi'in demonstrating their extreme caution in issuing fatwas. These narratives reinforce the principle that fatwa is a serious responsibility not to be taken lightly. The passage also cites a hadith warning that giving a fatwa without certain knowledge places the sin on the mufti.

Guided Path
1
Review the report of Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla
Read the narration that he encountered 120 companions of the Ansar who would pass a question from one to another, each referring it to someone else, until it returned to the first. This shows their reluctance to take responsibility for a fatwa.
Another version: they wished that another would suffice them the hadith and the fatwa.
2
Understand the statement of Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Abbas
They said: 'Whoever gives fatwa about everything he is asked is insane.' This strong language indicates that indiscriminate fatwa-issuing is a sign of foolishness, not knowledge.
This is a key warning against overconfidence.
3
Consider the report about Umar ibn al-Khattab
Al-Sha'bi, al-Hasan, and Abu Husayn said: 'One of you gives fatwa on a matter which, had it been presented to Umar, he would have gathered the people of Badr for it.' This shows the high standard of consultation and caution expected.
The people of Badr were the elite companions; Umar would consult them on difficult matters.
4
Reflect on the trembling of early scholars
Ata' ibn al-Sa'ib said: 'I encountered people; if one of them was asked about something, he would speak while trembling.' This physical reaction shows their fear of error.
This is the opposite of casual fatwa-giving.
5
Connect to the hadith of Abu Hurayra
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever gives a fatwa without firm knowledge, his sin is upon the one who consulted him.' This hadith directly justifies the caution of the salaf.
The mufti bears the sin if he speaks without certain knowledge.

۱۲
وكل منهم ذكر نفائس لم يذكرها الآخران، وقد طالعت كتب الثلاثة، و لخصت منها جملة مختصرة مستوعبة لكل ما ذكروه من المهم، وضممت إليها نفائس من متفرقات كلام الأصحاب، وبالله التوفيق.

Commentary
This passage, from Imam al-Nawawi's 'Adab al-Fatwa', opens by highlighting the immense gravity and merit of issuing fatwas. Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Abbas are quoted saying that whoever gives a fatwa on everything asked is 'insane.' These reports underscore the principle that fatwa is a serious responsibility, not to be undertaken lightly. The footnotes provide hadith sources, including a narration from Abu Hurayra that the sin of a fatwa given without certain knowledge falls upon the mufti. This passage thus instills in the student the proper adab: humility, fear of error, and the importance of referring to more knowledgeable scholars when in doubt.

Key Terms
الإفتاء (al-ifta'): The act of issuing a legal ruling (fatwa) on a question of Islamic law.
المفتى (al-mufti): The person who gives a fatwa; the deputy of Allah in conveying His rulings.
فرض الكفاية (fard kifaya): A communal obligation; if some members fulfill it, the rest are absolved of sin.
الورع (al-wara'): Scrupulous piety; refraining from doubtful matters out of fear of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Abbas say about a person who gives a fatwa on every question they are asked?
A) He is fulfilling a communal obligation.
B) He is following the sunnah.
C) He is insane.
D) He is an inheritor of the prophets.

Key Takeaway
The salaf's extreme reluctance to issue fatwas, along with the hadith on the sin of fatwa without knowledge, teaches that ifta' requires certainty and humility, not boldness.

The Virtue of Refraining from Fatwa and Saying 'I Don't Know'
Collection of narrations on caution in fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the adab of refraining from fatwa (tawaqquf) and apply the principle that saying 'la adri' is praiseworthy and sometimes obligatory in Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Core Idea
Fatwa is a grave responsibility; the mufti must prefer silence or 'I do not know' over hasty answers.

This lesson presents a series of narrations from the Salaf and later scholars illustrating their overwhelming caution and reluctance in issuing fatwas. These accounts ground the Hanafi principle that tawaqquf (refraining) is not a sign of ignorance but of piety and responsibility. By studying these examples, you will internalize the weight of fatwa and the virtue of saying 'la adri' when uncertain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contextual pattern
Read each narration and note the common attitude: scholars deferred questions, trembled, or said 'I do not know'. Recognize that this was a widespread practice among the most knowledgeable.
The pattern shows reluctance, not ignorance.
2
Extract the underlying principle
From the narrations, derive the principle that fatwa is a serious responsibility that requires certainty and taqwa. The mufti must weigh the consequences before answering.
E.g., Imam Malik advised imagining Paradise and Hellfire.
3
Apply to Hanafi fatwa methodology
Connect these adab to Hanafi rules: saying 'la adri' is praiseworthy and sometimes obligatory. The mufti must refrain unless he has clear evidence and confidence.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on caution.
4
Contrast with common misconception
Understand that saying 'I don't know' is not a sign of incompetence but of humility and religious devotion. The narrations show even the greatest scholars like Malik and Abu Hanifa hesitated.
The common misunderstanding is the opposite.

Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. We have narrated from the Salaf and the virtuous successors many well-known things about refraining from fatwa; we mention some of them for blessing. And we narrated from Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla, he said: I encountered one hundred and twenty Ansar from the Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him); one of them would be asked a question, and he would refer it to another, and that one to another, until it returned to the first. In another narration: 'None of them would narrate a hadith except that he wished his brother would suffice him from it, and he would not be asked about anything except that he wished his brother would suffice him the fatwa.' And from Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them): 'Whoever gives a fatwa about everything he is asked is insane.' And from al-Sha'bi, al-Hasan, and Abu Hushayn (with fatha on the ha), the Tabi'un, they said: 'One of you gives a fatwa on a matter which, if it had been presented to Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him), he would have gathered the people of Badr for it.' And from Ata ibn al-Sa'ib al-Tabi'i: I encountered people; one of them would be asked about something and he would speak while trembling. And from Ibn Abbas and Muhammad ibn Ajlan: 'If the scholar neglects [saying] “I do not know,” his vital parts are struck.' And from Sufyan ibn Uyaynah and Sahnun: 'The most daring of people in giving fatwa are the least knowledgeable.' And from al-Shafi'i – he was asked a question and did not answer, so it was said to him, he said: 'Until I know whether the virtue is in silence or in answering.' And from al-Athram: I heard Ahmad ibn Hanbal frequently saying 'I do not know,' and that was regarding matters for which he knew the various opinions. And from al-Haytham ibn Jamil: I witnessed Malik being asked forty-eight questions, and he said 'I do not know' regarding thirty-two of them. And from Malik also: He would sometimes be asked fifty questions and not answer even one of them. He used to say: 'Whoever answers a question should, before answering, present himself to Paradise and Hellfire, and [consider] how his deliverance will be, then answer.' And he was asked a question and said 'I do not know,' so it was said: 'It is a light, easy question,' so he became angry and said: 'There is nothing light in knowledge.' And al-Shafi'i said: 'I have not seen anyone in whom Allah Almighty has gathered the tools of fatwa as He has in Ibn Uyaynah, yet he was more silent regarding fatwa than anyone.' And Abu Hanifa said: 'Were it not for the fear of Allah Almighty that knowledge would be lost, I would not have given fatwa.'
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage, from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa, presents a series of narrations from the Salaf and later scholars illustrating the overwhelming caution and reluctance they exhibited when asked for legal rulings. The passage type is a collection of evidence for the adab of tawaqquf (refraining) in fatwa. The text opens with the author's statement that many incidents of refraining from fatwa are known from the predecessors, and he selects a few for blessing. He then narrates from Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla that among the Companions, they would defer questions to one another, each wishing his brother would suffice him from the responsibility. Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Abbas are reported to have said that one who gives fatwa on everything asked is insane, emphasizing that not every question merits an answer. The Tabi'un al-Sha'bi, al-Hasan, and Abu Hushayn point out that a mufti might give a fatwa on a matter that even Umar ibn al-Khattab would have consulted the Badr veterans about. Ata ibn al-Sa'ib describes earlier generations trembling when speaking about a fatwa. Ibn Abbas and Muhammad ibn Ajlan warn that if a scholar neglects saying 'I do not know,' his vital parts (i.e., his religion and credibility) are struck. Sufyan ibn Uyaynah and Sahnun state that the most daring in fatwa are the least knowledgeable. Imam al-Shafi'i hesitated to answer until he knew whether silence or speech was more virtuous. Ahmad ibn Hanbal frequently said 'I do not know' even when he knew the differing opinions. Imam Malik was asked 48 questions and answered 'I do not know' to 32 of them; he also advised that a mufti should imagine himself before Paradise and Hell before answering. When told a question was light, he retorted that nothing in knowledge is light. Al-Shafi'i praised Ibn Uyaynah's knowledge but noted his silence. Finally, Abu Hanifa expressed that only fear of knowledge being lost compelled him to give fatwa. The overarching lesson is that fatwa is a grave responsibility, and the mufti must be extremely cautious, preferring silence or 'I do not know' over hasty answers.

Key Terms
لا أدرى (la adri): A phrase meaning 'I do not know,' used by scholars to indicate lack of knowledge or caution in answering.
التوقف عن الفتيا (al-tawaqqufu 'an al-fatya): Refraining from issuing a fatwa, often due to lack of knowledge or fear of error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narrations, what did Imam Malik do when someone called a question 'light' after he said 'I don't know'?
A) He agreed and answered immediately.
B) He became angry and said 'There is nothing light in knowledge.'
C) He said it was indeed light and gave the answer.
D) He referred the questioner to another scholar.

Key Takeaway
The mufti's default should be caution; saying 'I do not know' is a virtue that protects the religion and the mufti's accountability.

The Mufti's Reluctance: The Virtue of 'I Don't Know'
Quoting early scholars on reluctance to issue fatwas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proper attitude of a mufti towards fatwa and apply the principles of reluctance and the virtue of admitting ignorance.

Core Idea
Eagerness for fatwa brings failure; reluctance brings divine help.

The passage from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa presents a crucial lesson for muftis: the spiritual danger of hastening to issue fatwas and the virtue of admitting ignorance. Citing early scholars and prophetic hadith, it establishes that true success in fatwa comes from humility, reluctance, and reliance on Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the opening statement by al-Saymari and al-Khatib. What do they say about someone who is eager to give fatwas versus someone who dislikes it?
Focus on the contrast between 'success is little' and 'assistance from Allah is greater'.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
The authors adduce the hadith 'Do not seek leadership...'. How does this support the claim about fatwa?
Think of fatwa as a form of authority; the analogy is between seeking leadership and seeking fatwa.
3
Analyse the stories
Read the anecdotes of al-Qasim ibn Muhammad and Abu al-Darda'. What lesson do they teach about saying 'I don't know'?
Note the humility and fear of Allah in their responses.
4
Synthesize the adab
Combine the teachings: what is the ideal attitude of a mufti when asked a question he does not know?
The mufti should not hesitate to say 'I don't know' and should avoid playing the role of a quack.

Commentary on the Etiquettes of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. 4- Al-Saymari and al-Khatib said: 'Rare is the one who is eager for fatwa, hastens to it, and persists in it, except that his success is little and he becomes confounded in his affairs. And if he dislikes that, does not prefer it as long as he finds an alternative, and refers the matter to others, then Allah's assistance for him is greater, and correctness in his answer is more frequent.' They adduced the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in the authentic hadith: 'Do not seek leadership, for if you are given it by asking, you will be entrusted to it; if you are given it without asking, you will be helped in it.' And you are the jurist of the people of Iraq? He said: But the angels were not ashamed when they said: 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.' Some scholars said: Learn 'I don't know', for if you say 'I don't know', they will teach you until you know; and if you say 'I know', they will ask you until you don't know. Al-Qasim ibn Muhammad was asked about something and said: 'I am not good at it.' The questioner said: 'I came to you not knowing anyone else.' Al-Qasim said: 'Do not look at the length of my beard and the many people around me; by Allah, I am not good at it!' An old man from Quraysh sitting next to him said: 'O nephew, stick to it, for by Allah, we have not seen you in a gathering more noble than today.' Al-Qasim said: 'By Allah, having my tongue cut off is dearer to me than speaking about what I have no knowledge of.' Sulayman wrote to Abu al-Darda' (may Allah be pleased with them both), and there was brotherhood between them: 'It has reached me that you have sat as a physician. Beware of being a quack, or you might kill a Muslim.' So sometimes two litigants would come to him, he would judge between them, then say: 'Take them back to the quack! By Allah, repeat your case.'
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa presents the virtue of reluctance in giving fatwas. Al-Saymari and al-Khatib emphasize that eagerness for fatwa leads to lack of success, while reluctance brings divine assistance. They support this with the hadith about not seeking leadership, analogizing fatwa to leadership. The text then narrates stories of al-Qasim ibn Muhammad and Abu al-Darda' to illustrate the importance of saying 'I don't know' and avoiding playing the role of a physician (mufti) without qualification. The footnotes clarify linguistic points and provide the hadith's source in Bukhari.

Key Terms
الفتيا (al-fatya): Issuing legal verdicts or fatwas
لا أدرى (la adri): The phrase 'I don't know' used to indicate lack of knowledge
متطبب (mutatabbib): One who acts as a physician without qualification; quack

The Ruler's Duty to Supervise Muftis
Ruler's duty to supervise muftis' qualifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruler's duty to proactively examine muftis' qualifications and apply the method of consulting reliable scholars to determine suitability.

Core Idea
The ruler must actively scrutinize muftis, confirming the qualified and preventing the unqualified.

This passage establishes a fundamental principle of fatwa governance: the ruler (imam) bears the responsibility of supervising muftis. Al-Khatib outlines a proactive duty to examine conditions, not merely wait for complaints. The method involves consulting trustworthy scholars. The passage reinforces this with Imam Malik's personal strictness and later scholars' warnings that unqualified muftis are like blind guides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
Read the opening phrase 'ينبغي للإمام أن يتصفح أحوال المفتين'. This is an imperative duty for the ruler regarding muftis.
The word 'ينبغي' indicates strong recommendation or duty in this context.
2
Understand the Duty
The text specifies two actions: confirm the suitable, and prevent the unsuitable with threats of punishment. Note that this is proactive, not reactive.
The ruler must actively investigate, not wait for complaints.
3
Recognize the Method
The Imam determines suitability by asking the scholars of his time and relying on reports of trustworthy people. This shows delegation to experts.
The method is indirect: consult others rather than self-assessment.
4
Evaluate the Evidence
The passage cites a hadith against appointing someone less qualified when a better person exists, and quotes Imam Malik's personal example. This strengthens the rule.
The hadith is used to support the punishment aspect.

فصل
ينبغي للإمام أن يتصفح أحوال المفتين
ه - قال الخطيب : ينبغى للإمام (١) أن يتصفّح أحوال المفتين، فمن صلح للفتيا أقره (۳)، ومن لا يصلح منعه ونهاه أن يعود، وتواعده بالعقوبة إن عاد . وطريق الإمام إلى معرفة من يصلح للفتوى أن يسأل علماء وقته، ويعتمد أخبار الموثوق بهم . ٦- ثم روى بإسناده عن مالك رحمه الله قال : ما أفتيت حتى شهد لي سبعون أنى أهل لذلك ((۳) وفي رواية: "ما أفتيت حتى سألت من هو أعلم مني : هل يراني موضعاً لذلك؟" .
(۱) قوله: "ينبغي للإمام ... " ومثله في البحر (٢٦٣/٦) عن شرح الروض . (سعيد أحمد)
وقال ابن القيم : من أفتى الناس وليس بأهل للفتوى فهو أثم عاص، ومن أقره من ولاة الأمور على ذلك فهو آثم أيضا . قال أبو الفرج ابن الجوزی رحمه الله : ويلزم ولى الأمر منعهم كما فعل بنو أمية، وهؤلاء بمنزلة من يدل الركب وليس له علم بالطريق وبمنزلة الأعمى الذى يرشد الناس إلى القبلة، وبمنزلة من لا معرفة له بالطب وهو يطب الناس، بل هو أسوأ حالا من هؤلاء كلهم ، وإذا تعين على ولى الأمر منع من لم يحسن التطبب من مداواة المرض، فكيف بمن لم يعرف الكتاب والسنة ولم يتفقه في الدين؟ (الإعلام : ١٨٣/٤)
:
(۲) قوله : "أقرّة" لقوله عليه السلام من قلد إنسانًا عملا وفي رعيته من هو أولى منه، فقد خان الله ورسوله وجماعة المسلمين». أخرجه الحاكم في المستدرك، وابن

Chapter: The Imam should examine the conditions of muftis. H - Al-Khatib said: It is befitting for the Imam to examine the conditions of the muftis. Whoever is suitable for giving fatwa, he confirms him; whoever is not suitable, he prevents him, forbids him from returning, and threatens him with punishment if he returns. The Imam's way to know who is suitable for fatwa is to ask the scholars of his time and rely on the reports of trustworthy people. 6- Then he narrated with his chain from Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'I did not give fatwa until seventy witnesses testified that I was qualified for that.' In another narration: 'I did not give fatwa until I asked someone more knowledgeable than me: Do you consider me a place for that?'
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This section establishes the ruler's duty to oversee the qualifications of muftis. Al-Khatib instructs the Imam to investigate and only allow qualified muftis, preventing and punishing unqualified ones. The method to determine qualification is consulting reliable scholars. The passage then quotes Imam Malik's personal example: he did not issue fatwas until he received collective attestation from seventy scholars, or until he consulted a superior. The footnotes add that appointing someone less qualified when a better person exists is a betrayal of Allah and the Muslims, citing a hadith. Ibn al-Qayyim and Ibn al-Jawzi compare unqualified muftis to blind guides and quack doctors, emphasizing that the ruler must prevent them just as he prevents unqualified physicians. This aligns with Hanafi practice of ensuring muftis are mujtahids or at least qualified for taqlid.

Key Terms
الإمام (al-imam): The ruler or leader of the Muslims
يتصفح (yataṣaffaḥ): To examine or scrutinize

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Khatib, how should the Imam identify who is suitable for giving fatwa?
A) By testing them himself.
B) By asking the scholars of his time and relying on trustworthy people.
C) By waiting for recommendations from the public.
D) By looking at their age and experience.

Key Takeaway
The ruler is responsible for ensuring only qualified muftis issue fatwas, and must actively supervise their conditions through consultation with reliable scholars.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
beginner
6 min
The Imam's Duty to Supervise Muftis
Explaining the ruler's obligation to vet muftis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the imam's responsibility in regulating fatwa issuance and explain the method of vetting muftis according to the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
The imam must actively vet muftis; only those declared fit by trustworthy scholars may give fatwa.

This passage from the Rasm al-Mufti literature addresses the public authority's role in safeguarding the integrity of fatwa. It establishes that the imam, as the political leader, is responsible for supervising muftis, ensuring that only qualified individuals issue religious verdicts. The text provides a practical mechanism: consulting the scholars of the time and relying on trustworthy reports. The example of Imam Malik demonstrates the immense caution required before one deems oneself fit for this serious task.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agent and the obligation
The imam is required to 'examine the conditions of the muftis'. This is a communal obligation (fard kifayah) on the ruler.
The word 'ينبغي' carries a strong recommendation, almost obligation, in this context.
2
Distinguish the two categories of mufti
Those who are 'suitable' (salah) and those who are not. The imam confirms the former and prevents the latter, threatening punishment if they continue.
This shows a clear regulatory mechanism.
3
Understand the method of verification
The imam does not rely on his own knowledge but consults the 'scholars of his time' and accepts the reports of trustworthy individuals.
This emphasizes communal validation over individual judgment.
4
Appreciate the cautionary example
Imam Malik's insistence on seventy witnesses and approval of a more learned scholar illustrates the high bar for fatwa.
Even the greatest scholars did not self-certify.
5
Apply the principle
In contemporary contexts, this translates to institutional oversight bodies that certify muftis, such as state mufti offices or scholarly councils.
The principle of external validation remains central.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل ينبغي للإمام أن يتصفح أحوال المفتين

ه - قال الخطيب: ينبغي للإمام أن يتصفح أحوال المفتين، فمن صلح للفتيا أقره، ومن لا يصلح منعه ونهاه أن يعود، وتواعده بالعقوبة إن عاد. وطريق الإمام إلى معرفة من يصلح للفتوى أن يسأل علماء وقته، ويعتمد أخبار الموثوق بهم. ٦- ثم روى بإسناده عن مالك رحمه الله قال: ما أفتيت حتى شهد لي سبعون أني أهل لذلك. وفي رواية: "ما أفتيت حتى سألت من هو أعلم مني: هل يراني موضعاً لذلك؟"
Chapter: It is befitting for the imam to examine the conditions of the muftis. Al-Khatib said: It is befitting for the imam to examine the conditions of the muftis, so whoever is suitable for fatwa he confirms him, and whoever is not suitable he prevents him and forbids him from returning, and threatens him with punishment if he returns. The way for the imam to know who is suitable for fatwa is that he asks the scholars of his time and relies on the reports of those who are trustworthy. Then he narrated with his chain from Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, who said: I did not give fatwa until seventy people testified that I am qualified for that. In another narration: "I did not give fatwa until I asked someone more knowledgeable than me: 'Do you see me as a place suitable for that?'"
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage addresses the public authority's (the imam's) responsibility in regulating the issuance of fatwas. The imam must actively supervise the muftis in his domain, confirming those who are qualified and preventing unqualified individuals from issuing religious verdicts. The standard for qualification is determined by consulting reputable scholars and relying on trustworthy witnesses. The example of Imam Malik is cited to illustrate the extreme caution required: even a scholar of his stature sought seventy witnesses and the approval of those more knowledgeable before deeming himself fit to give fatwa. This emphasizes that giving fatwa is a serious responsibility and not a matter of personal ambition.

Key Terms
المفتي (al-mufti): The one who issues fatwas, a qualified jurist.
الإمام (al-imam): Here, the ruler or political authority responsible for appointing and supervising muftis.
الصلاح للفتوى (al-salah lil-fatwa): Suitability for fatwa, i.e., possessing the necessary knowledge and piety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how should the imam determine who is qualified to give fatwa?
A) By personal knowledge
B) By asking trustworthy scholars
C) By public popularity
D) By written exam

Key Takeaway
The imam bears the duty of supervising muftis through consultation with scholars, ensuring only qualified individuals issue fatwas.

The Qualities of a Mufti: Piety and Caution
Describing the required piety and caution for a mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three essential qualities of a mufti (wara', diyana, siyana) and explain the principle of self-imposed stricter standards using the example of Imam Malik.

Core Idea
A mufti must be outwardly pious and manifest caution, imposing upon himself a higher standard than he obliges others.

The role of a mufti is not merely to issue rulings but to embody the religion. This passage from Rasm al-Mufti outlines the personal qualities required: piety, religiosity, and a heightened caution (siyana). It illustrates a profound principle through the practice of Imam Malik, who held himself to a stricter standard than the general public.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three required qualities
From the Arabic text, extract the three key terms: al-wara', al-diyana, al-siyana. These are the core traits a mufti must cultivate.
These are not merely recommended but considered essential for the mufti's role.
2
Understand the meaning of each quality
Define each term in the context of a mufti: wara' (piety with abstinence from doubtful matters), diyana (religiosity and adherence to obligations), siyana (caution and care to preserve religious integrity).
Wara' goes beyond basic taqwa; it involves avoiding permissible things that may lead to sin.
3
Analyse the principle of self-imposed stricter standards
Examine the statement from Imam Malik: he would do in his private affairs what he did not oblige others to do, and would not sin if he omitted it. This illustrates that a scholar's personal practice should be more careful than what he requires of the public.
This principle guards against the mufti becoming lax or forcing his personal caution on others.
4
Connect to the mufti's role
Explain why this principle is crucial: the mufti is a role model. His manifest piety builds trust and authority. By being more cautious, he sets a positive example without making the religion burdensome.
The mufti's caution (siyana) protects his own religious standing and the community's perception.
5
Apply to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a mufti who personally avoids a borderline halal food due to doubt, but issues a fatwa permitting it for others. This perfectly aligns with the principle: he practices wara' for himself but does not oblige it.
The mufti's personal choice does not change the legal ruling for others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان ما ينبغي للمفتي

قالوا: وينبغي أن يكون المفتى ظاهر الورع، مشهوراً بالديانة الظاهرة، والصيانة الباهرة.

وكان مالك رحمه الله يعمل بما لا يلزمه الناس، ويقول: "لا يكون عالماً حتى يعمل في خاصة نفسه بما لا يلزمه الناس، مما لو تركه لم يأثم". وكان يحكي نحوه عن شيخه ربيعة.
Chapter: Explaining what is incumbent upon the mufti. They said: It is incumbent that the mufti be outwardly pious, famous for manifest religiosity, and outstanding caution. And Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, used to do what he did not oblige people to do, and he said: 'A person is not a scholar until he acts in his private matters with what he does not oblige people to do, something which if he omitted he would not sin.' And he used to relate something similar from his teacher Rabi'a.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage describes the personal qualities a mufti must cultivate. He should be known for taqwa (piety), din (religiosity), and a heightened sense of caution (siana). The key practical principle, taken from Imam Malik and his teacher Rabi'a, is that a true scholar imposes upon himself a stricter standard than he imposes upon the general public. He may voluntarily do things (such as avoiding certain permissible things) as an expression of caution, even though he does not oblige others to do the same. This is because the mufti's example guides the community, and his piety must be manifest.

Key Terms
الورع (al-wara'): Piety and abstinence, especially from doubtful matters.
الصيانة (al-siyana): Caution and care, refraining from anything that could compromise one's religious integrity.
الديانة (al-diyana): Religiosity, adherence to religious obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the practice of Imam Malik regarding obligations upon himself versus others?
A) He obliged others to follow his personal practice
B) He was stricter on himself than on others
C) He was lenient on himself
D) He made no distinction

Key Takeaway
A mufti must embody piety and caution, voluntarily adopting a stricter personal standard to safeguard his religious authority without imposing it on others.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

Conditions of the Mufti: Uprightness, Alertness, and Gender
Conditions and desirable qualities of the mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of a Hanafi mufti and apply the Hanafi position on the permissibility of a woman issuing fatwas.

Core Idea
A mufti must be upright and alert; a woman's fatwa is valid like a narrator's, not a witness's.

This passage from al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa, quoted in the commentary on Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, outlines the ethical and juristic requirements for a mufti. It details nine conditions, and the commentaries expand on the importance of alertness in later times and the permissibility of women serving as muftis. Understanding these conditions is essential for recognising who is qualified to issue fatwas in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
List the nine conditions
From the Arabic text, identify the nine conditions mentioned for the mufti: being mukallaf, Muslim, thiqah, ma'mum, free from fisq and khawarim al-muru'a, faqih al-nafs, salim al-dhihn, rasin al-fikr, sahih al-tasarruf wa al-istinbat, and mutayaqqiz.
The footnotes elaborate on some conditions, especially 'alertness'.
2
Distinguish antara 'adalah and 'mutayaqqiz'
Understand that 'adalah (uprightness) is a core condition, but later scholars like Ibn 'Abidin argued that 'alertness' (mutayaqqiz) is also necessary in times of widespread deception.
Al-Bahr considered alertness redundant given 'adalah, but Ibn 'Abidin insists it is indispensable.
3
Analyse the dispute on the fasiq mufti
The text discusses whether an immoral person (fasiq) can be a mufti. Most Hanafis reject it, citing that the fasiq's testimony is not accepted in religious matters. However, some (e.g. al-Kanz) allowed it if he strives to avoid error.
Al-Tahawi criticized this reasoning, noting that in his time some deliberately distort texts.
4
Apply the Hanafi view on female mufti
The text explicitly states it is the same for free, slave, or woman. The footnote clarifies she is like a narrator (rawi), not a witness or judge. Thus, her fatwa is valid.
This distinguishes the mufti's role from that of a qadi, where Hanafis restrict women's judgeship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آداب الفتوى للإمام النووى
قال مالك : " ولا ينبغى لرجل أن يرى نفسه أهلا لشيء، حتى يسأل من هو أعلم منه " .
فصل
فى بيان ما ينبغي للمفتى
قالوا : وينبغي أن يكون المفتى ظاهر الورع، مشهوراً بالديانة الظاهرة، والصيانة الباهرة ) .
- وكان مالك رحمه الله يعمل بما لا يُلزمه الناس، ويقول : "لا يكون عالماً حتى يعمل في خاصة نفسه بما لا يلزمه الناس ، مما لو تركه لم يأثم " . وكان يحكي نحوه عن شيخه ربيعة .
فصل
فى شروط المفتى
-۹ شرط المفتى كونه مكلفا ، مسلما ، ثقة، مأموما، متنزها عن أسباب الفسق وخوارم المروءة، فقيه النفس، سليم الذهن، رصين الفكر صحيح التصرف والاستنباط ، متيقظا ) ، سواء فيه الحر والعبد، والمرأة،
(۱) قوله : " والصيانة الباهرة " فهذه صفات ثلاثة تتعلق الأولى بالتخلية عن الرذائل، والثانية بالتحلى بالفضائل، والثالثة بالاحتياط في الفتيا. وإن شئت قلت : حاصل الأولى التجنب عن ما لا يعنيه، والثانية الاشتغال بما ينبغى له، والثالثة الجزم والتيقظ .
(۲) قوله : مكلفاً قال ابن نجيم: "فشرط المفتى إسلامه وعدالته ، ولزم منها اشتراط بلوغه وعقله، فترد فتوى الفاسق والكافر، وغير المكلف ؛ إذ لا يقبل خبرهم " . وفى الدر المختار": "والفاسق لا يصلح مفتيًا؛ لأن الفتوى من أمور الدين والفاسق لا يقبل قوله في الديانات" . زاد العينى واختاره كثير من المتأخرين، وجزم به صاحب المجمع في متنه، وله في شرحه عبارات بليغة، وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة أيضاً.
وظاهر ما في "التحرير" : أنه لا يحلّ استفتاؤه اتفاقا كما بسطه المصنف ، وقيل : نعم . يصلح، وبه جزم فى "الكنز" ؛ لأنه يجتهد حذار نسبة الخطأ، ولا خلاف في اشتراط إسلامه، وعقله.
قال الطحاوي: "هذا التعليل لا يظهر في زماننا، وأنه قد يعرض عن النص الضروري قصداً؛ لغرض فاسد، وربما عورض بالنص فيدعى فساد النص" . (سعيد أحمد)
(۱) قوله : "متيقظاً" وفي "البحر" : " ولا حاجة إلى اشتراط التيقظ وقوة الضبط، كما في الروض " للاحتراز عمن غلب عليه الغفلة والسهو؛ لأن اشتراط العدالة يغني عنهما . اهـ .
وقال السيد ابن عابدين : "وهذا شرط لازم في زماننا ؛ فإنّ العادة اليوم أن من صار بیده فتوى المفتى استطال على خصمه وقهره بمجرد قوله : أفتاني المفتى بأن الحق معي والخصم جاهل لا يدرى ما في الفتوى فلا بد أن يكون المفتى متيقظا يعلم حيل الناس ودسائسهم، فإذا جاءه السائل يقرره من لسانه، ولا يقول له : إن كان كذا فالحق معك، وإن كان كذا فالحق مع خصمك ؛ لأنه يختار لنفسه ما ينفعه ولا يعجز عن إثباته بشاهدى زور بل الأحسن أن يجمع بينه وبين خصمه، فإذا ظهر له الحق مع أحدهما ، كتب الفتوى لصاحب الحق. وليحترز من الوكلاء في الخصومات ؛ فإن أحدهم لا يرضى إلا بإثبات دعواه لموكله بأي وجه أمكن، ولهم مهارة في الحيل، والتزوير، وقلب الكلام، وتصوير الباطل بصورة الحق . فإذا أخذ الفتوى قهر خصمه، ووصل إلى غرضه الفاسد، فلا يحل للمفتى أن يعينه على ضلاله وقد قالوا من جهل بأهل زمانه، فهو جاهل " . وقد يسأل عن أمر شرعي، وتدل القرائن للمفتى المتيقظ أن مراده التوصل به إلى غرض فاسد، كما شهدناه كثيراً . والحاصل أن غفلة المفتى يلزم منها ضرر عظيم في هذا الزمان والله تعالى المستعان . (رد المحتار، کتاب القضاء : ٣٥٩/٥ ، كراتشي) (سعيد أحمد)
(۲) قوله : " سواء فيه الحر والعبد" فهو كالراوي لا كالشاهد والقاضي، ولذا تصح فتواه
Manners of issuing fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. Malik said: 'A man should not consider himself fit for anything until he asks one more learned than him.' Section: Explanation of what is desirable for the mufti. They said: The mufti should be outwardly pious, well-known for manifest religiosity and brilliant integrity. And Malik used to act in ways he did not oblige people, saying: 'One is not a scholar until he acts privately in ways he does not oblige people, such that if he omitted it he would not be sinful.' He related similar from his teacher Rabi'a. Section: Conditions of the mufti. The condition of the mufti is that he be accountable, Muslim, trustworthy, reliable, free from causes of sin and breaches of manliness, a jurist in intellect, sound of mind, firm in thought, correct in action and deduction, alert. It is the same whether free or slave, or woman. Footnote (1): 'And brilliant integrity' – these are three qualities: the first relates to abandoning vices, the second to adorning with virtues, the third to caution in issuing fatwa. If you wish, the gist of the first is avoiding what does not concern him, the second is engaging in what is proper for him, the third is decisiveness and alertness. Footnote (2): 'Accountable' – Ibn Nujaym said: 'The condition of the mufti is his Islam and uprightness, and from that follows the requirement of his maturity and sanity. So the fatwa of the immoral, disbeliever, and non-accountable is rejected, because their news is not accepted.' In al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'The immoral person is not fit to be a mufti, because the fatwa is among religious matters and the immoral person's statement in religious matters is not accepted.' Al-‘Ayni added and many of the later scholars chose it, and the author of al-Majma‘ affirmed it in his matn, and he has eloquent expressions in his commentary, and it is also the saying of the three imams. And what appears from al-Tahrir is that it is unanimously not permissible to seek his fatwa, as the author explained. And it was said: Yes, he is fit, and al-Kanz affirmed it, because he strives to avoid attributing error. And there is no disagreement on the requirement of his Islam and sanity. Al-Tahawi said: 'This reasoning does not appear in our time, because he may intentionally turn away from the necessary text for an evil purpose, and perhaps he may contradict the text by claiming the text is corrupt.' (Sa‘id Ahmad) Footnote (1): 'Alert' – In al-Bahr: 'There is no need to require alertness and strength of retention, as in al-Rawd, to avoid one who is overcome by heedlessness and forgetfulness, because the requirement of uprightness suffices from both.' End. Sayyid Ibn ‘Abidin said: 'This condition is necessary in our time, because today it is customary that whoever obtains the fatwa of the mufti becomes arrogant over his opponent and overcomes him merely by saying: The mufti gave me a fatwa that the truth is with me, while the opponent is ignorant and does not know what is in the fatwa. So the mufti must be alert, knowing people's tricks and intrigues. When the questioner comes, he should interrogate him from his own words, and not tell him: If it is such, the truth is with you, and if it is such, the truth is with your opponent, because he chooses for himself what benefits him and is not incapable of proving it with false witnesses. Rather, it is better to bring together him and his opponent, and when the truth appears with one of them, he writes the fatwa for the rightful person. He should beware of lawyers in disputes, because one of them is not satisfied except with proving his client's claim by any means possible, and they have skill in tricks, forgery, twisting words, and portraying falsehood as truth. So if he takes the fatwa, he overcomes his opponent and attains his evil aim. So it is not permissible for the mufti to help him in his misguidance. And they have said: Whoever is ignorant of the people of his time is ignorant.' And he may be asked about a legal matter, and the alert mufti finds indications that his aim is to achieve an evil end through it, as we have witnessed often. The conclusion is that the negligence of the mufti entails great harm in this time, and Allah is the Helper. (Radd al-Muhtar, Kitab al-Qada, 5/359, Karachi) (Sa‘id Ahmad) Footnote (2): 'It is the same whether free or slave' – He is like the narrator, not like the witness or judge, and therefore his fatwa is valid.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage from al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa, quoted in the commentary on Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, outlines the ethical and juristic requirements for a mufti. It begins with a reminder from Imam Malik that one should not deem himself fit without consulting a superior, emphasizing humility. The first section discusses desirable qualities: outward piety, religiosity, and integrity. It notes that Imam Malik practiced optional acts of virtue to illustrate that a scholar's private conduct should exceed public obligations. The second section lists nine conditions: being legally accountable (mukallaf), Muslim, trustworthy (thiqah), reliable (ma'mum), free from sin and breaches of manliness, a jurist at heart, sound-minded, firm in thought, correct in deduction, and alert (mutayaqqiz). These conditions ensure the mufti's fatwa is authoritative. The footnotes clarify that 'brilliant integrity' comprises three aspects: avoiding vices, cultivating virtues, and caution in issuing fatwas. On 'accountable,' Ibn Nujaym and al-Durr al-Mukhtar state that an immoral person (fasiq) or disbeliever cannot be a mufti because their testimony is unacceptable in religious matters. Al-‘Ayni and later scholars upheld this, though some (e.g., al-Kanz) argued that even a fasiq may strive to avoid error, but consensus requires Islam and sanity. Al-Tahawi critiques this reasoning, noting that in his time, some deliberately distort texts. The most extensive commentary is on 'alertness' (mutayaqqiz). While al-Bahr considered it redundant given the condition of uprightness, Sayyid Ibn ‘Abidin argues it is essential in later times because cunning litigants exploit fatwas to oppress opponents. The mufti must be aware of tricks, avoid giving rulings that favor one side without hearing both, and guard against unscrupulous lawyers who manipulate facts. Failing to be alert leads to great harm. Finally, the text states that a slave or woman may serve as mufti, likening them to narrators of hadith rather than witnesses or judges, so their fatwa is valid.

Key Terms
مفتى (muftī): The person who issues fatwas (sharia rulings) to questioners
مكلف (mukallaf): Legally responsible, i.e., adult, sane, and Muslim
عدالة (ʿadālah): Uprightness, moral integrity that prevents one from committing major sins and persisting on minor ones
متيقظ (mutayaqqiz): Alert, perceptive, aware of deceptive practices and legal tricks
فسق (fisq): Sinfulness, flagrant disobedience that compromises one's reliability

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of a woman's fatwa?
A) It is invalid because women cannot be witnesses.
B) It is valid because she is like a narrator of hadith.
C) It is valid only if she is also a judge.
D) It is invalid because women cannot be muftis.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms that a woman may serve as mufti, distinguishing her role from that of a witness or judge, and requires the mufti to be alert to deception in addition to being upright.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

Conditions of the Mufti: Alertness and Sensory Capacities
Expansion on mufti conditions: alertness and sensory capacities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sensory and attentiveness conditions for a mufti according to the Hanafi school and evaluate the debate on whether alertness is a separate requirement.

Core Idea
A mufti must be alert to prevent fatwa abuse; sensory impairments do not disqualify him if he can communicate or hear sufficiently.

The qualifications of a mufti go beyond basic uprightness. This passage from the commentary on Adab al-Fatwa discusses whether a mufti needs to be 'mutayyaqiz' (alert) in addition to being 'adil' and also addresses the validity of fatwas given by blind, mute, and hard-of-hearing persons. These issues are crucial for ensuring fatwas are reliable and not misused.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attributes listed
Read the beginning of the passage: the mufti must be free from fisq, possess sound mind, firm thought, correct deduction, and be alert.
These are conditions; note that freedom, slavery, and gender are irrelevant.
2
Analyze the alertness debate
Compare the view of the author of al-Bahr (alertness not needed because 'adalah suffices) with Ibn Abidin's rebuttal: in later times, alertness is necessary to counter litigants' tricks and lawyers' manipulation.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; Ibn Abidin's view is preferred for public fatwa.
3
Examine fatwa validity for the blind and mute
Note that the three imams (including Abu Hanifa) allow fatwa from the blind. The mute's fatwa is valid if he writes or his gesture is understood, but he cannot be a judge.
The distinction is based on the nature of fatwa (communication of ruling) vs. judgeship (requires oral formula).
4
Understand the hearing condition
Distinguish between al-atrash (hears loud sounds) and al-asamm (completely deaf). The atrash's fatwa is valid; the asamm's is not, unless he can read and write. A general mufti must have good hearing due to the nature of his public work.
Even if written communication is possible, practical need for hearing in public fatwa duties outweighs.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a mufti who is hard of hearing but can read and write. Determine if he can be a general mufti. Based on the text, he should not, because he may miss critical parts of oral cases, leading to injustice.
This application uses the reasoning from Radd al-Muhtar.

Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. Causes of fisq and acts contrary to muru'a, a jurist by nature, sound of mind, firm of thought, correct in action and deduction, alert. The free and the slave are equal in this, and the woman. In his commentary, eloquent expressions, and it is also the saying of the three Imams. And the apparent in al-Tahrir: that it is not permissible to seek his fatwa by agreement, as the author expanded upon, and it is said: yes, he is suitable, and that is asserted in al-Kanz; because he strives to avoid attributing error, and there is no dispute in requiring his Islam and his intellect. Al-Tahawi said: 'This reasoning is not apparent in our time, and that he may deliberately turn away from the necessary text for a corrupt purpose, and perhaps the text is objected to and he claims the text is defective.' (Sa'id Ahmad) (1) His statement: 'alert' and in al-Bahr: 'There is no need to stipulate alertness and strength of memory, as in al-Rawd, to exclude one overcome by heedlessness and forgetfulness, because the stipulation of uprightness suffices for them... End.' And Sayyid Ibn Abidin said: 'This is a necessary condition in our time, because the custom today is that when someone obtains the fatwa of the mufti, he dominates his opponent and overpowers him simply by saying: The mufti gave me a fatwa that the truth is with me, and the opponent is ignorant and does not know what is in the fatwa. So the mufti must be alert, knowing people's tricks and intrigues. When the questioner comes to him, he should interrogate him from his tongue, and not say to him: If it is such, then the truth is with you, and if it is such, then the truth is with your opponent, because he chooses for himself what benefits him and is not unable to prove it with false witnesses. Rather, the better is to bring together him and his opponent, and when the truth appears to him with one of them, he writes the fatwa for the one with truth. And let him beware of agents in lawsuits, because one of them is not satisfied except with proving his claim for his client in any possible way, and they have skill in tricks, forgery, twisting words, and portraying falsehood as truth. So when he takes the fatwa, he overwhelms his opponent and achieves his corrupt purpose. So it is not permissible for the mufti to help him in his error. And they have said: Whoever is ignorant of his contemporary people is ignorant.' And he may be asked about a legal matter, and the indications for the alert mufti show that his intention is to achieve a corrupt purpose, as we have witnessed many times. The conclusion: that heedlessness of the mufti necessarily results in great harm in this time, and Allah is the one sought for help. (Radd al-Muhtar, Kitab al-Qada, 5/359, Karachi) (Sa'id Ahmad) (2) His statement: 'The free and the slave are equal in it' so he is like the narrator, not like the witness and judge, and therefore his fatwa is valid. Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. And the blind, and the mute if he writes or his gesture is understood. 10 - Shaykh Abu Amr ibn al-Salah said: He should be like the narrator for one whose testimony is not accepted. (Radd al-Muhtar: 468) (Sa'id Ahmad) (1) His statement: 'and the mute.' In Talqih al-Mahbubi: The gesture of a speaking mufti is acted upon, so it is not exclusive to the mute. And in al-Qunya, referring to 'Ayn al-A'imma al-Makki: The gesture of the mufti with his head in place of his saying 'yes', and the mustafti may act upon it. And he referred to al-Nawazil from Abu al-Qasim similarly, and he referred to Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani: 'No, because the gesture of a speaking person is not considered.' (al-Bahr, 6/263) And in al-Durr: His freedom, masculinity, and speech are not conditions, so the fatwa of a mute is valid, not his judgeship. And his gesture suffices, but not from the judge, because a specific formula is necessary like 'I judged' and 'I obliged' after a valid claim. And as for al-atrash: he who hears loud sound, the correct is validity unlike the deaf, and the ruling should be the same for the mufti. If you say: There may be a difference between them because the mufti reads the question and writes his answer, so he does not need hearing. I say: The apparent from their speech is that this is not sufficient for the judge, even though he could have written to him the answer of the two litigants, so also for the mufti. And a distinction is possible: because judgment requires a specific formula after a valid claim, so caution is taken, unlike fatwa; because it is the communication of a legal ruling, even by gesture, so hearing is not required. (al-Manh, summarized) I say: There is no doubt that if it is written to him and he answers it, his fatwa may be acted upon. But if he is appointed for general fatwa and the general public come to him, including women, Bedouins, and others, then he must be of sound hearing, because not every questioner can write his question. And sometimes the two litigants come to him, and one of them speaks in a way that the truth is against him, not for him, and the mufti did not hear that from him, so he gives a fatwa based on what he heard from some of his speech, thus ruining the right of his opponent. And I have witnessed this many times, so there should be no hesitation that he is not fit to be a general mufti whose answer the judge awaits to rule by it, for the harm of such a person is greater than his benefit. And Allah knows best. (Radd al-Muhtar, 4/468) (Sa'id Ahmad)
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage, from a commentary on Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa, discusses the qualifications of a mufti. The author begins by listing the attributes of a mufti: he must be free from fisq and acts contrary to muru'a, be a jurist by nature, sound of mind, firm of thought, correct in action and deduction, and alert. It notes that these conditions apply equally to free men, slaves, and women, and that the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) similarly hold this view. The text then addresses a debate: the author of al-Tahrir states that it is not permissible to seek the fatwa of a mufti who lacks these qualities by consensus, while others (like the author of al-Kanz) argue that he is still suitable because he strives to avoid error. There is no dispute that Islam and intellect are required. 

Al-Tahawi critiques this reasoning, arguing that in his time, a mufti might deliberately ignore clear texts for corrupt purposes. Then a footnote from al-Bahr suggests that alertness is not an additional condition, as uprightness covers it. However, Sayyid Ibn Abidin strongly asserts that alertness is necessary in later times because people misuse fatwas to oppress opponents. He advises the mufti to question the litigant carefully, avoid giving one-sided verdicts, and beware of lawyers who twist facts. 

The passage continues with a discussion on who can be a mufti: a slave, free person, woman, blind person, and mute (if he writes or gestures understood) can give fatwa. Ibn al-Salah likens the mufti to a narrator, not a witness or judge. The text then elaborates on the mute's gesture, citing various opinions on whether a speaking mufti's gesture suffices. It rules that a mute's fatwa is valid but not his judgeship, as judgeship requires a specific oral formula. Concerning hearing, the text distinguishes between the hard of hearing (atrash) and the completely deaf (asamm): the former can give fatwa, but the latter cannot unless they can read and write. In general, a mufti appointed for the public must have good hearing because the public may not be able to write their questions, and missing part of the case can lead to injustice. The author concludes that such a hearing-impaired person should not be a general mufti whose fatwas are used by judges, as the harm outweighs benefit.

Key Terms
متيقظ (mutayyaqiz): Alert, attentive; a mufti who is aware of people's tricks and can discern true intent.
الأخرس (al-akhras): The mute person; his fatwa is valid if he writes or his gesture is understood.
الأطرش (al-atrash): The hard of hearing; one who can hear loud sounds, his fatwa is valid unlike the completely deaf.
الأعمى (al-a'ma): The blind person; his fatwa is valid according to the three imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Abidin, why is alertness (اليقظة) particularly necessary for a mufti in modern times?
A) Because it is a condition in all Islamic schools.
B) Because people are less pious now and cannot be trusted.
C) Because litigants and lawyers may misuse fatwas to oppress opponents, so the mufti must be aware of tricks.
D) Because the mufti must be more knowledgeable than earlier scholars.

Key Takeaway
A mufti's qualifications include both intellectual and sensory capacities; alertness is a debated condition for public fatwa, and sensory impairments do not automatically disqualify a mufti as long as he can communicate or hear sufficiently.

The Mufti as Adversary: When Fatwa is Rejected
Ruling on a mufti who becomes hostile to a person in his fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a mufti's fatwa is rejected due to enmity and apply the analogy with testimony.

Core Idea
If a mufti shows open enmity to a person, his fatwa against that person is rejected — just as a biased witness's testimony is rejected.

A mufti must be impartial. But what happens when he becomes hostile to a specific individual? This rule, from al-Hawi and endorsed by the Hanafi school, draws a sharp line: open enmity disqualifies his fatwa for that person, just as it would disqualify his testimony. This lesson unpacks the condition and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger
Look for the key term 'nābaza' — open opposition out of enmity. It is more than mere disagreement; it is public hostility akin to declaring war.
The footnote clarifies: 'nābaza' means opposing out of enmity.
2
State the consequence
Once enmity is established, the mufti becomes a legal opponent (khaṣman ḥukman). His fatwa against that person is rejected.
Rejection is limited to the person he opposes; his other fatwas remain valid.
3
Understand the analogy
The ruling is based on qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) with a biased witness. Just as a witness who bears enmity is disqualified, so too is the mufti.
The underlying illa is loss of impartiality.
4
Apply the rule
In practice, if a mustafti knows the mufti is hostile, he must seek another mufti. Relying on such a fatwa would be invalid for that person.
The rule does not extend to the mufti's general qualifications, only to this specific case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۱- قال : وذكر صاحب "الحاوى " : أن المفتى إذا نابذ (۳) في فتواه شخصاً معيناً، صار خصماً حكمًا (٤) معاندا، فترد فتواه على من عاداه كما ترد شهادته عليه . (۳) قوله : "نابذ " أى خالف عن عداوة ، يقال : نابذه الحرب أى جاهره به . (٤) قوله : " حكماً وفي نسخة : بإسقاط "حكما " . (سعيد أحمد)
11. He said: The author of al-Hawi mentioned: If the mufti opposes (nābaza) a specific person in his fatwa, he becomes an opponent legally, a stubborn adversary, so his fatwa against the one he opposes is rejected just as his testimony against him is rejected. (3) His saying: "nābaza" — i.e., he opposed out of enmity. It is said: nābadha al-harb, i.e., he declared war on him. (4) His saying: "legally" — and in a copy: without "legally". (Sa'id Ahmad)
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule about the mufti's conduct: when a mufti shows open hostility (nābaza) to a specific person in his fatwa, he becomes a legal opponent and adversary. Consequently, his fatwa against that person is not accepted, just as his testimony against him would not be accepted in court. The reason is that the mufti has demonstrated bias, so his fatwa loses its reliability. The footnote clarifies the meaning of nābaza: opposition out of enmity, as in declaring war. This rule is analogous to the disqualification of a witness who bears enmity. The Hanafi school considers that such a mufti's fatwa is rejected specifically against that person, but not generally.

Key Terms
نابذ (nābaza): To oppose out of enmity, to declare hostility; used for a mufti who shows bias against someone in his fatwa.
خصمًا حكمًا (khaṣman ḥukman): Legally an opponent; meaning the mufti is considered a formal adversary to the person he opposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A mufti who displays open enmity to a person becomes a legal opponent, and his fatwa against that person is rejected — analogous to a disqualified witness.

Mufti Is Like Narrator, Not Witness
Rasm rule: mufti's personal ties do not affect fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule that personal bias does not affect a fatwa and explain why the mufti is analogous to a narrator (rawi) rather than a witness (shahid).

Core Idea
The mufti is a narrator of divine law, not a witness; his fatwa is general and non-binding, so personal bias does not invalidate it.

This lesson examines a foundational rule of mufti conduct: a mufti may issue a fatwa for anyone, even a relative or enemy, because his role is like that of a hadith narrator transmitting a general ruling. Unlike a witness, whose testimony serves a specific party, the mufti's fatwa is an announcement of Allah's law, applicable to all similar cases. Understanding this distinction protects the mufti from undue restrictions and preserves the accessibility of fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
Read the first line: personal relations (kinship, enmity, benefit, harm) do not affect the mufti. This is the core rule.
2
Understand the Analogy
The text compares the mufti to a narrator (rawi) and contrasts him with a witness (shahid). Identify why each analogy fits or does not fit.
Narrators transmit general reports; witnesses testify for specific parties.
3
Contrast with Judge
Note that a judge's ruling is binding and specific, so bias matters. The mufti's fatwa is not binding, so the standard is different.
4
Recognize the Consequence
The mufti may give fatwa for someone for whom he could not testify or judge. This is the practical implication.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Consider: a mufti's business partner asks for a fatwa on a contract. Using this rule, the mufti can answer because his fatwa is a general ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه لا يؤثر فيه قرابة وعداوة، وجرّ نفع ، ودفع ضر؛ لأن المفتي في حكم مخبر عن الشرع بما لا اختصاص له بشخص، فكان كالراوى لا كالشاهد، وفتواه لا يرتبط بها إلزام ، بخلاف حكم القاضي . قوله : "لا يؤثر " كذا في "البحر" عن "شرح الروض" . قوله : فكان كالراوى لا كالشاهد فيجوز للمفتى أن يفتى من لا تقبل شهادته له وإن لم يجز أن يشهد أو يقضى له، والفرق بينهما أن الإفتاء يجرى مجرى الرواية فكأنه حكم عام بمنزلة إخبار العباد عما شرعه ربهم لهم، بخلاف الشهادة والحكم فإنه يخص المشهود له والمحكوم له فيتطرق التهمة إليهما . والحاصل أن الفتيا أوسع من الحكم والشهادة.
That kinship and enmity, attraction of benefit, and repulsion of harm do not affect him; because the mufti is in the position of one informing about the law with no particular attachment to a person, so he is like a narrator (rawi), not like a witness (shahid). And his fatwa is not associated with binding enforcement, unlike the ruling of a judge. His statement: 'does not affect' - thus in al-Bahr from Sharh al-Rawd. His statement: 'so he is like a narrator not like a witness' - so it is permissible for the mufti to give a fatwa for someone whose testimony is not admissible for him, although it is not permissible for him to testify or judge for him. The difference between them is that giving fatwa follows the course of narration (riwaya), as if it is a general ruling in the form of informing the servants about what their Lord has legislated for them, unlike testimony and judgment, which are specific to the one testified for and the one judged for, so suspicion creeps into them. The conclusion is that fatwa is broader than judgment and testimony.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a core rasm rule for muftis: a mufti's fatwa is not invalidated by personal ties or biases. The reasoning is that the mufti is like a transmitter of divine law, not a witness in a specific dispute. A witness gives testimony that directly benefits or harms a particular party, so bias matters. A mufti announces a general ruling that applies to all similar cases; his personal relationship with the questioner does not change the ruling. Thus, the mufti may give fatwa even for someone against whom he would not be allowed to testify. This principle distinguishes fatwa from qada (judgment), as a judge's ruling binds specific parties and requires impartiality.

Key Terms
المفتي (al-mufti): The one who issues fatwa, i.e., informs about Allah's ruling based on evidence.
الراوي (al-rawi): A narrator of hadith, whose report is accepted generally without regard to personal benefit.
الشاهد (al-shahid): A witness in court, whose testimony is specific to a party and thus subject to suspicion of bias.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a mufti's personal relationship with the questioner not invalidate his fatwa according to this text?
A) Because the mufti is not required to be impartial
B) Because the mufti is like a narrator, not a witness
C) Because the mufti only gives opinions, not rulings
D) Because the mufti is always free from bias

Key Takeaway
A mufti is like a narrator of shari'a, not a witness; personal bias does not disqualify his fatwa because it is a general, non-binding announcement.

Mufti's Enmity Nullifies Fatwa Against That Person
States disqualification rule for mufti's enmity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a mufti's fatwa is rejected due to enmity and apply the analogy to testimony rejection.

Core Idea
A mufti who targets a specific person with enmity in his fatwa becomes a legal opponent—his fatwa is rejected like hostile testimony.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a mufti may give fatwa even if he has a general dislike for a party. However, there is a critical exception: if the mufti openly opposes a specific individual in his fatwa, he becomes a 'khasman hukman'—a theoretical opponent—and his ruling is void. This passage from al-Hawi draws an analogy with the rejection of testimony from an enemy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The mufti's fatwa against a specific person is the act under consideration.
2
Identify the condition
The mufti must have 'nabadha' (openly opposed) that specific person in the fatwa itself.
The enmity must be expressed in the fatwa, not just in personal feelings.
3
Determine the legal effect
He becomes a theoretical opponent (khasman hukman) and his fatwa is rejected.
The term 'khasman hukman' is a technical legal status.
4
Observe the analogy
This mirrors the rule for testimony: the testimony of an enemy is rejected.
The qiyas (analogy) on testimony is the basis for this ruling.
5
Note the scope
This rule applies only when the enmity is targeted at a specific person; general bias does not qualify.
The source uses 'shakhsan mu'ayyanan' (specific person).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر صاحب "الحاوى " : أن المفتى إذا نابذ في فتواه شخصاً معيناً، صار خصماً حكمًا معاندا، فترد فتواه على من عاداه كما ترد شهادته عليه .
And the author of al-Hawi mentioned: that if the mufti opposes a specific person in his fatwa, he becomes a hostile opponent theoretically, so his fatwa against the one he opposes is rejected just as his testimony against him is rejected.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This is an exception or refinement of the previous rule: while general bias does not affect fatwa, if a mufti deliberately targets a specific person out of enmity, his fatwa against that individual is void. The analogy is with testimony: a hostile witness's testimony is rejected. The mufti becomes a 'khasman hakaman' (a theoretical opponent), so his fatwa is unreliable.

Key Terms
نابذ (nabadha): To openly oppose or declare war, here meaning to show enmity in a fatwa against a specific person.
خصماً حكمًا (khasman hukman): A legal opponent, meaning in the eyes of the law he is considered a biased party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever he dislikes the questioner
B) When he openly opposes a specific person in the fatwa
C) When the questioner is his relative
D) When the fatwa benefits him

Key Takeaway
A mufti who openly targets a specific person with enmity in his fatwa becomes a legal opponent, and his fatwa against that person is rejected, just as hostile testimony is rejected.
