Fasiq as Mufti: The Hanafi Ruling on Invalid Fatwa
Declares that an open sinner's fatwa is not valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the fatwa of a fasiq, identify the evidence (ijma'), and explain the later tarjih that absolutely rejects the fasiq's fatwa.

Core Idea
A fasiq's fatwa is absolutely invalid; even asking him for a fatwa is prohibited.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position that an open sinner (fasiq) cannot serve as a mufti. We will look at the consensus cited by al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, a minority view recorded in al-Kanz, and the final relied-upon position in the later Hanafi school as recorded in Radd al-Muhtar. Understanding this rule is essential for preserving the integrity of Islamic legal judgments.

Guided Path
1
State the rule and its evidentiary basis
Identify the core ruling: the fatwa of a fasiq is invalid. Note that the proof given is consensus (ijma' al-muslimin), as transmitted by al-Khatib. Also note the secondary proof: fatwa is a religious matter requiring uprightness (adalah).
This is the foundational point from the passage.
2
Define 'fasiq' in the Hanafi legal context
Understand who qualifies as a fasiq: one who commits major sins openly or persists in minor sins, thereby losing the quality of 'adalah (uprightness). This affects credibility in testimony and fatwa alike.
Open sin is key – it destroys public trust.
3
Examine the minority view from al-Kanz
The passage notes a view that a fasiq may still be suitable as a mufti because he strives (mujtahid) to avoid error in his ijtihad. This is recorded in al-Kanz and was initially accepted by some scholars.
This view is considered weak in the later school.
4
Analyze the later Hanafi response and tarjih
In Radd al-Muhtar, the reasoning of the minority view is critiqued: in later times, the fasiq may deliberately twist the text for corrupt purposes. Therefore, the fatwa of a fasiq is not to be relied upon at all. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
The tarjih mechanism: changed circumstances (taghayyur al-zaman) affect the legal conclusion.
5
Memorize the final ruling and its application
Commit to memory: 'The fatwa of the fasiq mufti is not relied upon absolutely.' Understand that this applies regardless of the fasiq's knowledge. The fasiq himself, however, must still perform ijtihad for his own actions.
This is a rasm rule – it guides the mufti's conduct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتفقوا على أن الفاسق لا تصح فتواه، ونقل الخطيب فيه إجماع المسلمين ، ويجب عليه إذا وقعت له واقعة أن يعمل باجتهاد نفسه. قال في التنوير وشرحه : " والفاسق لا يصلح مفتيًا لأن الفتوى من أمور الدين والفاسق لا يقبل قوله في الديانات ، واختاره كثير من المتأخرين وجزم به صاحب المجمع في متنه، وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة أيضا. وظاهر ما في التحرير أنه لا يحل استفتاؤه اتفاقاً . وقيل : نعم يصلح، وبه جزم في الكنز ؛ لأنه يجتهد حذار نسبة الخطأ وفى رد المحتار: قوله : "لأنه يجتهد إلخ " هذا التعليل لا يظهر في زماننا ؛ لأنه قد يعرض عن النص الضرورى قصداً لغرض فاسد، وربما عورض بالنص فيدعى فساد النص. ثم قال المحقق الشامي والحاصل أنه لا يعتمد على فتوى المفتى الفاسق مطلقا
And they agreed that the fasiq's (open sinner) fatwa is not valid, and al-Khatib transmitted consensus of the Muslims on it. And it is obligatory upon him, when an incident occurs to him, to act according to his own ijtihad. He said in al-Tanwir and its commentary: 'The fasiq is not suitable to be a mufti because fatwa is among religious matters and the fasiq is not accepted in religious matters, and many later scholars chose this, and the author of al-Majma' affirmed it in his matn, and it is also the saying of the three Imams. And the apparent meaning in al-Tahrir is that it is not permissible to ask him for a fatwa by consensus. And it was said: Yes, he is suitable, and it was affirmed in al-Kanz, because he strives to avoid attributing error. And in Radd al-Muhtar: his statement 'because he strives' etc., this reasoning is not apparent in our time, because he may deliberately turn away from the necessary text for a corrupt purpose, and perhaps he may oppose the text by claiming it is corrupt. Then al-Muhaqqiq al-Shami said: the conclusion is that the fatwa of the fasiq mufti is not relied upon absolutely.'
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage presents the established Hanafi ruling that a fasiq (one who openly commits major sins) cannot be a mufti. It cites consensus and quotes three Imams. However, there is a minority view from al-Kanz that a fasiq may still strive to be correct, but the later Hanafi position (as in Radd al-Muhtar) dismisses that due to the prevalence of corrupt motives. The obligatory duty on the fasiq himself to perform ijtihad emphasizes that fatwa is a religious responsibility. The final fatwa: the fasiq's fatwa is absolutely unreliable.

Key Terms
الفاسق (al-fasiq): One who commits major sins openly or persists in minor sins, impairing his reliability in religious matters.
إجماع المسلمين (ijma' al-muslimin): Consensus of the scholars, here claimed for the invalidity of a fasiq's fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the final relied-upon Hanafi ruling regarding the fatwa of a fasiq?
A) It is valid if he strives to be correct
B) It is valid but makruh
C) It is not to be relied upon at all
D) It is valid only in personal matters

Key Takeaway
A fasiq's fatwa is invalid by consensus; later Hanafis emphasized absolute rejection to protect the integrity of Islamic legal rulings.

Mastur and Fatwa: The Rule of Apparent Righteousness
Rasm al-mufti rule on mastur's fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a mastur (person of apparent but unverified righteousness) may give fatwa, and distinguish it from the ruling on testimony.

Core Idea
A mastur may give fatwa; inner integrity is harder to verify than for testimony.

In the Hanafi tradition, the qualification of a mufti includes 'adala (uprightness). But what level of scrutiny is required? The term 'mastur' refers to someone whose outward conduct appears upright but whose inner state has not been investigated. This passage presents the stronger opinion that such a person may still issue fatwas, because fatwa does not demand the same certainty as testimony in court.

Guided Path
1
Define 'mastur'
Identify the term: one whose apparent state is 'adala but whose inner 'adala has not been tested. Note that 'adala here means religious uprightness (avoiding major sins and minor sins consistently).
This is a technical term in Hanafi ifta' literature.
2
State the two positions
The text mentions two opinions: (1) permissible to give fatwa (the more correct view), (2) not permissible by analogy to testimony. Memorize both.
The second opinion analogizes fatwa to testimony, where mastur witnesses are rejected.
3
Identify the preferred view and its reasoning
The preferred view is the first: fatwa is allowed because inner justice is difficult for non-judges to ascertain. Fatwa is not a judicial matter requiring the same evidentiary standards.
This ruling is specific to fatwa, not to giving testimony.
4
Connect to the parallel ruling on marriage witnesses
The text notes that the dispute mirrors another: validity of marriage contracted in the presence of mastur witnesses. Know that this is a separate but related issue.
This shows the consistency of the principles across different areas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المستور : وهو الذى ظاهره العدالة ولم تختبر عدالته باطنا ، ففيه وجهان أصحهما جواز فتواه ؛ لأن العدالة الباطنة يعسر معرفتها على غير القضاة، والثاني : لا يجوز كالشهادة و الخلاف كالخلاف في صحة النكاح بحضور المستورين.
As for the mastur (concealed) – and he is one whose apparent state is justice but whose inner justice has not been tested – then there are two positions: the more correct is the permissibility of his fatwa, because inner justice is difficult to know for other than judges. The second: it is not permissible, like testimony. And the disagreement is like the disagreement regarding the validity of marriage in the presence of mastur witnesses.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage addresses the mastur: a person who seems upright outwardly but has not been verified internally. The stronger Hanafi opinion allows his fatwa because fatwa does not require the same level of scrutiny as testimony; inner justice is hard for non-judges to ascertain. The second opinion prohibits it, drawing analogy to testimony, where a mastur's testimony is rejected. The text notes that this mirrors the dispute about marriage contracts witnessed by mastur witnesses.

Key Terms
المستور (al-mastur): One whose apparent state is 'adala (uprightness) but whose inner state has not been investigated.

Key Takeaway

Fatwa of Innovators: When Accepted and When Rejected
Rasm al-mufti rule on innovator fatwa acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on the acceptance of fatwas from innovators, distinguishing between mild innovation and extreme deviation that necessitates rejection.

Core Idea
Fatwa of an innovator is valid unless the innovation removes him from the fold or entails enmity to the Salaf.

This lesson examines a key rule in Rasm al-Mufti: when can a mufti accept the fatwa of an innovator? Al-Saymari and al-Khatib lay down the principle: mild innovation does not disqualify a fatwa, but extreme deviation or hostility to the Companions does. This distinction preserves the integrity of the fatwa while avoiding wholesale rejection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Al-Saymari states a general rule: fatwas of ahl al-ahwa' and Khawarij who are not excommunicated are valid. Al-Khatib transmits this then adds an exception for those who insult the Salaf.
This is a base rule with a qualifier.
2
Define the categories
Ahl al-ahwa' = people of desires (innovators in belief but not takfir). Khawarij = those who rebel. Shurah = sinful Khawarij. Rafidah = extreme Shi'a who insult the Companions.
The terms from the passage.
3
Apply the condition of acceptance
An innovator's fatwa is accepted as long as we do not excommunicate him (takfir) or declare him sinful (tafsiq) solely due to his bid'a.
This preserves basic adala.
4
Identify the disqualifying factors
Al-Khatib rejects fatwas of Shurah (extreme Khawarij) and Rafidah who insult the righteous predecessors. Their hostility makes them untrustworthy.
Enmity to the Salaf is a disqualifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الصيمري وتصح فتاوى أهل الأهواء، والخوارج، ومن لا نكفره ببدعته ولا نفسقه. ونقل الخطيب هذا ثم قال : وأما الشراة والرافضة الذين يسبون السلف الصالح ، ففتاويهم مردودة، وأقوالهم ساقطة .
Al-Saymari said: The fatwas of the people of desires, Kharijites, and those whom we do not excommunicate due to their innovation nor consider sinful, are valid. And al-Khatib transmitted this, then said: As for the Kharijites (Shurah) and the Rafidah who insult the righteous predecessors, their fatwas are rejected and their statements are void.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
Al-Saymari distinguishes between innovators whose innovation does not constitute kufr or fisq – their fatwas are accepted. However, al-Khatib adds that groups like the Khawarij (who rebel) and the Rafidah (extreme Shi'a) who insult the Companions are beyond the pale; their fatwas are rejected. This ruling ties fatwa acceptance to the mufti's religious integrity; those who reject core Islamic beliefs cannot be trusted as transmitters of divine law.

Key Terms
الشراة (al-shurah): Another name for Khawarij, those who rebel against the legitimate ruler and excommunicate sinners.
الرافضة (al-rafidah): Extreme Shi'a who reject the first three caliphs and insult the Companions.
أهل الأهواء (ahl al-ahwa'): People of desires, i.e., theological innovators whose innovation does not reach the level of kufr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All people of desires
B) Only those who commit major sins
C) Khawarij and Rafidah who insult the Salaf
D) Those whose innovation is minor

Key Takeaway
An innovator's fatwa is accepted unless the innovation removes him from the fold or he displays hostility to the Salaf.

Judge May Also Give Fatwas
Discusses whether a judge can also act as a mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the well-known Hanafi ruling on a judge giving fatwa, explain the basis for the two opinions, and apply the principle of avoiding tuhma in mufti adab.

Core Idea
A judge may give fatwas, but caution is advised when fatwa relates to matters that may later come to court.

The role of a judge and a mufti are distinct, yet often overlap. This passage explores whether a judge can also issue fatwas. The well-known Hanafi view affirms permissibility, but a minority opinion warns of suspicion. Understanding this tension is crucial for a mufti who also serves as a judge, ensuring he upholds both roles without compromising integrity.

Guided Path
1
State the mashhur view
Identify the well-known Hanafi ruling: a judge may give fatwa without dislike. Note the phrase 'without dislike' (bila karahah).
This is the relied-upon position.
2
Identify the second opinion
Find the alternative view: some Hanafis say a judge should not give fatwa on matters of judgment because it is a place of suspicion (tuhma).
This opinion is not the mashhur.
3
Understand the distinction between fatwa and judgment
Recognize that fatwa is based on diyana (religious duty) while judgment is based on outward evidence (zahir). This can lead to apparent inconsistency.
The judge's integrity is not necessarily compromised.
4
Analyze the practical consequence
Consider what happens if a judge gives a fatwa then later judges a similar case differently: the losing litigant may use the fatwa as a criticism.
This is the core of the tuhma objection.
5
Apply the adab principle
A judge-mufti should avoid giving fatwa on matters likely to come before him as judge, or clearly separate the two roles to prevent suspicion.
Caution does not negate permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقاضي كغيره في جواز الفتيا بلا كراهة، هذا هو الصحيح المشهور من مذهبنا . قال الشيخ : ورأيت في بعض تعاليق الشيخ أبي حامد أن له الفتوى فى العبادات، وما لا يتعلّق بالقضاء. وفي القضاء وجهان لأصحابنا: أحدهما: الجواز ؛ لأنه أهل والثاني : لا ؛ لأنه موضع تهمة(۳). قوله : "لأنه موضع تهمة " فيكون لكلام الناس عليه مجالا ، ولأحد الخصمين عليه مقالا، وهذا لأنه ربما يقع الفرق بين الفتوى والقضاء؛ لأن المفتي يفتي بالديانة، والقاضى يقضى بالظاهر ، ولأن القاضى إذا راعي الحجج والوثائق والبينات، يثبت الحكم عنده بخلاف ما أفتى به ، فإذا أفتى بشيء ثم وقع الخصام في مثله واحتكموا إليه وقضى بخلاف ما أفتى اتخذ ذلك طعنا عليه، ويكون هو صادقا في فتواه وقضاءه كليهما . من أجل ذلك قال شريح: "أنا أقضى لكم ولا أفتى " .
And the judge is like others in the permissibility of giving fatwa without dislike; this is the correct and well-known view of our madhhab. The shaykh said: I saw in some glosses of Abu Hamid that he may give fatwa in matters of worship and what does not pertain to judgment. Regarding matters of judgment, there are two opinions among our companions: one: permissibility, because he is qualified; the second: no, because it is a place of suspicion (tuhma). His statement: 'because it is a place of suspicion' – thus people's talk would have scope against him, and one of the litigants would have a claim against him. This is because sometimes there is a difference between fatwa and judgment: the mufti gives fatwa based on religious duty (diyana), while the judge judges based on outward evidence. And because the judge, when he considers arguments, documents, and proofs, the ruling is established in his view contrary to what he gave as fatwa. So if he gave a fatwa on something, then a similar dispute occurs and they take him as judge, and he judges contrary to his fatwa, that becomes a criticism against him, although he is truthful in both his fatwa and his judgment. For this reason, Shuraih said: 'I judge for you, but I do not give fatwa.'
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
The well-known Hanafi position is that a judge may give fatwas without any dislike. However, there is a concern when the subject matter relates to qada (judgment) itself: giving a fatwa on a matter that may later come before him as a judge could create suspicion of bias. Abu Hamid restricts the judge's fatwa to areas outside judicial matters (like worship). The two opinions reflect this tension. The text explains the reason: a judge's fatwa is based on religious duty, but his judgment relies on external proofs; inconsistency could lead people to question his integrity. Shuraih's famous saying illustrates the caution of early judges. The lesson is that while a judge can be a mufti, it is safer to avoid fatwa on matters that may later come to his court.

Key Terms
القاضي (al-qadi): A judge who decides disputes with binding authority based on outward evidence.
الفتيا (al-fatya): A non-binding legal opinion based on evidence, given by a mufti.
التهمة (al-tuhma): Suspicion or accusation, here referring to potential suspicion of bias if the judge's fatwa conflicts with his judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the well-known Hanafi ruling on a judge giving fatwa?
A) It is not allowed at all
B) It is allowed but disliked
C) It is allowed without dislike
D) It is allowed only in worship matters

Key Takeaway
A judge is permitted to give fatwas without dislike, but he should exercise caution when the subject matter relates to cases that may later come before him.

When a Judge May Issue a Fatwa: Reconciling the Rulings
Dispute and reconciliation on judge giving fatwa to litigants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a judge may issue a fatwa and explain the reconciliation between the conflicting views.

Core Idea
A judge may give fatwa to a non-litigant but must avoid giving fatwa to a litigant to prevent abuse.

This lesson addresses an important aspect of mufti adab: the rules governing a judge who also issues fatwas. The passage presents two seemingly contradictory views—one discouraging fatwa in legal matters, another separating judging from fatwa—and then reconciles them by distinguishing between litigants and non-litigants. Understanding this distinction is essential for maintaining judicial impartiality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two initial views
Read the passage and note the positions of Ibn al-Mundhir (fatwa disliked in legal matters) and Shuraih (I judge but do not give fatwa).
These represent the strict view.
2
Recognize the reconciliation
Identify the sources (Bahr, Kafi al-Hakim) that harmonize the views by focusing on the recipient of the fatwa.
The key is the distinction between litigant and non-litigant.
3
Understand the distinction
Analyze the wording: 'no harm for a judge to give fatwa to one who has not litigated before him' vs 'should not give fatwa to either of the two litigants'.
The prohibition applies only to litigants.
4
Note the authority approval
The passage states that this reconciliation was approved in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, a key Hanafi reference. Recognize this as the mufta bihi.
This gives weight to the reconciled view.
5
Apply to a judicial scenario
Consider a modern court: a judge is asked for a fatwa by a litigant. Apply the rule and explain why fatwa is not given. Then consider a non-litigant.
Practice applying the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن المنذر : تكره الفتوى فى مسائل الأحكام الشرعية ) ، وقال شريح: أنا أقضى ولا أفتى. ووفق بعضهم بين القولين، ففى البحر عن الظهيرية : ولا بأس للقاضي أن يفتي من لم يخاصم إليه، ولا يفتى أحد الخصمين فيما خوصم إليه . وفي كافي الحاكم : وأكره للقاضي أن يفتى فى القضاء للخصوم كراهة أن يعلم خصمه قوله فيتحرز منه بالباطل . فهذا كما ترى جمع بين القولين بحمل قول الجواز على الإفتاء لغير الخصم، وعدم الجواز على الإفتاء للخصم . وأقره فى الدر المختار فراجعه .
Ibn al-Mundhir said: 'Fatwa is disliked in matters of [litigious] legal rulings.' And Shuraih said: 'I judge but do not issue fatwa.' And some reconciled between the two statements: in al-Bahr from al-Zahiriyyah: 'There is no harm for a judge to give fatwa to one who has not litigated before him, but he should not give fatwa to either of the two litigants in what is litigated before him.' And in Kafi al-Hakim: 'I dislike for a judge to give fatwa in judgment to litigants, lest the litigant learn his statement and thereby guard himself with falsehood.' This, as you see, is a reconciliation between the two statements by carrying the permissive statement on giving fatwa to other than the litigant, and the non-permissive on giving fatwa to the litigant. And it was approved in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa presents a ruling on whether a judge may issue fatwas to those who appear before him. The text first quotes two views: Ibn al-Mundhir declares fatwa disliked in legal matters, and Shuraih (a famous judge) separates his judicial role from fatwa-giving. The commentary then harmonizes these views by citing the Zahiriyyah position that a judge may give fatwa to someone not his litigant, but should refrain from giving fatwa to the litigants themselves, lest they misuse his statement. Al-Hakim's Kafi adds dislike for giving fatwa to litigants due to potential abuse. The reconciliation is that the prohibition applies only to litigants, not to others. This is later approved in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, a key Hanafi reference. The lesson teaches mufti adab: a judge must be cautious not to compromise his impartiality.

Key Terms
الفتوى (al-fatwa): A non-binding legal opinion issued by a qualified jurist (mufti) on a matter of Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciled view, when is it permissible for a judge to issue a fatwa?
A) To any person seeking a fatwa.
B) Only to the litigants in his court.
C) To anyone who has not litigated before him, but not to the litigants.
D) He should never issue fatwas because he is a judge.

Key Takeaway
The rule for a judge giving fatwa depends on the recipient: fatwa to a litigant is prohibited, while fatwa to a non-litigant is permitted.

Definition of the Absolute Mufti and Fard Kifayah
Defining the absolute mufti and the obligation of fard kifayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qualifications of an absolute mufti and explain why issuing fatwa is a communal obligation (fard kifayah).

Core Idea
The absolute mufti must possess comprehensive knowledge of evidence and usul; his existence fulfills the community's obligation.

This passage from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa defines the highest level of legal authority: the absolute independent mufti. It lists the extensive requirements — from knowledge of detailed evidence to practical training — and clarifies that having such a scholar is a communal duty. Understanding this definition helps us appreciate the gravity of issuing fatwa and the qualifications needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Recognize that the passage defines 'al-mufti al-mustaqil' (absolute independent mufti). Note that this is the highest level of legal authority.
The title 'mufti' in later Hanafi usage may refer to a lower rank; here it is the absolute mujtahid.
2
List the qualifications
Extract the necessary knowledge and skills: detailed evidence (Quran, Sunnah, ijma, qiyas), usul al-fiqh for derivation, auxiliary sciences (Arabic, naskh, etc.), scholarly differences, and practical training.
The passage uses 'al-umuru bi-maqasidiha'? No, but it emphasizes comprehensive knowledge.
3
Understand the legal status
The mufti's existence is a fard kifayah (communal obligation). The community must produce at least one such scholar; if all refrain, all sin.
Al-Baghawi's quote reinforces this with Qur'anic evidence (Surah al-Tawbah 9:122).
4
Distinguish from lower levels
The absolute mufti is independent (mustaqil) and not bound by a madhhab. Partial mujtahids or muqallid muftis do not meet the definition.
Footnote distinguishes absolute mujtahid from the partial or restricted.

Source Text

Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi (26): By knowledge of the evidences of legal rulings from the Book, Sunnah, consensus, analogy, and what is attached to them in detail (1). They have been detailed in books of fiqh, so they are made easy - praise be to Allah. And that he be knowledgeable of what is required in the evidences, the aspects of their indication, and the method of deriving rulings from them - this is derived from usul al-fiqh. He must be knowledgeable of the sciences of Quran, hadith, abrogating and abrogated, grammar, language, morphology, and the differences and agreements of scholars to the extent that he can fulfill the conditions of the evidences and derive from them. He must have practice and training in using that, be knowledgeable of fiqh, and have mastery of its major issues and details. So whoever gathers these descriptions is the absolute independent mufti, by whom the communal obligation is fulfilled (2), and he is the absolute independent mujtahid (3); because he is independent with the evidences without imitation and adherence to a particular school. (1) His saying: 'in detail' is attached to his saying 'قيما' (upholding), not to his saying 'and what is attached to them'. (2) His saying: 'communal obligation' – Muhyi al-Sunnah Abu Muhammad al-Baghawi said in his book 'al-Tahdhib', which is among the greatest books authored in fiqh: 'Knowledge is divided into individual obligation and communal obligation. He mentioned the individual obligation, then said: 'The communal obligation is to learn what reaches the rank of ijtihad, the position of fatwa and judgment, and to exit from the ranks of imitators. It is upon all people to undertake learning it, but if one or two from every area arise, the obligation falls from the rest. If all refrain from learning it, they all sin because of the suspension of the rulings of Shariah. Allah said: {If only a group from every party among them would go out to gain deeper knowledge in religion and to warn their people when they return to them that they might be cautious.} Reference: Usul al-Fiqh by al-Zuhayli p.1086. (3) His saying: 'the absolute independent' is a precaution against the partial and the restricted. The absolute mujtahid is the one who has acquired the faculty of ijtihad in all chapters of Shariah. The independent is the one who independently establishes the rules and derives particular cases, not imitating others, neither in principles nor in branches, and he does not attribute himself to another mujtahid.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute mufti (المفتى المطلق), who is the highest level of legal authority. The text lists his qualifications: knowledge of detailed evidences from Quran, Sunnah, ijma, and qiyas; mastery of usul al-fiqh to know how to derive rulings; proficiency in auxiliary sciences like Arabic grammar, language, morphology, and knowledge of naskh; familiarity with scholarly agreement and disagreement; and practical experience. He must be a hafiz (memorizer) of major fiqh issues. Whoever possesses all these is the absolute independent mufti, and his existence fulfills the fard kifayah (communal obligation). The commentary clarifies that 'on detail' refers to the knowledge of evidences, not the attached matters. Al-Baghawi’s quote in the footnote explains fard kifayah: it is obligatory on the community to have someone reach the level of ijtihad for fatwa and judgment; if none do, all sin. The footnote also distinguishes the absolute mujtahid (full ijtihad in all chapters) from the partial or restricted mujtahid, emphasizing independence from taqlid.

Key Terms
المفتى المطلق (al-mufti al-mustaqil): The absolute independent mufti who is a mujtahid mutlaq, capable of issuing fatwa in all areas without taqlid.
فرض الكفاية (fard kifayah): A communal obligation; if some perform it, the rest are relieved.
المجتهد المطلق (al-mujtahid al-mustaqil): The independent jurist who can derive rulings from primary sources without following an imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Memorizing one madhhab's rulings
B) Knowing evidences, usul, auxiliary sciences, and having practice
C) Being appointed by a ruler
D) Having ijaza from a scholar

Key Takeaway
The absolute mufti is defined by comprehensive knowledge and independence; his presence is a communal obligation.

Memorizing Fiqh: Condition for Mufti, Not for Mujtahid Mutlaq
Dispute on memorization as condition for ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scholarly positions on memorizing fiqh and identify which type of mufti requires it.

Core Idea
Fiqh is the fruit of ijtihad; condition must precede fruit, but the public mufti needs memorization for efficiency.

This passage from al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa presents a nuanced dispute: while absolute ijtihad does not require memorization of all fiqh rulings, the mufti who serves the community and must issue fatwas promptly does require it. The text reconciles two opposing views, and this lesson will help you understand the rational argument (dalil aqli) behind the preferred position, which aligns with the Hanafi distinction between mujtahid mutlaq and mujtahid fi al-madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
The first view (from many famous books) holds that memorizing fiqh is not a condition for ijtihad; the second view (al-Isfarayini et al.) holds that it is a condition. Note the key argument against: fiqh is the fruit of ijtihad, and a condition cannot be posterior to its conditioned.
2
Understand the argument against memorization
The phrase 'because fiqh is its fruit, so it is delayed from it, and the condition of a thing does not be delayed from it' is a logical argument: since ijtihad produces fiqh, memorizing fiqh would require ijtihad to have already occurred, leading to circularity.
3
Recognize the third view as a compromise
The third view distinguishes between the absolute mujtahid (who can derive rulings directly from sources) and the mufti who fulfills the communal obligation (who needs quick access). The text declares this third view 'correct' because the mufti's role demands efficiency, which memorization provides.
This distinction mirrors the Hanafi categories of mujtahid mutlaq and mujtahid fi al-madhhab.
4
Analyze the rational argument (dalil aqli)
The footnote explains: 'the state of the mufti requires that he be in a condition that makes it easy for him to perceive the rulings of incidents quickly without great exertion. And this cannot be attained by anyone except by memorizing the chapters of fiqh and its issues.' This is a practical, rational argument based on the function of the mufti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آداب الفتوى للإمام النووي
۲۷
وما شرطناه من حفظه لمسائل الفقه لم يُشترط في
كثير من الكتب المشهورة ؛ لكونه ليس شرطاً لمنصب الاجتهاد ؛ لأن الفقه ثمرته فيتأخر عنه ، وشرط الشيء لا يتأخر عنه . وشرطه الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني وصاحبه أبو منصور البغدادي وغيرهما .
واشتراطه فى المفتى (۱) الذي يتأدّى به فرض الكفاية هو الصحيح، وإن
لم يكن كذلك في المجتهد المستقل.
(۱) قوله : واشتراطه فى المفتى هذا قول ثالث، وهو بمنزلة التحكيم بين القولين السابقين. وقوله : "هو الصحيح " لأن حال المفتى يقتضى اشتراط كونه على صفة يسهل عليه معها إدراك أحكام الوقائع على القرب من غير تعب كبير . وهذا لا يحصل لأحد من الخلق إلا بحفظ أبواب الفقه ومسائله .
Etiquette of Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi (27): And what we stipulated of his memorization of fiqh issues is not made a condition in many famous books; because it is not a condition for the position of ijtihad; because fiqh is its fruit, so it is delayed from it, and the condition of a thing does not be delayed from it. And Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and his companion Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi and others made it a condition. And making it a condition for the mufti (1) by whom the communal obligation is fulfilled is correct, although it is not so for the independent mujtahid. (1) His saying: 'and making it a condition for the mufti' is a third view, and it is like an arbitration between the two previous views. His saying: 'it is correct' because the state of the mufti requires that he be in a condition that makes it easy for him to perceive the rulings of incidents quickly without great exertion. And this cannot be attained by anyone except by memorizing the chapters of fiqh and its issues.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-madhhab dispute (within the Shafi'i school, but relevant for general usul). It begins by reporting that many famous books do not require a mujtahid to have memorized all fiqh issues, because fiqh is the fruit of ijtihad, and a condition cannot be posterior to the conditioned. However, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi held that memorization is a condition. The text then presents a third (preferred) view: memorization of fiqh is a condition for the mufti who fulfills the communal obligation (i.e., the mufti who gives fatwa to the public), but not for the absolute mujtahid who can derive rulings instantly. The footnote explains this third view as a compromise: the mufti needs easy access to rulings for quick fatwa, which requires memorization. The argument is rational (dalil aqli): the mufti's role demands quick answers, so he must have fiqh at his fingertips. This lesson shows the nuance within the qualifications: the absolute mujtahid can derive from sources any time, but the practicing mufti benefits from storing the madhhab's rulings in memory for efficiency.

Key Terms
شرط الاجتهاد (shart al-ijtihad): A condition for the position of ijtihad; the text discusses whether memorization of fiqh is such a condition.
المفتى المتأدى به فرض الكفاية (al-mufti al-muta'add bihi fard al-kifayah): The mufti by whom the communal obligation of fatwa is fulfilled, the one who gives fatwa to the community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) For the absolute mujtahid only
B) For the mufti who fulfills the communal obligation (fard kifayah), but not for the absolute mujtahid
C) For both the mufti and the absolute mujtahid
D) For neither the mufti nor the absolute mujtahid

Key Takeaway
Memorization of fiqh is required for the public mufti who must issue fatwas promptly, but not for the absolute mujtahid who can derive rulings from primary sources.

The Mufti's Memory Standard: Most Rulings, Not All
Clarifying the memory requirement for a mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi adab requirement for a mufti's memory and apply it to assess whether a mufti's knowledge is sufficient.

Core Idea
A mufti must know most rulings, not all — perfection is not required.

A common misconception is that a mufti must have instant recall of every fiqh ruling. This lesson clarifies the realistic standard set by the classical Hanafi adab: the mufti must have memorized the majority of rulings and be able to quickly find the remainder. This balance prevents excessive burden while ensuring competence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Requirement
Read the passage and identify what is explicitly stated about the mufti's memory. The text says it is not required to have all rulings in mind.
Focus on the phrase 'لا يشترط أن يكون جميع الأحكام على ذهنه'.
2
Understand the Sufficient Standard
The text states that it suffices for the mufti to be a memorizer of most (حافظا المعظم). Define what 'most' means in this context—the majority of rulings, not every detail.
The footnote mentions a variant: 'حافظا للمعظم' (memorizer of the majority).
3
Apply the Ability to Retrieve
The mufti must be able to perceive the remaining rulings quickly (متمكنا من إدراك الباقى على قرب). This implies research skill, not just memory.
4
Connect to Mufti's Practice
Consider how this standard applies in real fatwa-giving. A mufti can be trusted even if he occasionally needs to look up a ruling, as long as he has mastery over the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٩ - ثم لا يشترط أن يكون جميع الأحكام على ذهنه، بل يكفيه كونه حافظا المعظم (۳) ، متمكنا من إدراك الباقى على قرب .
(۲) قوله : "حافظ المعظم" كذا في جميع النسخ التي بأيدينا، وفي فتاوى ابن الصلاح : حافظا للمعظم . (۲۷/۱)
19 - Then it is not required that all rulings be in his mind, rather it suffices that he be a memorizer of most (3), able to perceive the rest quickly. (2) His saying: 'memorizer of most' – as in all copies we have; in the Fatawa of Ibn al-Salah: 'memorizer of the majority'.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This short point sets a practical standard: the mufti does not need to have every single ruling committed to memory. It is sufficient that he has memorized the majority (المعظم) of rulings and can retrieve the remaining ones quickly when needed. The footnote notes a textual variation: in Ibn al-Salah's Fatawa, it is 'حافظا للمعظم' (memorizer of the majority). This ruling relieves the mufti of impossible burden while maintaining competence.

Key Terms
المعظم (al-mu'azzam): The majority (of rulings); the mufti must have memorized most of the fiqh issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only the famous rulings

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti must have memorized the majority of rulings and be able to quickly retrieve the rest; perfect recall is not required.

The Mufti's Requirement of Arithmetic Knowledge
Resolving the ikhtilaf on arithmetic requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ikhtilaf on whether a mufti must know arithmetic and state the preferred Hanafi position with its rationale.

Core Idea
A mufti must know enough arithmetic to resolve fara'idh and similar quantitative fiqh problems.

A mufti's qualifications include not only mastery of fiqh and usul but also the computational skills needed to correctly calculate inheritance shares, zakah, and other numerical issues. This lesson examines a debated point among Hanafi scholars: is arithmetic knowledge a condition (shart) for giving fatwa? The passage presents the ikhtilaf and settles on the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
Read the opening phrase: 'وهل يشترط أن يعرف من الحساب...' – the question is whether a mufti must know arithmetic to correct fiqhi problems that involve calculations.
The term 'المسائل الحسابية الفقهية' refers to issues like fara'idh, where arithmetic is essential.
2
Recognize the ikhtilaf
The text reports that Abu Ishaq and Abu Mansur narrated a difference of opinion among 'our companions' (أصحابنا) – meaning Hanafi scholars. Some required arithmetic, some did not.
Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi are early transmitters of Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
State the two positions
One position: arithmetic is not required. The other: it is required. The text does not name the proponents of each, but the ikhtilaf is real.
The basis for not requiring it may be that arithmetic is a tool, not core fiqh.
4
Determine the tarjih
The text says 'والأصح اشتراطه' – the most correct view is that it is required. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The term 'الأصح' indicates a clear preference among later Hanafi authorities.
5
Explain the rationale
Arithmetic is needed to issue correct fatwas in fara'idh and other quantitative matters. Without it, errors in shares and balances would lead to incorrect legal rulings.
This ensures the mufti fulfills the duty of accuracy in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-۲۰ - وهل يشترط أن يعرف من الحساب ما يصحح به المسائل الحسابية الفقهية؟ حكى أبو إسحاق وأبو منصور فيه خلافا لأصحابنا، والأصح
اشتراطه .
20 – And is it required that he know from arithmetic what enables him to correct the arithmetic-fiqh problems? Abu Ishaq and Abu Mansur narrated a difference among our companions [on this], and the most correct is that it is required.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This point addresses whether a mufti must learn arithmetic (حساب) to handle fiqhi issues that involve numerical calculations, such as inheritance (fara'idh) and some commercial transactions. The text reports that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi transmitted an ikhtilaf (difference of opinion) among the Shafi'i scholars. The preferred view (الأصح) is that such knowledge is required. This ensures that the mufti can compute shares and balances correctly without error.

Key Terms
الحساب (al-hisab): Arithmetic, specifically calculation skills needed for fara'idh and other quantitative fiqh matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The mufti must have sufficient arithmetic to handle fara'idh and similar calculations; the later Hanafi authorities require it.

Partial Ijtihad and Specialized Mufti
Discussing the validity of partial ijtihad in specific fiqh chapters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of partial ijtihad (tajzi' al-ijtihad) and evaluate the arguments for and against its validity in Hanafi fatwa hierarchy.

Core Idea
A mufti can specialize in one fiqh chapter if he masters all its evidences.

Ijtihad is often thought to be an all-or-nothing ability, but classical scholarship recognizes specialization. This lesson explores whether a scholar can be a mujtahid in only one area, such as inheritance (fara'idh) or rituals (manasik). The Hanafi school, along with many Shafi'i scholars, permits this, while some oppose it. We will examine the reasoning and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
The passage asks: can a person have ijtihad ability limited to one chapter of fiqh (e.g., fara'idh)? This is called tajzi' al-ijtihad (partial ijtihad).
Contrast with absolute ijtihad required for a mufti in all chapters.
2
Identify the positions
There are three views: some (al-Ghazali and Ibn Burhan) permit it; some absolutely forbid it; Ibn al-Sabbagh permits it only for inheritance. The text indicates the preferred view is permission.
The footnote (3) explains the opposing view and its rebuttal.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
Opponents argue that unknown evidences from other chapters may contradict what is known, leading to error.
This is based on qiyas? Actually, it is a rational argument: the possibility of contradiction.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The response is that this concern is far-fetched because the specialized scholar is assumed to know all evidences of that chapter, so no unknown contradiction can arise.
The response uses a conditional: 'the assumption is that he knows all evidences of the chapter'.
5
Determine the preferred view
The text ends with 'wal-ashah' (the most correct), indicating that the permissive view is relied upon.
This aligns with the Hanafi hierarchy allowing 'mujtahid fi al-bab'.

-۲۱- ثم إنما نشترط اجتماع العلوم المذكورة في مفت مطلق في جميع أبواب الشرع، فأما مفت فى باب خاص كالمناسك والفرائض، فيكفيه معرفة ذلك الباب، كذا قطع به الغزالی و صاحبه ابن برهان - بفتح الباء وغيرهما، ومنهم من منعه مطلقا (۳)، وأجازه ابن الصباغ فى الفرائض خاصة، والأصح
(۳) قوله : ( ومنهم من منعه مطلقا " أى منع تجزء الاجتهاد بأن تحصل لبعض الناس قوة الاجتهاد في بعض الأبواب كالفرائض بأن يعلم أدلته باستقراء منه أو من مجتهد كامل وينظر فيها، ودليل هذا القول إنه يحتمل أن يكون فيما لم يعلمه من الأدلة معارض لما علمه بخلاف من أحاط بالكل ونظر فيه . وأجيب بأن هذا بعيد جدا؛ لأن الفرض أنه علم جميع أدلة الباب .
21 – Then we only stipulate the gathering of the mentioned sciences for an absolute mufti in all chapters of Shariah. As for a mufti in a specific chapter like rituals (manasik) and inheritance (fara'idh), knowledge of that chapter suffices him. This was decisively stated by al-Ghazali and his companion Ibn Burhan (with a fath on the ba) and others. And among them are those who absolutely forbid it (3). And Ibn al-Sabbagh permitted it specifically for inheritance. And the most correct is… (3) His saying: 'and among them are those who absolutely forbid it' i.e., they forbid the division of ijtihad, meaning that some people obtain the power of ijtihad in some chapters like inheritance by knowing its evidences through his own induction or from a complete mujtahid and examines them. The proof of this view is that it is possible that in what he does not know of the evidences there is a contradiction to what he knows, unlike one who encompasses all and examines it. And it was answered that this is very far-fetched because the assumption is that he knows all the evidences of that chapter.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of partial ijtihad (تجزئ الاجتهاد) – ijtihad limited to a specific field like rituals (manasik) or inheritance (fara'idh). For an absolute mufti in all chapters, all sciences are required. But for a mufti specializing in one chapter, knowledge of that chapter alone is sufficient. Al-Ghazali and Ibn Burhan affirmed this. Other scholars absolutely forbade it, arguing that unknown evidences may conflict with what is known. However, the answer is that such conflict is unlikely because the person knows all evidences of that chapter. Ibn al-Sabbagh allowed it specifically for inheritance. The text ends with 'والأصح' (the most correct), implying that the permissive view is preferred. The footnote provides the reasoning against partial ijtihad and the response. This lesson shows that Hanafi scholars also recognized specialization, e.g., in fara'idh, though they considered it a lower level of ijtihad.

Key Terms
تجزئ الاجتهاد (tajzi' al-ijtihad): Partial ijtihad, where a scholar has ijtihad ability only in specific chapters, e.g., fara'idh.
مفت مطلق (mufti mutlaq): An absolute mufti who can issue fatwa in all chapters of Shariah.
باب خاص (bab khas): A specific chapter of fiqh, such as inheritance or rituals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view, can a person be a mufti only in the chapter of inheritance?
A) No, ijtihad must be absolute
B) Yes, if he knows all evidences of that chapter
C) Yes, but only if he also knows all other chapters
D) No, it is forbidden

Key Takeaway
Partial ijtihad is valid in Hanafi law for specialized chapters like inheritance, provided the scholar knows all its evidences.

Independent vs Bound Mujtahids: The Hanafi Position
Discussion of ijtihad ranks in the Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two views on whether later Hanafi scholars (e.g., Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) were independent mujtahids or muqallids, and identify the relied-upon position of the verifiers.

Core Idea
Later Hanafi imams were not blind followers; they followed Abu Hanifa's method because they found it soundest.

This passage from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa addresses a critical question in Hanafi fiqh history: Were later luminaries like Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar independent mujtahids (mustaqill) or mere imitators (muqallid)? The answer determines the authority of their fatwas and the nature of the school's development. Al-Nawawi presents two positions and clarifies the stance of the verifiers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage to find the two opposing views: one claiming later scholars were pure muqallids, the other (held by the verifiers) that they were not muqallids but followed the imam's method because they found it soundest.
The first view is attributed to companions of Malik, Ahmad, Dawud, and most Hanafis.
2
Analyze the verifiers' evidence
The verifiers argue that later scholars engaged in ijtihad using the imam's method, as evidenced by their disagreements with the imam and their own fatwas.
Quote from Abu Ali al-Sanji: 'We followed al-Shafi'i ... not that we imitated him.'
3
Note the dissent
Some, like Abu Amr, argue that denying taqlid entirely is untenable; the scholars were bound to the school's framework.
This is a nuanced middle position.
4
Understand the consequence
The practical result (thamarah al-khilaf): fatwas from these later scholars carry authority but can be critiqued using the imam's method.
They are considered mujtahid muqayyad (bound mujtahid).
5
Review the footnote on independent ijtihad
al-Lakhnawi refutes the claim that independent ijtihad ended after the early imams, listing later independent mujtahids.
This affirms that the door of ijtihad remains open.

Adab al-Fatwa by Imam al-Nawawi. 29. Its evidence: because he is described with the attribute of the independent, and it is attributed to him because he followed his path in ijtihad. And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq claimed this attribute for our companions, so it is related from the companions of Malik, Ahmad, Dawud, and most Hanafis that they went to the schools of their imams out of taqlid for them. Then he said: The correct opinion that the verifiers adopted is what our companions adopted, which is that they went to the school of al-Shafi'i not out of taqlid for him, but because they found his methods in ijtihad and qiyas to be the soundest methods, and they had no alternative but to engage in ijtihad, so they followed his path and sought to know the rulings by the method of al-Shafi'i. And Abu Ali al-Sanji (with kasra of the sin) mentioned something similar, saying: 'We followed al-Shafi'i to the exclusion of others because we found his statement to be the most preponderant and most just of statements, not that we imitated him.' I say: What they mentioned agrees with what al-Shafi'i commanded them, then al-Muzani at the beginning of his Mukhtasar and others with his statement: 'While informing them of his prohibition of following him and following others.' Neither independent nor bound; since he did not imitate his imam, but followed his method in ijtihad, like Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar from the Hanafis, Ibn al-Qasim and Ashhab from the Malikis, and al-Bariti and al-Za'farani from the Shafi'is. (1) His statement: 'this attribute for our companions' said al-Shaykh Siddiq Hasan Khan in Adab al-Mufti: 'And this rank has been claimed by from the Hanbalis al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, and al-Qadi Abu Ali ibn Abi Musa in his Sharh al-Irshad, and from the Shafi'is many people. And the Hanafis differed concerning Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl; the Shafi'is concerning al-Muzani, Ibn Suraij, Ibn al-Mundhir, and Muhammad ibn Nasr al-Marwazi; the Malikis concerning Ashhab, Ibn Abd al-Hakam, Ibn al-Qasim, and Ibn Wahb; and the Hanbalis concerning Abu Hamid and al-Qadi: Were these individuals independent in ijtihad or bound by the schools of their imams? And whoever examines the states of these individuals, their fatwas, and their choices knows that they were not imitators of their imams in everything they said, and their disagreement with them is more apparent than can be denied, although among them were the independent and the abundant. And their rank is below the rank of the imams in independence of ijtihad.' (2) His statement: 'then al-Muzani at the beginning of his Mukhtasar' said Abu Ibrahim Isma'il ibn Yahya al-Muzani at the beginning of his Mukhtasar: 'I abbreviated this book from the knowledge of Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and from his statement; to make it close for whoever desires it, while informing them of his prohibition of following him and following others, so that he may consider it for his religion and be cautious for himself. And success is from Allah.' And his statement: 'while informing them of his prohibition' i.e., with my informing whoever desires the knowledge of al-Shafi'i that he, may Allah be pleased with him, prohibited following him and following others. The first person pronoun refers to al-Muzani, the first third person object refers to the learner, and the second to the Imam. And his statement: 'and others' in Fatawa of Ibn al-Salah: 'then al-Muzani at the beginning of his Mukhtasar and in others.' So understand. And Mukhtasar al-Muzani in the branches of the Shafi'ites is one of the five famous books among the Shafi'ites that they circulate most widely, and it is prevalent in all regions. Al-Muzani is the first to author in the school of al-Shafi'i. Ibn Suraij said: 'Al-Muzani's Mukhtasar emerged from the world as a virgin, and upon its model they tied the knot, and with his words they interpreted and explained.' And the Shafi'ites are devoted to it and study it and review it for a long time. Then among them were those who gave detailed expositions and those who gave abbreviated explanations with reasons, and many of them acknowledged that they had comprehended only a little of its truths, like Ibn Suraij. Among its commentaries: the commentary of Abu al-Tayyib Tahir ibn Abd Allah al-Tabari, the commentary of Abu al-Futuh Yahya ibn Isma'il ibn Muhammad Ibn Milahs al-Yamani al-Shafi'i, and the commentary of Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn Ahmad al-Marwazi in about eight volumes, and others. (1) His statement: 'there did not exist after the era of al-Shafi'i an independent mujtahid' said Imam al-Lakhnawi, explaining the meaning of this statement: 'The conclusion is that whoever claims that the rank of absolute independent ijtihad has been cut off with the imams, an interruption that cannot return, has erred and blundered; for ijtihad is a mercy from Allah, and the mercy of Allah is not restricted to a certain time nor to a certain human, and whoever claims its interruption in reality while its existence is possible in every time, if he means that there did not exist after the four a mujtahid upon whose ijtihad the majority agreed and they acknowledged his independence as they agreed on their ijtihad - then it is granted, otherwise there existed after them also people of independent ijtihad, such as Abu Thawr al-Baghdadi and Dawud al-Zahiri and Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Bukhari and others, as is not hidden from whoever peruses the books of classes. See: al-Nafi' al-Kabir: 16.'
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage from 'Adab al-Fatwa' by Imam al-Nawawi discusses the classification of mujtahids: whether later scholars like Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar from the Hanafis, as well as counterparts from other madhhabs, were independent mujtahids (mustaqill) or bound mujtahids (muqayyad) who merely followed their imam. The text presents two positions: one that later scholars were muqallids, and another, which the verifiers adopt, that they were not blind followers but rather followed their imam because they found his method in ijtihad and qiyas to be the soundest, and they engaged in ijtihad using that method. This is distinguished from taqlid. The author cites examples from different schools and then quotes Abu Amr who argues that denying taqlid altogether for these scholars is not correct; their disagreements with their imams show they were not pure imitators. The passage concludes with a footnote from Imam al-Lakhnawi refuting the claim that independent ijtihad ended after the early imams, affirming that it is a mercy of Allah not restricted to time, and listing later independent mujtahids like Abu Thawr, Dawud al-Zahiri, and Imam Bukhari.

Key Terms
مستقل (mustaqill): An independent mujtahid who derives rulings directly from primary sources without taqlid.
مقيد (muqayyad): A bound mujtahid who follows the method of a specific imam but exerts ijtihad within that framework.
تقليد (taqlid): Following an imam without knowing the evidence, considered less than ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers in this passage, why did later Hanafi scholars like Abu Yusuf follow the Hanafi school?
A) Because they were obligated to imitate Abu Hanifa blindly.
B) Because they found Abu Hanifa's method in ijtihad and qiyas to be the soundest.
C) Because they had no other choice.
D) Because they were forced by the political authorities.

Key Takeaway
Later Hanafi scholars were bound mujtahids (muqayyad), not blind followers, and their fatwas carry authority within the school's method.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
intermediate

Categories of Muftis and Conditions for Fatwa
Classification of muftis and conditions for fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of muftis, state the conditions for each, and apply the hierarchy of fatwa authority to determine when a mufti's fatwa is valid.

Core Idea
A mufti must have both memorization of the madhhab and juristic perception; without both, he is only a transmitter.

This passage from the Hanafi usul al-iftaa tradition, citing Imam al-Nawawi and Imam al-Haramayn, classifies muftis into categories based on their knowledge and ability. It provides critical guidance on who may issue fatwa independently and when one must refer to a higher authority. Understanding these categories ensures that fatwa is given by those truly qualified, preserving the integrity of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth category of mufti
Read paragraph 37 to understand the mufti who has memorized and understands the madhhab but is weak in proofs and analogies.
This is not a mujtahid; he is a transmitter.
2
Define the limits of his authority
From paragraphs 37-38: he can only transmit explicit rulings and analogize if the meaning is clearly the same or it falls under an established principle.
If not, he must refrain per paragraph 38.
3
Examine the condition of faqih al-nafs
Paragraph 40 states that for this category, the mufti must be faqih al-nafs and have most of the madhhab on his mind.
This is a condition for all categories per paragraph 41.
4
Distinguish the deficient mufti (qasir)
Paragraphs 42-43 address someone who memorized books but lacks full qualifications; he may only transmit verbatim from a reliable book when no other mufti is available.
He is like a narrator, not an independent mufti.

٣٧ - الحالة الرابعة : أن يقوم بحفظ المذهب ونقله وفهمه في الواضحات والمشكلات ولكن عنده ضعف في تقرير أدلته، وتحرير أقيسته. فهذا يعتمد نقله وفتواه به فيما يحكيه من مسطورات مذهبه من نصوص إمامه، وتفريع المجتهدين في مذهبه .

في بيان ما يشترط لهذه الأقسام
٤١ - هذه أصناف المفتين وهى خمسة وكل صنف منها يشترط فيه حفظ المذهب، وفقه النفس . فمن تصدى للفتيا، وليس بهذه الصفة، فقد باء بأمر عظيم
(١)
٤٢ - ولقد قطع إمام الحرمين وغيره بأن الأصولي الماهر المتصرف في الفقه لا يحل له الفتوى بمجرد ذلك . ولو وقعت له واقعة لزمه أن يسأل عنها . ويلتحق به المتصرف النظار البحاث من أئمة الخلاف، وفحول المناظرين؛ لأنه ليس أهلا لإدراك حكم الواقعة استقلالا ؛ لقصور آلته، ولا (٢) من مذهب ولا(٢) إمام ؛ لعدم حفظه له على الوجه المعتبر
٤٣ - فإن قيل : من حفظ كتاباً أو أكثر في المذهب وهو قاصر لم يتصف بصفة أحد ممن سبق، ولم يجد العامي في بلده غيره، هل له الرجوع إلى قوله؟
(٣)
فالجواب : إن كان في غير بلده مفت يجد السبيل إليه، وجب التوصل إليه بحسب إمكانه . فإن تعذر ذكر مسألته للقاصر. فإن وجدها بعينها في کتاب موثوق بصحته وهو ممن يقبل خبره ، نقل له حكمها بنصه، وكان العامى فيها مقلّداً صاحب المذهب. قال أبو عمرو : وهذا وجدته في ضمن كلام
(١) قوله : بأمر عظيم ألا يظن أولئك أنهم مبعوثون ليوم عظيم) (المطففين : ٤ ) .
(٢) قوله : "ولا . . . " عطف على قوله : "استقلالا" ..
(٣) قوله : "على الوجه المعتبر " قال إمام الحرمين الجويني في شرح الرسالة " : "من حفظ نصوص الشافعي، وأقوال الناس بأسرها، غير أنه لا يعرف حقائقها ومعانيها ، لا يجوز له أن يجتهد ويقيس، ولا يكون من أهل الفتوى ولو أفتى فإنه لا يجوز ..
37 - The fourth category: that he undertakes memorization of the madhhab, transmitting it, and understanding it in clear matters and problematic ones, but he has weakness in articulating its evidences and refining its analogies. So his transmission and fatwa are relied upon in what he reports from the recorded texts of his madhhab from the statements of his imam and the branching of the mujtahids in his madhhab.
38- What he does not find transmitted, if he finds its meaning in the transmitted, such that he perceives without great thought that there is no difference between them, it is permissible to annex it to the transmitted and give fatwa by it. Likewise, what is known to fall under an established principle in the madhhab. What is not like that, he must refrain from issuing fatwa on it.
39 - Such a situation rarely occurs for the mentioned person; for it is unlikely, as Imam al-Haramayn said, that an issue arise that is not explicitly stated in the madhhab, nor similar to an explicit text, nor falling under a principle.
40 - Its condition is that he be faqih al-nafs (juristically perceptive), having a substantial share of fiqh. Abu Amr said: And that he suffice in memorization of the madhhab in this and the previous category with most of it being on his mind, and he is able through discernment to ascertain the remainder shortly.
(1) His saying: "And that..." is coordinated with his saying: "faqih al-nafs". And the one of this rank has nothing of ijtihad. There remain those who give fatwa by what they have memorized or found in the books of the companions, as is now the case. They said: Apparently they are in the position of transmitters and narrators, so their statement should be accepted upon fulfillment of the condition of the narrator...
Chapter
On explaining what is required for these divisions
41 - These are the categories of muftis, and they are five, and each category requires memorization of the madhhab and faqih al-nafs. So whoever undertakes giving fatwa without this quality has incurred a grave matter.
(1)
42 - And Imam al-Haramayn and others have definitively stated that the skilled usuli who is knowledgeable in fiqh is not permitted to give fatwa on that basis alone. And if an incident occurs to him, he must ask about it. Joined to him is the skilled examiner and researcher among the leaders of disagreement and the eminent debaters; because he is not qualified to independently discern the ruling of the incident due to the deficiency of his tools, nor does he follow a madhhab or an imam due to his lack of memorization of it in the considered manner.
43 - If it is said: One who memorized a book or more in the madhhab while being deficient, not characterized by any of the aforementioned qualities, and the common person in his town finds no other, may he refer to his statement?
(3)
The answer: If there is a mufti in another town who he can reach, he must seek him out according to his ability. If that is not possible, he mentions his issue to the deficient one. If he finds it exactly in a book whose authenticity is relied upon and he is among those whose report is accepted, he transmits its ruling verbatim, and the common person in that case is a muqallid of the madhhab's founder. Abu Amr said: I found this within the speech...
(1) His saying: "a grave matter" — Do they not think that they will be raised for a great day? (Al-Mutaffifin: 4).
(2) His saying: "Nor..." is coordinated with his saying: "independently".
(3) His saying: "in the considered manner" — Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said in Sharh al-Risala: "One who memorized the texts of al-Shafi'i and all the sayings of the people, but does not know their realities and meanings, is not permitted to engage in ijtihad and analogy, nor is he among the people of fatwa. And if he gives a fatwa, it is not permissible."
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa, quoted in a Hanafi work on usul al-iftaa, delineates the conditions and categories of muftis. It begins by describing the fourth category of mufti: one who has memorized and transmitted the madhhab and understands its clear and problematic points but is weak in articulating its evidences and in deriving analogies. Such a mufti is reliable only when transmitting explicit rulings from the madhhab's texts or the branch rulings of its mujtahids. For issues not explicitly found, he may analogize if the meaning is clearly the same, or if it falls under an established principle; otherwise, he must refrain. Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) comments that such cases are rare. The condition for this category is that the mufti is 'faqih al-nafs'—possessing juristic instinct and substantial fiqh—and that he has most of the madhhab in his memory, with ability to quickly find the rest. The footnote clarifies that such a person is not a mujtahid; he is like a transmitter. The 'Fasl' then states that all five categories of muftis require both memorization of the madhhab and faqih al-nafs. Even a skilled usuli (theorist) without this is not allowed to give fatwa independently; he must ask experts. Finally, it addresses a common scenario: a learned but deficient person (qasir) who has memorized books but lacks the full qualifications—can a layperson ask him? The answer is that if another qualified mufti is reachable, the layperson must seek him. If not, the deficient one may transmit a ruling verbatim from a reliable book, and the layperson becomes a muqallid of the madhhab's founder. The passage emphasizes that mere memorization without deep understanding does not qualify one for independent fatwa.

Key Terms
فقيه النفس (faqih al-nafs): Juristic perceptiveness or instinct; an innate ability to grasp fiqh rulings and their reasoning.
حفظ المذهب (hifz al-madhhab): Memorization of the rulings, texts, and branch issues of the madhhab.
ضابط ممهد (dabit mumahhad): An established principle or rule in the madhhab that governs multiple cases.
قاصر (qasir): A person who has memorized texts but lacks the full qualifications of a mufti, especially the ability to extract rulings independently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for a mufti in the fourth category to analogize a new issue to a transmitted ruling?
A) That he is a mujtahid in the madhhab.
B) That he perceives without great thought that there is no difference between them, or that it falls under an established principle.
C) That he has memorized the entire madhhab word for word.
D) That he asks another mufti for permission.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must combine memorization of the madhhab with juristic insight (faqih al-nafs); without both, he is only a transmitter of specific rulings.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

Can a Muqallid Issue a Fatwa?
Disagreement on muqallid fatwa and the permitted form with attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the disagreement on muqallid fatwa and identify the Hanafi position of attributing the fatwa to the imam.

Core Idea
A muqallid may issue a fatwa only by attributing it to his imam, not as his own judgment.

This part of the passage addresses a critical question: can a follower of a madhhab (muqallid) issue a fatwa? The text presents a disagreement between scholars who forbid it outright and those who permit it, with a compromise that the muqallid must attribute the ruling to his imam. This lesson unpacks the arguments and identifies the practical ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Note that the question is: can a muqallid give a fatwa on what he follows?
2
List the opposing views
Al-Halimi, al-Juwayni, al-Ruyani forbid; al-Qaffal al-Marwazi permits.
These are Shafi'i scholars, but the discussion is general.
3
Understand the compromise
Abu Amr clarifies that the prohibition means the muqallid must not present the ruling as his own; he must attribute it to his imam.
This makes the muqallid a transmitter, not an independent mujtahid.
4
Distinguish muqallid from layman
The selected view (from the footnote) is that a muqallid who is a 'mujtahid in the madhhab' may give fatwa, distinguishing him from a layman.
He must be able to derive from the school's principles.
5
Apply to Hanafi practice
Hanafi muftis typically attribute rulings to Imam Abu Hanifa, following the compromise.
This is consistent with the idea that fatwa is transmitted from the imam.

And if he does not find it written explicitly, he should not analogize it to what is written with him, even if he believes it from analogy without difference, for he may mistakenly assume that in the wrong place. 44 - If it is said: Can a muqallid issue a fatwa on what he is a muqallid in? We say: Abu Abdullah al-Halimi, Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, Abu al-Mahasin al-Ruyani, and others explicitly forbade it, and al-Qaffal al-Marwazi allowed it. , ا + Abu Amr said: The meaning of those who forbid it is that he should not mention it in the form of someone speaking from himself, but rather attribute it to his imam whom he follows. 45 - Thus, those we count among the muftis who are muqallids are not muftis in reality, but because they stand in their place and convey from them, they are counted with them. Their way is to say, for example: the school of al-Shafi'i is such, or something like that. Whoever among them omits the attribution is doing so relying on the known situation rather than explicit statement, and there is no harm in that. 46 - The author of al-Hawi mentioned regarding the layman who knows the ruling of an incident based on its evidence, three views: One: it is permissible for him to issue a fatwa (2) and it is permissible to follow him, because he has attained knowledge of it. (1) His statement: 'forbidding it' because he is only asked about what he knows, not about what others know, and because if fatwa by way of narrating the school of others were permissible, that would be permissible for the layman, which is impossible and contrary to consensus. And some permitted it if it is established by a reliable transmitter. The chosen view is that if he is a mujtahid in the school such that he is aware of the sources of the absolute mujtahid he follows and is able to derive rulings from his imam's principles and statements, capable of distinguishing, combining, considering, and debating in that, then he may issue fatwa, distinguishing him from the layman. Its proof is the continuous consensus of scholars of every era in accepting this type of fatwa. If not, then not. See: al-Ihkam by al-Amidi, 4/458. (2) His statement: 'it is permissible to issue fatwa with it' because he has acquired knowledge of the ruling of that incident from its evidence just as the scholar has, though the scholar is distinguished by strength enabling him to explain the evidence and refute objections, which is an extra ability beyond knowing the truth by its evidence. The second: it is permissible if its evidence is the Book or Sunnah, but not if it is other than them (1). The third: it is not permissible at all, and it is the most correct view, and Allah knows best. Chapter: On the rulings of muftis, containing issues. First: 47 - Issuing fatwa is a collective obligation. If someone seeks a fatwa and there is no one else in the area, it becomes personally obligatory on him to answer. If there are others present, then answering is a collective obligation on both of them (1). (4) Obligation (3). If no one else is present, there are two views: the more correct is that it does not become personally obligatory (4); due to what preceded from Ibn. (1) His statement: 'collective obligation' in al-Bahr from al-Rawd: 'If there is no one else, it becomes personally obligatory on him; if there is someone else, it is a collective obligation. Nevertheless, hastening to what is not verified is not permissible.' As for the hadith of Abu Huraira from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'Whoever is asked about knowledge and conceals it will be bridled with a bridle of fire', and other traditions on this topic, a sample of which was mentioned at the beginning of the book, their meaning according to the investigators is that this applies to knowledge that he is obliged to teach and whose obligation is personally incumbent upon him, such as one who sees someone wanting to embrace Islam and asks him what Islam is, or who sees a new Muslim asking to be taught how to pray, or someone who came...
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa discusses two key aspects of issuing fatwa. First (section 44-46), it addresses whether a muqallid (a follower of a madhhab) can issue a fatwa. There is a disagreement: some scholars like al-Halimi, al-Juwayni, and al-Ruyani forbid it unconditionally, while al-Qaffal al-Marwazi permits it. Abu Amr clarifies that the prohibition means the muqallid must attribute the fatwa to his imam, not speak as if it is his own. The text then explains that such muqallids are not true muftis but act as transmitters. A further discussion (46) concerns a layman who knows a ruling from its evidence: three opinions are given, with the most correct being that he may not issue fatwa at all. The second part (Chapter, first issue) states that issuing fatwa is a collective obligation (fard kifayah). If no qualified mufti is available in a locality, it becomes an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) on the available mufti. If there are multiple muftis, it remains collective; if none are present, there is a difference of opinion, but the stronger view is that it does not become individually obligatory on a single person. The hadith about concealing knowledge is interpreted to apply only when one is personally obliged to teach, not to general fatwa.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid): A person who follows a particular imam in legal rulings without necessarily knowing the evidence or having the ability to derive rulings.
عامي (ʻāmmī): A layperson who does not have specialized knowledge of legal evidence or methodology.
فرض كفاية (fard kifayah): A collective obligation: if some fulfill it, the rest are absolved; if none do, all are sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the most correct view (al-ashah) regarding a layman who knows the ruling of an incident based on its evidence issuing a fatwa?
A) It is permissible in all cases.
B) It is permissible only if the evidence is from the Quran or Sunnah.
C) It is not permissible at all.
D) It is obligatory if he is the only one available.

Key Takeaway
The muqallid mufti must attribute his fatwa to the imam of the madhhab; a layman cannot issue a fatwa even if he knows the ruling.

Issuing Fatwa as a Collective Obligation
Ruling on ifta as fard kifayah and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that ifta is a collective obligation and identify when it becomes individually obligatory.

Core Idea
Issuing fatwa is a collective obligation; it becomes an individual duty only if no other qualified mufti is available in the locality.

This section of the passage establishes the legal classification of ifta. It is a collective obligation (fard kifayah) on the community. However, if a specific person is the only qualified mufti in an area, the obligation becomes individual (fard 'ayn). The passage also clarifies that the hadith on concealing knowledge applies only when one is personally obliged to teach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Issuing fatwa is a collective obligation (fard kifayah).
2
Determine the condition for individual obligation
If no other mufti is in the district, it becomes fard 'ayn on the available mufti.
This is the default view.
3
Understand the alternative view
If no one else is present, there is a difference; the stronger view is that it does not become personally obligatory.
The text says 'the more correct is that it does not become personally obligatory' (4).
4
Relate to the hadith
The hadith about concealing knowledge applies only when one is personally obliged, not to general fatwa.
5
Apply to mufti practice
A mufti should not rush to answer unless he is sure and the situation requires it.
The footnote warns against hastening to what is not verified.
