And if he does not find it written explicitly, he should not analogize it to what is written with him, even if he believes it from analogy without difference, for he may mistakenly assume that in the wrong place. 44 - If it is said: Can a muqallid issue a fatwa on what he is a muqallid in? We say: Abu Abdullah al-Halimi, Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, Abu al-Mahasin al-Ruyani, and others explicitly forbade it, and al-Qaffal al-Marwazi allowed it. , ا + Abu Amr said: The meaning of those who forbid it is that he should not mention it in the form of someone speaking from himself, but rather attribute it to his imam whom he follows. 45 - Thus, those we count among the muftis who are muqallids are not muftis in reality, but because they stand in their place and convey from them, they are counted with them. Their way is to say, for example: the school of al-Shafi'i is such, or something like that. Whoever among them omits the attribution is doing so relying on the known situation rather than explicit statement, and there is no harm in that. 46 - The author of al-Hawi mentioned regarding the layman who knows the ruling of an incident based on its evidence, three views: One: it is permissible for him to issue a fatwa (2) and it is permissible to follow him, because he has attained knowledge of it. (1) His statement: 'forbidding it' because he is only asked about what he knows, not about what others know, and because if fatwa by way of narrating the school of others were permissible, that would be permissible for the layman, which is impossible and contrary to consensus. And some permitted it if it is established by a reliable transmitter. The chosen view is that if he is a mujtahid in the school such that he is aware of the sources of the absolute mujtahid he follows and is able to derive rulings from his imam's principles and statements, capable of distinguishing, combining, considering, and debating in that, then he may issue fatwa, distinguishing him from the layman. Its proof is the continuous consensus of scholars of every era in accepting this type of fatwa. If not, then not. See: al-Ihkam by al-Amidi, 4/458. (2) His statement: 'it is permissible to issue fatwa with it' because he has acquired knowledge of the ruling of that incident from its evidence just as the scholar has, though the scholar is distinguished by strength enabling him to explain the evidence and refute objections, which is an extra ability beyond knowing the truth by its evidence. The second: it is permissible if its evidence is the Book or Sunnah, but not if it is other than them (1). The third: it is not permissible at all, and it is the most correct view, and Allah knows best. Chapter: On the rulings of muftis, containing issues. First: 47 - Issuing fatwa is a collective obligation. If someone seeks a fatwa and there is no one else in the area, it becomes personally obligatory on him to answer. If there are others present, then answering is a collective obligation on both of them (1). (4) Obligation (3). If no one else is present, there are two views: the more correct is that it does not become personally obligatory (4); due to what preceded from Ibn. (1) His statement: 'collective obligation' in al-Bahr from al-Rawd: 'If there is no one else, it becomes personally obligatory on him; if there is someone else, it is a collective obligation. Nevertheless, hastening to what is not verified is not permissible.' As for the hadith of Abu Huraira from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'Whoever is asked about knowledge and conceals it will be bridled with a bridle of fire', and other traditions on this topic, a sample of which was mentioned at the beginning of the book, their meaning according to the investigators is that this applies to knowledge that he is obliged to teach and whose obligation is personally incumbent upon him, such as one who sees someone wanting to embrace Islam and asks him what Islam is, or who sees a new Muslim asking to be taught how to pray, or someone who came...
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from Imam al-Nawawi's Adab al-Fatwa discusses two key aspects of issuing fatwa. First (section 44-46), it addresses whether a muqallid (a follower of a madhhab) can issue a fatwa. There is a disagreement: some scholars like al-Halimi, al-Juwayni, and al-Ruyani forbid it unconditionally, while al-Qaffal al-Marwazi permits it. Abu Amr clarifies that the prohibition means the muqallid must attribute the fatwa to his imam, not speak as if it is his own. The text then explains that such muqallids are not true muftis but act as transmitters. A further discussion (46) concerns a layman who knows a ruling from its evidence: three opinions are given, with the most correct being that he may not issue fatwa at all. The second part (Chapter, first issue) states that issuing fatwa is a collective obligation (fard kifayah). If no qualified mufti is available in a locality, it becomes an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) on the available mufti. If there are multiple muftis, it remains collective; if none are present, there is a difference of opinion, but the stronger view is that it does not become individually obligatory on a single person. The hadith about concealing knowledge is interpreted to apply only when one is personally obliged to teach, not to general fatwa.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid): A person who follows a particular imam in legal rulings without necessarily knowing the evidence or having the ability to derive rulings.
عامي (ʻāmmī): A layperson who does not have specialized knowledge of legal evidence or methodology.
فرض كفاية (fard kifayah): A collective obligation: if some fulfill it, the rest are absolved; if none do, all are sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does issuing fatwa become an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) for a mufti?
A) Always, because knowledge must be shared.
B) When no other qualified mufti is in the locality.
C) When the questioner is a family member.
D) When the mufti is a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
Ifta is a collective obligation; it becomes individual only when no other qualified mufti is available.

Ruling on Answering Hypothetical Questions
Ruling on whether a mufti must answer about hypothetical events

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scholarly positions on answering hypothetical questions and apply the Hanafi detailed view (tafsil) to determine when a mufti may respond.

Core Idea
A mufti should not answer hypothetical questions without textual basis; the preferred view distinguishes matters with evidence from remote speculations.

This lesson addresses a key adab of ifta: how should a mufti respond when asked about something that has not yet occurred? The classical scholars differed, and the Hanafi school adopts a nuanced position that balances caution with the duty to inform. You will explore the three opinions reported by Ibn Qayyim and learn the criterion that governs the mufti's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the passage deals with hypothetical questions (masa'il muqaddara) that have not occurred. The matn states the mufti should not answer such questions, but the commentary introduces a spectrum of opinions.
Distinguish between actual events and hypotheticals.
2
Recognize the three opinions
Ibn Qayyim reports three views: 1) recommended to avoid, 2) disliked to answer, 3) a choice. Understand that the 'choice' view is further detailed.
The third view is the one that leads to the detailed criterion.
3
Understand the salaf practice
The passage recounts that many salaf would not engage in hypotheticals; some would ask 'Did that happen?' before answering. This illustrates the caution that underlies the Hanafi approach.
The salaf prioritized practical, occurring issues over theoretical speculation.
4
Grasp the detailed view criterion
The preferred view (tafsil) distinguishes: if there is a text from Quran, Sunnah, or a Companion’s statement, it is not disliked to answer. If there is no text and the matter is far-fetched, it is not recommended.
Textual evidence is the key that transforms a hypothetical into a legitimate inquiry.
5
Apply to mufti's conduct
As a mufti, you must first verify the existence of a clear textual basis. If absent, you may politely decline or say 'Leave us in safety.' This preserves the integrity of fatwa and avoids error.
This application is part of the adab al-mufti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٨ - ولو سأل عامى عما لم يقع ، لم يجب جوابه

48 - And if a common person asks about something that has not occurred, he should not answer his query. (footnote) He said: 'He should not answer' – Ibn Qayyim said: If a seeker of fatwa asks about a matter that has not occurred, is it recommended to answer, or disliked, or a choice? There are three opinions. It is reported that many of the salaf would not speak about what had not occurred. Some of the salaf, when asked a question, would say: 'Did that happen?' If yes, they would undertake to answer; otherwise, they would say: 'Leave us in safety.' Imam Ahmad said to some of his companions: 'Beware of speaking about a matter for which you have no imam.' The correct view is differentiation: if there is a text from Quran, Sunnah, or a Companion’s statement, it is not disliked to speak about it. If there is no text, and the matter is unlikely to occur or hypothetical, then it is not recommended...
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage addresses the adab of a mufti concerning hypothetical questions. The matn states that a mufti should not answer a layman's question about something that has not happened. The commentary elaborates, citing Ibn Qayyim's three opinions: 1) recommended to avoid, 2) disliked to answer, 3) a choice. The practice of the salaf was to avoid hypotheticals. The preferred view is detailed: if there is explicit textual evidence (Quran, hadith, or athar), it is permissible to answer; if not, and the matter is far-fetched, it is not recommended. The passage emphasizes that the mufti should not burden himself with imaginary scenarios, especially when there is no precedent from the imams.

Key Terms
ما لم يقع (ma lam yaqa‘): Something that has not yet occurred; a hypothetical or future event.
مسألة مقدرة (mas'ala muqaddara): A hypothetical question about an event that is unlikely to happen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the detailed view mentioned in the passage, when is it not disliked to answer a hypothetical question?
A) When the question is about halal and haram.
B) When there is textual evidence (Quran, Sunnah, or Companion's statement).
C) When the scholar fears harm from not answering.
D) It is always disliked regardless.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti follows a detailed view: he may answer hypothetical questions only if they are grounded in clear textual evidence; otherwise, he refrains to preserve the integrity of fatwa.

Questioner Unaware of Mufti's Retraction
Ruling on the mufti's duty to inform of retraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that the mufti must inform the questioner of his retraction and distinguish the questioner's status.

Core Idea
The mufti must inform the questioner of retraction; ignorance does not change the questioner's original knowledge.

This lesson addresses a key obligation of the mufti: what happens if he retracts a fatwa but the questioner remains unaware? The text clarifies that the questioner's knowledge stays as before the retraction, and the mufti must inform him before he acts. This ensures the questioner is not misled and preserves trust in the fatwa process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the passage applies when the questioner does not know the mufti has retracted his earlier fatwa.
Ignorance here is factual unawareness, not willful.
2
Determine the questioner's status
The questioner's knowledge remains as before the retraction — he still believes the original fatwa is valid.
This protects the questioner from being held accountable for an unknown change.
3
State the mufti's obligation
The mufti is obliged (lazim) to inform the questioner of the retraction before the questioner acts on the fatwa.
The obligation exists both before and after the action if the questioner has not yet acted.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, the mufti must actively notify the questioner; silence or assumption of knowledge does not suffice.
The ruling emphasizes the mufti's responsibility to avoid misleading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥١ - أما إذا لم يعلم المستفتى برجوع المفتي، فحال المستفتى في علمه كما قبل الرجوع، ويلزم المفتى إعلامه قبل العمل، وكذا بعده حيث
51. As for when the questioner does not know of the mufti's retraction, then the questioner's situation in his knowledge is as before the retraction, and it is obligatory upon the mufti to inform him before action, and likewise after [action] when [he has not acted?].
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This final ruling covers the case where the questioner remains unaware of the mufti's retraction. In such a case, from the questioner's perspective, the original fatwa is still valid, and the mufti is obligated to inform him of the retraction before the questioner acts on it. Similarly, even after acting, the mufti must inform him if he was unaware. The full text likely continues to clarify that the mufti must inform regardless, ensuring the questioner is not misled.

Key Terms
الإعلام (al-i'lam): The obligation of the mufti to inform the questioner of his retraction.
الرجوع (al-ruju): The mufti's retraction of a previously issued fatwa.
المستفتى (al-mustafti): The questioner, the person seeking a fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufti obligated to do if the questioner is unaware of his retraction?
A) The mufti may remain silent.
B) The mufti must inform the questioner before he acts.
C) The questioner is responsible to check for retractions.
D) The retraction is void if the questioner is unaware.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must actively inform the questioner of any retraction before the questioner acts on the original fatwa.

Mufti's Duty to Inform on Retraction
Ruling on mustafti unaware of mufti's retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the mustafti's actions based on a retracted fatwa remain valid until he is informed, and state the mufti's obligation to notify.

Core Idea
Until the mustafti knows of the retraction, his fatwa remains valid — the mufti must inform him promptly.

This passage from Rasm al-Mufti addresses a critical ethical and procedural duty of the mufti: informing the mustafti when a fatwa is retracted. It balances the mufti's responsibility to correct errors with the mustafti's reliance on the fatwa. Understanding this rule protects the mustafti from unintended invalid acts and ensures the mufti discharges his trust properly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine when this rule applies: the mufti has retracted a previous fatwa, but the mustafti has not yet been informed of the retraction.
2
State the mustafti's position
For the mustafti, the fatwa remains valid in his knowledge until he is informed. His actions taken in reliance on the original fatwa are not invalidated retroactively.
The rule is: 'فحال المستفتي في علمه كما قبل الرجوع' — his situation is as before the retraction.
3
State the mufti's duty
The mufti is obligated to inform the mustafti. This duty applies before the mustafti acts (to prevent acting on error) and also after he has acted if the fatwa must be retracted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥١ - أما إذا لم يعلم المستفتى برجوع المفتي، فحال المستفتي في علمه كما قبل الرجوع، ويلزم المفتى إعلامه قبل العمل، وكذا بعده حيث يجب النقض.
51. As for when the mustafti (seeker of fatwa) does not know of the mufti's retraction, then the situation of the mustafti, in his knowledge, is as before the retraction; and the mufti is obliged to inform him before acting [on the fatwa], and likewise after [acting] when retraction is obligatory.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a mufti has retracted a fatwa but the mustafti has not yet been informed. The ruling clarifies that the mustafti's legal position remains as it was before the retraction — i.e., the fatwa is still valid for him until he is informed. The mufti is obliged to notify the mustafti. This notification must be done before the mustafti acts on the fatwa if possible; if the mustafti has already acted, the mufti must still inform him after the fact if retraction is required. This ensures the mustafti can adjust his actions accordingly and avoid sin or invalid transactions.

Key Terms
مستفتى (mustafti): The person who seeks a fatwa from a mufti
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction of a fatwa by the mufti when he realizes error
إعلام (i'lam): Informing the mustafti of the retraction

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mufti retracts a fatwa but does not inform the mustafti, what is the mustafti's position regarding actions taken based on the fatwa?
A) His actions are invalid because the fatwa was retracted
B) His position remains as before the retraction until he is informed
C) He must repeat all actions after learning of the retraction
D) He is responsible for not checking the mufti's current opinion

Key Takeaway
Until the mustafti is informed of a fatwa's retraction, the fatwa remains valid for him, and the mufti must notify him promptly.

Liability for Erroneous Fatwa and the Duty to Correct
Rasm rule on mufti liability and duty to correct

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on a mufti's liability for an erroneous fatwa and explain the ethical obligation to correct such errors.

Core Idea
A mufti may be liable if he violates a definitive ruling; liability for the unqualified is contested, but he must always correct his mistake.

This passage from Rasm al-Mufti confronts a delicate question: when a fatwa leads to harm, who bears the cost? The author presents conflicting views on liability and illustrates the mufti's duty to rectify errors with the example of Hasan ibn Ziyad. Understanding this helps muftis navigate both legal responsibility and ethical conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering scenario
Note the conditions: the mustafti acts on the fatwa, causes damage (itlaf), and later the fatwa turns out to be erroneous and violates a definitive ruling (qati').
The qati' violation is important because it makes the error clear and undeniable.
2
State Abu Ishaq's distinction
Abu Ishaq holds that a qualified mufti (ahl) is liable, while an unqualified mufti is not liable because the mustafti was negligent in seeking his fatwa.
Negligence (taqsir) removes liability from the mufti.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author (likely Ibn Abidin) finds Abu Ishaq's view problematic and suggests instead deriving liability from the two well-known opinions on ghurur (deception) in ghasb and nikah chapters.
This shows the issue is unresolved (mushkil).
4
Analyze the ghurur analogy
The two ghurur opinions: (1) the deceiver is liable for all damages, (2) the deceiver is only liable if he directly caused the loss. The passage also mentions a third option: no liability at all because fatwa is not deception.
The author does not prefer one; he leaves the matter open.
5
Extract the moral from the story
The story of Hasan ibn Ziyad shows that when a mufti realizes his error, he must suspend giving fatwa, seek out the mustafti, and inform him of the correct ruling.
This is an adab (ethical duty) regardless of financial liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٢ - وإذا عمل بفتواه في إتلاف، فبان خطأه، وأنه خالف القاطع، فعن الأستاذ أبي إسحاق أنه يضمن إن كان أهلا للفتوى، ولا يضمن إن لم يكن أهلا؛ لأن المستفتى قصر، كذا حكاه الشيخ أبو عمرو، وسكت عليه، وهو مشكل، وينبغى أن يخرج الضمان على قولى الغرور المعروفين في بابي الغصب والنكاح وغيرهما، أو يُقطع بعدم الضمان؛ إذ ليس في الفتوى فمن كان أفتاه الحسن بن زياد بشيء فليرجع إليه، ثم لبث أياما لا يفتى حتى جاء صاحب الفتوى، فأعلمه أنه قد أخطأ، وأن الصواب خلاف ما أفتاه به.
52. And if he (the mustafti) acts on his (the mufti's) fatwa causing destruction (damage), then it becomes clear that the fatwa was erroneous and that it violated a definitive ruling, then according to al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq: the mufti is liable if he was qualified for fatwa, and he is not liable if he was not qualified, because the mustafti was negligent. This is what Shaykh Abu Amr narrated and remained silent on. But this is problematic. Rather, liability should be derived from the two well-known opinions about al-ghurur (deception) in the chapters of ghasb (usurpation) and nikah (marriage) and others, or one should definitively rule for non-liability, because there is no deception in fatwa. Whoever was given a fatwa by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad should return to him... then he stayed for days without giving fatwa until the seeker of fatwa came to him, and he informed him that he had erred and that the correct ruling was contrary to his fatwa.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability of a mufti when his fatwa leads to damage and it turns out he was wrong, especially if he violated a definitive ruling (qat'i). Abu Ishaq distinguishes between a qualified mufti (ahl al-fatwa) and an unqualified one: the qualified mufti is liable if he went against qat'i; the unqualified is not liable because the mustafti is considered negligent for consulting him. Abu Amr reports this opinion without comment. The commentator (likely Ibn Abidin) finds this problematic. He suggests that liability should be determined by the two well-known opinions on ghurur (deception) from ghasb and nikah chapters: either the deceiver is liable (like a usurper) or not (if no direct taking). Alternatively, one may hold that there is no liability at all because a fatwa inherently does not involve deception. The story of Hasan ibn Ziyad illustrates that a mufti who errs must correct himself and inform the seeker, but it does not address financial liability.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Financial liability for damages caused by one's action
غرور (ghurur): Deception leading someone into a harmful situation; a basis for liability
قاطع (qati'): A definitive text or ruling (from Quran, mutawatir hadith, or ijma) that allows no ijtihad

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which statement is true regarding a mufti's liability for an erroneous fatwa that caused damage?
A) All scholars agree the mufti is always liable
B) Abu Ishaq holds that an unqualified mufti is not liable because the mustafti was negligent
C) The author of the text strongly endorses Abu Ishaq's view
D) The story of Hasan ibn Ziyad shows that the mufti must pay compensation

Key Takeaway
The liability of an unqualified mufti for an erroneous fatwa remains a contested issue in Hanafi usul, but regardless, the mufti is ethically bound to correct his mistake.
